Document 0 231
[-LSB-edit-RSB- History The common guinea pig was first domesticated as early as 5000 BC for food by tribes in the Andean region of South America -LRB-present-day Ecuador, Peru, and Bolivia-RRB-, some thousands of years after the domestication of the South American camelids.][[1.0]]
[Statues dating from ca. 500 BC to 500 AD that depict guinea pigs have been unearthed in archaeological digs in Peru and Ecuador.][[0.0]]
[The Moche people of ancient Peru worshipped animals and often depicted the guinea pig in their art.][[0.0]]
[From ca. 1200 AD to the Spanish conquest in 1532, selective breeding resulted in many varieties of domestic guinea pigs, which form the basis for some of the modern domestic breeds.][[0.0]]
[They continue to be a food source in the region; most households in the Andean highlands raise the animal, which subsists off the family's vegetable scraps.][[0.0]]
[Folklore traditions involving guinea pigs are numerous; they are exchanged as gifts, used in customary social and religious ceremonies, and frequently referenced in spoken metaphors.][[0.0]]
[They also play a role in traditional healing rituals by folk doctors, or curanderos, who use the animals to diagnose diseases such as jaundice, rheumatism, arthritis, and typhus.][[0.0]]
[They are rubbed against the bodies of the sick, and are seen as a supernatural medium.][[0.0]]
[Black guinea pigs are considered especially useful for diagnoses.][[0.0]]
[The animal also may be cut open and its entrails examined to determine whether the cure was effective.][[0.0]]
[These methods are widely accepted in many parts of the Andes, where Western medicine is either unavailable or distrusted.][[0.0]]
[Spanish, Dutch, and English traders brought guinea pigs to Europe, where they quickly became popular as exotic pets among the upper classes and royalty, including Queen Elizabeth I.][[0.0]]
[The earliest known written account of the guinea pig dates from 1547, in a description of the animal from Santo Domingo; because cavies are not native to Hispaniola, the animal was likely introduced there by Spanish travelers.][[0.0]]
[The guinea pig was first described in the West in 1554 by the Swiss naturalist Conrad Gessner.][[0.0]]
[Its binomial scientific name was first used by Erxleben in 1777; it is an amalgam of Pallas' generic designation -LRB-1766-RRB- and Linnaeus' specific conferral -LRB-1758-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Name The scientific name of the common species is Cavia porcellus, with porcellus being Latin for ``little pig''.][[0.0]]
[Cavia is New Latin; it is derived from cabiai, the animal's name in the language of the Galibi tribes once native to French Guiana.][[0.0]]
[Cabiai may be an adaptation of the Portuguese çavia -LRB-now savia-RRB-, which is itself derived from the Tupi word saujá, meaning rat.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs are called quwi or jaca in Quechua and cuy or cuyo -LRB-pl.][[0.0]]
[cuyes, cuyos-RRB- in the Spanish of Ecuador, Peru, and Bolivia.][[0.0]]
[Ironically, breeders tend to use the more formal ``cavy'' to describe the animal, while in scientific and laboratory contexts it is far more commonly referred to by the more colloquial ``guinea pig''.][[0.0]]
[How the animals came to be thought of as ``pigs'' is not clear.][[0.0]]
[They are built somewhat like pigs, with large heads relative to their bodies, stout necks, and rounded rumps with no tail of any consequence; some of the sounds they emit are very similar to those made by pigs, and they also spend a large amount of time eating.][[0.0]]
[They can survive for long periods in small quarters, like a `pig pen', and were thus easily transported on ships to Europe.][[0.0]]
[The animal's name carries porcine connotations in many European languages.][[0.0]]
[The German word for them is Meerschweinchen, literally ``little sea pig'', which has been translated into Polish as świnka morska, into Hungarian as tengerimalac and into Russian as морская свинка which can be translated to English as ``sea pig''.][[0.0]]
[This derives from nautical history: sailing ships stopping to reprovision in the New World would pick up stores of guinea pigs, which provided an easily transportable source of fresh meat.][[0.0]]
[The French term is Cochon d'Inde -LRB-Indian pig-RRB- or cobaye; the Dutch used to call it guinees biggetje -LRB-Guinean piglet-RRB- or Spaanse rat -LRB-Spanish rat-RRB- in some dialects, and in Portuguese the guinea pig is variously referred to as cobaia, from the Tupi word via its Latinization, or as porquinho da Índia -LRB-little Indian pig-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[This is not universal; for example, the common word in Spanish is conejillo de Indias -LRB-little rabbit of India / the Indies-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Equally peculiar, the Chinese refer to them as Holland pigs -LRB-荷蘭豬, hélánzhū-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The origin of ``guinea'' in ``guinea pig'' is harder to explain.][[0.0]]
[One theory is that the animals were brought to Europe by way of Guinea, leading people to think they had originated there.][[0.0]]
[``Guinea'' was also frequently used in English to refer generally to any far-off, unknown country, and so the name may simply be a colorful reference to the animal's foreignness.][[0.0]]
[Another theory suggests the ``guinea'' in the name is a corruption of ``Guiana'', an area in South America, though the animals are not native to that region.][[0.0]]
[A common misconception is that they were so named because they were sold for the price of a guinea coin; this theory is untenable, because the guinea was first struck in England in 1663, and William Harvey used the term ``Ginny-pig'' as early as 1653.][[0.0]]
[Others believe ``guinea'' may be an alteration of the word coney -LRB-rabbit-RRB-; guinea pigs were referred to as ``pig coneys'' in Edward Topsell's 1607 treatise on quadrupeds.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Traits and environment Guinea pigs are large for rodents, weighing between 700 and 1200g -LRB-1.5-2.5�pounds-RRB-, and measuring between 20 and 25�cm -LRB-8--10�inches-RRB- in length.][[0.0]]
[They typically live an average of four to five years, but may live as long as eight years.][[0.0]]
[According to the 2006 Guinness Book of Records the longest living guinea pig survived 14�years, 10.5�months.][[0.0]]
[In the 1990s, a minority scientific opinion emerged proposing that caviomorphs, such as guinea pigs, chinchillas, and degus, are not rodents and should be reclassified as a separate order of mammals -LRB-similar to lagomorphs-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Subsequent research using wider sampling has restored consensus among mammalian biologists that the current classification of rodents as monophyletic is justified.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Natural habitat Cavia porcellus is not found naturally in the wild; it is likely descendant from some closely related species of cavies, such as Cavia aperea, Cavia fulgida, and Cavia tschudii, which are still commonly found in various regions of South America.][[1.0]]
[Some species of cavy identified in the 20th century, such as Cavia anolaimae and Cavia guianae, may be domestic guinea pigs that have become feral by reintroduction into the wild.][[0.0]]
[Wild cavies are found on grassy plains and occupy an ecological niche similar to that of the cow.][[0.0]]
[They are social, living in the wild in small groups which consist of several females -LRB-sows-RRB-, a male -LRB-boar-RRB-, and the young -LRB-which in a break with the preceding porcine nomenclature are called pups-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[They move together in groups -LRB-herds-RRB- eating grass or other vegetation, and do not store food.][[0.0]]
[While they do not burrow or build nests, they frequently seek shelter in the burrows of other animals, as well as in crevices and tunnels formed by vegetation.][[0.0]]
[They are crepuscular, tending to be most active during dawn and dusk, when it is harder for predators to spot them.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Domestic habitat Domesticated guinea pigs thrive in groups of two or more; groups of sows, or groups of one or more sows and a neutered boar are common combinations.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs learn to recognize and bond with other individual guinea pigs, and testing of boars shows that their neuroendocrine stress response is significantly lowered in the presence of a bonded female when compared to the presence of unfamiliar females.][[0.0]]
[Groups of boars may also get along, provided that their cage has enough space, they are introduced at an early age, and no females are present.][[0.0]]
[Domestic guinea pigs have developed a different biological rhythm from their wild counterparts, and have longer periods of activity followed by short periods of sleep in between.][[0.0]]
[Activity is scattered randomly over the 24 hours of the day; aside from avoidance of intense light, no regular circadian patterns are apparent.][[0.0]]
[Domestic guinea pigs generally live in cages, although some owners of large numbers of guinea pigs will dedicate entire rooms to their pets.][[0.0]]
[Cages with solid or wire mesh floors are used, although wire mesh floors can cause injury and may be associated with an infection commonly known as bumblefoot -LRB-ulcerative pododermatitis-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[``Cubes and Coroplast'' -LRB-or C&C-RRB- style cages are now a common choice.][[0.0]]
[Cages are often lined with wood shavings or a similar material.][[0.0]]
[Bedding made from Red Cedar and pine, both softwoods, was commonly used in the past, however these materials are now believed to contain harmful phenols -LRB-aromatic hydrocarbons-RRB- and oils.][[0.0]]
[Safer beddings include those made from hardwoods -LRB-such as aspen-RRB-, paper products, and corn cob materials are other alternatives.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs tend to be messy within their cages; they often jump into their food bowls or kick bedding and feces into them, and their urine sometimes crystallizes on cage surfaces, making it difficult to remove.][[0.0]]
[After its cage has been cleaned, a guinea pig will typically urinate and drag the lower body across the floor of the cage to mark its territory.][[0.0]]
[Male guinea pigs may also mark their territory in this way when they are taken out of their cages.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs do not generally thrive when housed with other species.][[0.0]]
[Cohousing of guinea pigs with other rodents such as gerbils and hamsters may increase instances of respiratory and other infections, and such rodents may act aggressively toward the guinea pig.][[0.0]]
[Larger animals may regard guinea pigs as prey, though some -LRB-such as dogs-RRB- can be trained to accept them.][[0.0]]
[Opinion is divided over the cohousing of guinea pigs and domestic rabbits.][[0.0]]
[Some published sources say that guinea pigs and rabbits complement each other well when sharing a cage.][[0.0]]
[However, as lagomorphs, rabbits have different nutritional requirements, and so the two species cannot be fed the same food.][[0.0]]
[Rabbits may also harbor diseases -LRB-such as the respiratory infections Bordetella and Pasteurella-RRB-, to which guinea pigs are susceptible.][[0.0]]
[Even the dwarf rabbit is much stronger than the guinea pig and may cause intentional or inadvertent injury.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Behavior Guinea pigs can learn complex paths to food, and can accurately remember a learned path for months.][[0.0]]
[Their strongest problem solving strategy is motion.][[0.0]]
[While guinea pigs can jump small obstacles, they are poor climbers, and are not particularly agile.][[0.0]]
[They startle extremely easily, and will either freeze in place for long periods or run for cover with rapid, darting motions when they sense danger.][[0.0]]
[Larger groups of startled guinea pigs will ``stampede'', running in haphazard directions as a means of confusing predators.][[0.0]]
[When excited, guinea pigs may repeatedly perform little hops in the air -LRB-known as ``popcorning''-RRB-, a movement analogous to the ferret's war dance.][[0.0]]
[They are also exceedingly good swimmers.][[0.0]]
[Like many rodents, guinea pigs sometimes participate in social grooming, and they regularly self-groom.][[0.0]]
[A milky-white substance is secreted from their eyes and rubbed into the hair during the grooming process.][[0.0]]
[Groups of boars will often chew each other's hair, but this is a method of establishing hierarchy within a group, rather than a social gesture.][[0.0]]
[Dominance is also established through biting -LRB-especially of the ears-RRB-, piloerection, aggressive noises, head thrusts, and leaping attacks.][[0.0]]
[Non-sexual simulated mounting for dominance is also common among same-sex groups.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pig sight is not as good as a human, but they have a wider range of vision -LRB-about 340°-RRB- and see in partial color -LRB-dichromacy-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[They have well-developed senses of hearing, smell, and touch.][[0.0]]
[Vocalization is the primary means of communication between members of the species.][[0.0]]
[Some sounds are:  -LSB-edit-RSB- Breeding The guinea pig is able to breed year-round, with birth peaks usually coming in the spring; as many as five litters can be produced per year.][[0.0]]
[The gestation period lasts from 59--72 days, with an average of 63--68 days.][[0.0]]
[Because of the long gestation period and the large size of the pups, pregnant females may become large and eggplant-shaped, although the change in size and shape varies.][[0.0]]
[Newborn pups are well-developed with hair, teeth, claws, and partial eyesight; they are immediately mobile, and begin eating solid food immediately, though they continue to suckle.][[0.0]]
[Litters yield 1--6 pups, with an average of three; the largest recorded litter size is 17.][[0.0]]
[In smaller litters, difficulties may occur during labour due to over-sized pups.][[0.0]]
[Large litters result in higher incidences of stillbirth, but because the pups are delivered at an advanced stage of development, lack of access to the mother's milk has little effect on the mortality rate of newborns.][[0.0]]
[Cohabitating females assist in mothering duties if lactating.][[0.0]]
[Male and female guinea pigs do not differ in external appearance apart from general size.][[0.0]]
[The position of the anus is very close to the genitals in both sexes.][[0.0]]
[Female genitals are distinguished by a Y-shaped configuration formed from a vulvar flap; while the male genitals may look similar with the penis and anus forming a like shape, the penis will protrude if pressure is applied to the surrounding hair.][[0.0]]
[The male's testes may also be visible externally from scrotal swelling.][[0.0]]
[Males reach sexual maturity at 3--5 weeks; females can be fertile as early as four weeks and can carry litters before they are adults.][[0.0]]
[Females that have never given birth commonly develop irreversible fusing of the pubic symphysis, a joint in the pelvis, after six months of age.][[0.0]]
[If they become pregnant after this has happened, the birth canal will not widen sufficiently; this may lead to dystocia and death as they attempt to give birth.][[0.0]]
[Females can become pregnant 6--48 hours after giving birth, but it is not healthy for a female to be thus constantly pregnant.][[0.0]]
[Toxemia of pregnancy is common and kills many pregnant females.][[0.0]]
[Signs of toxemia include anorexia, lack of energy, excessive salivation, a sweet or fruity breath odor due to ketones, and seizures in advanced cases.][[0.0]]
[Pregnancy toxemia appears to be most common in hot climates.][[0.0]]
[Other serious complications of pregnancy can include a prolapsed uterus, hypocalcaemia, and mastitis.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Diet Grass is the guinea pig's natural diet.][[0.0]]
[Their molars are particularly suited for grinding plant matter, and grow continuously throughout the animal's life.][[0.0]]
[Most grass-eating mammals are quite large and have a long digestive tract; while guinea pigs have much longer colons than most rodents, they must also supplement their diet by coprophagy, the eating of their own feces.][[0.0]]
[However, they do not consume all their feces indiscriminately, but produce special soft pellets, called cecotropes, which recycle B vitamins, fiber, and bacteria required for proper digestion.][[0.0]]
[The cecotropes -LRB-or caecal pellets-RRB- are eaten directly from the anus, unless the guinea pig is pregnant or obese.][[1.0]]
[They share this behaviour with rabbits.][[0.0]]
[In geriatric boars or sows -LRB-the condition is rarer in young ones-RRB-, the muscles which allow the softer pellets to be expelled from the anus for consumption can become weak.][[0.0]]
[This creates a condition known as anal impaction, which prevents the boar from redigesting cecotropes, though harder pellets may pass through the impacted mass.][[0.0]]
[The condition may be temporarily alleviated by carefully expelling the impacted feces.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs benefit from feeding on fresh grass hay, such as timothy hay, in addition to food pellets which are often based from timothy.][[0.0]]
[Alfalfa is also a popular food choice; most guinea pigs will eat large amounts of alfalfa when offered it, though there exists some controversy over the feeding of alfalfa to adult guinea pigs.][[0.0]]
[Some pet owners and veterinary organizations have advised that, as a legume rather than a grass hay, alfalfa consumed in large amounts may lead to obesity, as well as bladder stones due to excess calcium, in any but pregnant and very young guinea pigs.][[0.0]]
[However, published scientific sources mention alfalfa as a source for replenishment of protein, amino acids, and fiber.][[0.0]]
[Like humans, but unlike most other mammals, guinea pigs cannot synthesize their own vitamin C and must obtain this vital nutrient from food.][[0.0]]
[If guinea pigs do not ingest enough vitamin C, they can suffer from potentially fatal scurvy.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs require about 10�mg -LRB-0.15�gr-RRB- of vitamin C daily -LRB-20�mg -LRB-0.31�gr-RRB- if pregnant-RRB-, which can be obtained through fresh, raw fruits and vegetables -LRB-such as broccoli, apple, cabbage, carrot, celery, and spinach-RRB- or through dietary supplements.][[0.0]]
[Healthy diets for guinea pigs require a complex balance of calcium, magnesium, phosphorus, potassium, and hydrogen ions; adequate amounts of vitamins E, A, and D are also necessary.][[0.0]]
[Imbalanced diets have been associated with muscular dystrophy, metastatic calcification, difficulties with pregnancy, vitamin deficiencies, and teeth problems.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs tend to be fickle eaters when it comes to fresh fruits and vegetables, having learned early in life what is and is not appropriate to consume, and their habits are difficult to change after maturity.][[0.0]]
[They do not respond well to sudden changes in diet; they may stop eating and starve rather than accepting new food types.][[0.0]]
[A constant supply of hay or other food is generally recommended, as guinea pigs feed continuously and may develop habits such as chewing on their own hair if food is not present.][[0.0]]
[Because guinea pigs' teeth grow constantly, they routinely gnaw, lest their teeth become too large for their mouth, a common problem in rodents.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs will also chew on cloth, paper, plastic, and rubber.][[0.0]]
[A number of plants are poisonous to guinea pigs, including bracken, bryony, buttercup, charlock, deadly nightshade, foxglove, hellebore, hemlock, Lily of the Valley, mayweed, monkshood, privet, ragwort, rhubarb, speedwell, toadflax, and wild celery.][[0.0]]
[Additionally, any plant which grows from a bulb -LRB-e.g., tulip and onion-RRB- is normally considered poisonous.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Health Common ailments in domestic guinea pigs include respiratory tract infections, diarrhea, scurvy -LRB-vitamin C deficiency, typically characterized by sluggishness-RRB-, abscesses due to infection -LRB-often in the neck, due to hay embedded in the throat, or from external scratches-RRB-, and infections by lice, mites, or fungus.][[0.0]]
[Mange mites -LRB-Trixacarus caviae-RRB- are a common cause of hair loss, and other symptoms may also include excessive scratching, unusually aggressive behavior when touched -LRB-due to pain-RRB-, and, in some instances, seizures.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs may also suffer from ``running lice'' -LRB-Gliricola porcelli-RRB-, a small white insect which can be seen moving through the hair; the eggs of these lice, which appear as black or white specks attached to the hair, are sometimes referred to as ``static lice''.][[0.0]]
[Giving a bath with neem oil soap is a gentle and effective way of ridding the pig of lice.][[0.0]]
[Other causes of hair loss can be due to hormonal upsets caused by underlying medical conditions such as ovarian cysts.][[0.0]]
[Neem oil is an effective treatment for the parasitic skin problems in pet Guinea Pigs, at a dilution rate of four parts of carrier oil to one part of Neem oil.][[0.0]]
[Foreign bodies, especially small pieces of hay or straw, can become lodged in the eyes of guinea pigs, resulting in excessive blinking, tearing, and in some cases an opaque film over the eye due to corneal ulcer.][[0.0]]
[Hay or straw dust can also cause sneezing.][[0.0]]
[While it is normal for guinea pigs to sneeze periodically, frequent sneezing may be a symptom of pneumonia, especially in response to atmospheric changes.][[0.0]]
[Pneumonia may also be accompanied by torticollis and can be fatal.][[0.0]]
[Because the guinea pig has a stout, compact body, the animal more easily tolerates excessive cold than excessive heat.][[0.0]]
[Its normal body temperature is 101--104�°F -LRB-38--40�°C-RRB-, and so its ideal ambient air temperature range is similar to the human's, about 65--75�°F -LRB-18--24�°C-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Consistent ambient temperatures in excess of 90�°F -LRB-32�°C-RRB- have been linked to hyperthermia and death, especially among pregnant sows.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs are not well suited to environments that feature wind or frequent drafts, and respond poorly to extremes of humidity outside of the range of 30--70%.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs are prey animals whose survival instinct is to mask pain and signs of illness, and many times health problems may not be apparent until a condition is severe or in its advanced stages.][[0.0]]
[Treatment of disease is made more difficult by the extreme sensitivity guinea pigs have to most antibiotics, including penicillin, which kill off the intestinal flora and quickly bring on episodes of diarrhea and in some cases, death.][[0.0]]
[Similar to the inherited genetic diseases of other breeds of animal -LRB-such as hip dysplasia in canines-RRB-, a number of genetic abnormalities of guinea pigs have been reported.][[0.0]]
[Most commonly, the roan coloration of Abyssinian guinea pigs is associated with congenital eye disorders and problems with the digestive system.][[0.0]]
[Other genetic disorders include ``waltzing disease'' -LRB-deafness coupled with a tendency to run in circles-RRB-, palsy, and tremor conditions.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Pets If handled correctly early in their life, guinea pigs become amenable to being picked up and carried, and seldom bite or scratch.][[0.0]]
[They are timid explorers, and rarely attempt to escape from their cages, even when an opportunity presents itself.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs who become familiar with their owner will whistle on the owner's approach; they will also learn to whistle in response to the rustling of plastic bags or the opening of refrigerator doors, where their food is most commonly stored.][[0.0]]
[Domesticated guinea pigs come in many breeds, which have been developed since their introduction to Europe and North America.][[0.0]]
[These varieties vary in hair and color composition.][[0.0]]
[The most common varieties found in pet stores are the English shorthair -LRB-also known as the American-RRB-, which have a short, smooth coat, and the Abyssinian, whose coat is ruffled with cowlicks, or rosettes.][[0.0]]
[Also popular among breeders are the Peruvian and the Sheltie -LRB-or Silkie-RRB-, both straight longhair breeds, and the Texel, a curly longhair.][[0.0]]
[Cavy Clubs and Associations dedicated to the showing and breeding of guinea pigs have been established worldwide.][[1.0]]
[The American Cavy Breeders Association, an adjunct to the American Rabbit Breeders' Association, is the governing body in the United States and Canada.][[0.0]]
[The British Cavy Council governs cavy clubs in the United Kingdom.][[0.0]]
[Similar organizations exist in Australia -LRB-Australian National Cavy Council-RRB- and New Zealand -LRB-New Zealand Cavy Club-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Each club publishes its own Standard of Perfection and determines which breeds are eligible for showing.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Cultural and media influence As a result of their widespread popularity in human domestic life, and especially because of their popularity in households with children, guinea pigs have shown a presence in culture and media.][[0.0]]
[Some noted appearances of the animal in literature are The Fairy Caravan, a novel by Beatrix Potter, and Michael Bond's Olga da Polga series for children, both of which feature guinea pigs as the central protagonist.][[0.0]]
[Another appearance is in The Magician's Nephew by C. S. Lewis: in the first -LRB-chronologically-RRB- of his The Chronicles of Narnia series, a guinea pig is the first creature to travel to the Wood between the Worlds.][[0.0]]
[The short story Pigs Is Pigs by Ellis Parker Butler is a tale of bureaucratic incompetence; two guinea pigs held at a train station breed unchecked while humans argue as to whether they are ``pigs'' for the purpose of determining freight charges.][[0.0]]
[In the Golden Hamster Saga books, there are two guinea pigs named Enrico and Caruso who are modern-day thespians -LRB-named after Enrico Caruso-RRB- who serve as secondary characters, and often irritate the main character, Freddy Auratus who strongly dislikes their acting antics.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs have also been featured in film and television.][[0.0]]
[In the TV movie Shredderman Rules, the main character and the main character's crush both have guinea pigs which both play a minor part in the plot.][[0.0]]
[A guinea pig named Rodney, voiced by Chris Rock, was a prominent character in the 1998 film Dr. Dolittle and Linny the Guinea pig is a co-star on Nick Jr.'s Wonder Pets.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs were used in some major advertising campaigns in the 1990s and 2000s, notably for Egg Banking plc, Snapple, and Blockbuster Video.][[0.0]]
[The Blockbuster campaign is considered by some guinea pig advocates to have been a factor in the rise of cohousing guinea pigs and rabbits.][[0.0]]
[In the South Park season 12 episode ``Pandemic 2: The Startling'',giant guinea pigs dressed in costumes, are portrayed as rampaging over the entire Earth.][[0.0]]
[The Walt Disney Pictures movie G-Force, released in the United States on July 24, 2009, features a group of highly intelligent guinea pigs trained as operatives of the U.S. government.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Scientific research The use of guinea pigs in scientific experimentation dates back at least to the 17th century, when the Italian biologists Marcello Malpighi and Carlo Fracassati conducted vivisections of guinea pigs in their examinations of anatomic structures.][[1.0]]
[In 1780, Antoine Lavoisier used a guinea pig in his experiments with the calorimeter, a device used to measure heat production.][[0.0]]
[The heat from the guinea pig's respiration melted snow surrounding the calorimeter, showing that respiratory gas exchange is a combustion, similar to a candle burning.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs played a major role in the establishment of germ theory in the late 19th century, through the experiments of Louis Pasteur, Émile Roux, and Robert Koch.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs have been launched into orbital space-flight several times, first by the USSR on the Sputnik 9 biosatellite of March 9, 1961 - with a successful recovery.][[0.0]]
[China also launched and recovered a biosatellite in 1990 which included guinea pigs as passengers.][[0.0]]
[In English, the term guinea pig is commonly used as a metaphor for a subject of scientific experimentation, or any experiment or test in modern times.][[0.0]]
[This dates back to the early 20th century; the Oxford English Dictionary notes its first usage in this capacity in 1913.][[0.0]]
[In 1933, Consumers Research founders F. J. Schlink and Arthur Kallet wrote a book entitled 100,000,000 Guinea Pigs, extending the metaphor to consumer society.][[0.0]]
[The book became a national bestseller in the United States, thus further popularizing the term, and spurred the growth of the consumer protection movement.][[0.0]]
[The negative connotation of the term was later employed in the novel The Guinea Pigs by Czech author Ludvík Vaculík as an allegory for Soviet totalitarianism.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs were popular laboratory animals until the later 20th century; about 2.5 million guinea pigs were used annually in the U.S. for research in the 1960s, but that total decreased to about 375,000 by the mid-1990s.][[0.0]]
[As of 2007, they constitute approximately 2% of the current total of laboratory animals.][[0.0]]
[In the past they were widely used to standardize vaccines and antiviral agents; they were also often employed in studies on the production of antibodies in response to extreme allergic reactions, or anaphylaxis.][[0.0]]
[Less common uses included research in pharmacology and irradiation.][[0.0]]
[Since the middle 20th century, they have been replaced in laboratory contexts primarily by mice and rats.][[1.0]]
[This is in part because research into the genetics of guinea pigs has lagged behind that of other rodents, although geneticists W. E. Castle and Sewall Wright made a number of contributions to this area of study, especially regarding coat color.][[0.0]]
[In 2004, the U.S.'s National Human Genome Research Institute announced plans to sequence the genome of the domestic guinea pig.][[0.0]]
[The guinea pig was most extensively implemented in research and diagnosis of infectious diseases.][[0.0]]
[Common uses included identification of brucellosis, Chagas disease, cholera, diphtheria, foot-and-mouth disease, glanders, Q fever, Rocky Mountain spotted fever, and various strains of typhus.][[0.0]]
[They are still frequently used to diagnose tuberculosis, since they are easily infected by human tuberculosis bacteria.][[1.0]]
[Because guinea pigs are one of the few animals which, like humans, cannot synthesize vitamin C but must obtain it from their diet, they are ideal for researching scurvy.][[0.0]]
[From the accidental discovery in 1907 that scurvy could be induced in guinea pigs, to their use to prove the chemical structure of the ``ascorbutic factor'' in 1932, the guinea pig model proved a crucial part of vitamin C research.][[0.0]]
[Complement, an important component for serology, was first isolated from the blood of the guinea pig.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pigs have an unusual insulin mutation, and are a suitable species for the generation of anti-insulin antibodies.][[0.0]]
[Present at a level 10 times that found in other mammals, the insulin in guinea pigs may be important in growth regulation, a role usually played by growth hormone.][[0.0]]
[Additionally, guinea pigs have been identified as model organisms for the study of juvenile diabetes and, because of the frequency of pregnancy toxemia, of pre-eclampsia in human females.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pig strains used in scientific research are primarily outbred strains.][[1.0]]
[Aside from the common American or English stock, the two main outbred strains in laboratory use are the Hartley and Dunkin-Hartley; these English strains are albino, although pigmented strains are also available.][[0.0]]
[Inbred strains are less common and are usually used for very specific research, such as immune system molecular biology.][[0.0]]
[Of the inbred strains that have been created, the two that are still used with any frequency are, following Sewall Wright's designations, ``Strain 2'' and ``Strain 13''.][[0.0]]
[Hairless breeds of guinea pigs have been used in scientific research since the 1980s, particularly for dermatological studies.][[0.0]]
[A hairless and immunodeficient breed was the result of a spontaneous genetic mutation in inbred laboratory strains from the Hartley stock at the Eastman Kodak Company in 1979.][[0.0]]
[An immunocompetent hairless breed was also identified by the Institute Armand Frappier in 1978, and Charles River Laboratories has reproduced this breed for research since 1982.][[0.0]]
[Cavy fanciers then began acquiring hairless breeds, and the pet hairless varieties are referred to as ``skinny pigs''.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- As food Guinea pigs -LRB-called cuy, cuye, curí-RRB- were originally domesticated for their meat in the Andes.][[0.0]]
[Traditionally, the animal was usually reserved for ceremonial meals by indigenous people in the Andean highlands, but since the 1960s it has become more socially acceptable for consumption by all people.][[0.0]]
[It continues to be a major part of the diet in Peru and Bolivia, particularly in the Andes Mountains highlands; it is also eaten in some areas of Ecuador -LRB-mainly in the Sierra-RRB- and Colombia.][[0.0]]
[Because guinea pigs require much less room than traditional livestock and reproduce extremely quickly, they are a more profitable source of food and income than many traditional stock animals, such as pigs and cows; moreover, they can be raised in an urban environment.][[0.0]]
[Both rural and urban families raise guinea pigs for supplementary income, and the animals are commonly bought and sold at local markets and large-scale municipal fairs.][[0.0]]
[Guinea pig meat is high in protein and low in fat and cholesterol, and is described as being similar to rabbit and the dark meat of chicken.][[0.0]]
[The animal may be served fried -LRB-chactado or frito-RRB-, broiled -LRB-asado-RRB-, or roasted -LRB-al horno-RRB-, and in urban restaurants may also be served in a casserole or a fricassee.][[0.0]]
[Ecuadorians commonly consume sopa or locro de cuy, a soup dish.][[0.0]]
[Pachamanca or huatia, a process similar to barbecueing, is also popular, and is usually served with corn beer -LRB-chicha-RRB- in traditional settings.][[0.0]]
[Peruvians consume an estimated 65 million guinea pigs each year, and the animal is so entrenched in the culture that one famous painting of the Last Supper in the main cathedral in Cusco shows Christ and the twelve disciples dining on guinea pig.][[0.0]]
[The animal remains an important aspect of certain religious events in both rural and urban areas of Peru.][[0.0]]
[A religious celebration known as jaca tsariy -LRB-``collecting the cuys''-RRB- is a major festival in many villages in the Antonio Raimondi province of eastern Peru, and is celebrated in smaller ceremonies in Lima.][[0.0]]
[It is a syncretistic event, combining elements of Catholicism and pre-Columbian religious practices, and revolves around the celebration of local patron saints.][[0.0]]
[The exact form that the jaca tsariy takes differs from town to town; in some localities, a sirvinti -LRB-servant-RRB- is appointed to go from door to door, collecting donations of guinea pigs, while in others, guinea pigs may be brought to a communal area to be released in a mock bullfight.][[0.0]]
[Meals such as cuy chactado are always served as part of these festivities, and the killing and serving of the animal is framed by some communities as a symbolic satire of local politicians or important figures.][[0.0]]
[In the Tungurahua and Cotopaxi provinces of central Ecuador, guinea pigs are employed in the celebrations surrounding the feast of Corpus Christi as part of the Ensayo, which is a community meal, and the Octava, where castillos -LRB-greased poles-RRB- are erected with prizes tied to the crossbars, from which several guinea pigs may be hung.][[0.0]]
[The Peruvian town of Churin has an annual festival which involves dressing guinea pigs in elaborate costumes for a competition.][[0.0]]
[Andean immigrants in New York City raise and sell guinea pigs for meat, and some ethnic restaurants in major United States cities serve cuy as a delicacy.][[0.0]]
[Peruvian research universities, especially La Molina National Agrarian University, began experimental programs in the 1960s with the intention of breeding larger-sized guinea pigs.][[0.0]]
[Subsequent university efforts have sought to change breeding and husbandry procedures in South America, in order to make the raising of guinea pigs as livestock more economically sustainable.][[0.0]]
[In the 1990s and 2000s, the university began exporting the larger breed guinea pigs to Europe, Japan, and the United States in the hope of increasing human consumption outside of these countries in northern South America.][[1.0]]
[Efforts have also been made to introduce guinea pig husbandry in developing countries in West Africa.][[1.0]]
[Nevertheless, as a food source they are still generally considered taboo in other countries in America and Europe; in reality television, guinea pig meat has been consumed as an exotic dish by such Western celebrity chefs as Andrew Zimmern -LRB-for his show Bizarre Foods-RRB- and Anthony Bourdain in No Reservations.][[1.0]]

[-LSB-edit-RSB- History The nucleus was the first organelle to be discovered.][[1.0]]
[The probably oldest preserved drawing dates back to the early microscopist Antonie van Leeuwenhoek -LRB-1632 -- 1723-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[He observed a ``Lumen'', the nucleus, in the red blood cells of salmon .][[0.0]]
[Unlike mammalian red blood cells, those of other vertebrates still possess nuclei.][[0.0]]
[The nucleus was also described by Franz Bauer in 1804 and in more detail in 1831 by Scottish botanist Robert Brown in a talk at the Linnean Society of London.][[0.0]]
[Brown was studying orchids microscopically when he observed an opaque area, which he called the areola or nucleus, in the cells of the flower's outer layer.][[0.0]]
[He did not suggest a potential function.][[0.0]]
[In 1838 Matthias Schleiden proposed that the nucleus plays a role in generating cells, thus he introduced the name ``Cytoblast'' -LRB-cell builder-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[He believed that he had observed new cells assembling around ``cytoblasts''.][[0.0]]
[Franz Meyen was a strong opponent of this view having already described cells multiplying by division and believing that many cells would have no nuclei.][[0.0]]
[The idea that cells can be generated de novo, by the ``cytoblast'' or otherwise, contradicted work by Robert Remak -LRB-1852-RRB- and Rudolf Virchow -LRB-1855-RRB- who decisively propagated the new paradigm that cells are generated solely by cells -LRB-``Omnis cellula e cellula''-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The function of the nucleus remained unclear.][[0.0]]
[Between 1876 and 1878 Oscar Hertwig published several studies on the fertilization of sea urchin eggs, showing that the nucleus of the sperm enters the oocyte and fuses with its nucleus.][[0.0]]
[This was the first time it was suggested that an individual develops from a -LRB-single-RRB- nucleated cell.][[0.0]]
[This was in contradiction to Ernst Haeckel's theory that the complete phylogeny of a species would be repeated during embryonic development, including generation of the first nucleated cell from a ``Monerula'', a structureless mass of primordial mucus -LRB-``Urschleim''-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Therefore, the necessity of the sperm nucleus for fertilization was discussed for quite some time.][[0.0]]
[However, Hertwig confirmed his observation in other animal groups, e.g. amphibians and molluscs.][[0.0]]
[Eduard Strasburger produced the same results for plants -LRB-1884-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[This paved the way to assign the nucleus an important role in heredity.][[0.0]]
[In 1873 August Weismann postulated the equivalence of the maternal and paternal germ cells for heredity.][[0.0]]
[The function of the nucleus as carrier of genetic information became clear only later, after mitosis was discovered and the Mendelian rules were rediscovered at the beginning of the 20th century; the chromosome theory of heredity was developed.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Structures The nucleus is the largest cellular organelle in animals.][[0.0]]
[In mammalian cells, the average diameter of the nucleus is approximately 6 micrometers -LRB-μm-RRB-, which occupies about 10% of the total cell volume.][[1.0]]
[The viscous liquid within it is called nucleoplasm, and is similar in composition to the cytosol found outside the nucleus.][[0.0]]
[It appears as a dense, roughly spherical organelle.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Nuclear envelope and pores The nuclear envelope otherwise known as nuclear membrane consists of two cellular membranes, an inner and an outer membrane, arranged parallel to one another and separated by 10 to 50 nanometers -LRB-nm-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The nuclear envelope completely encloses the nucleus and separates the cell's genetic material from the surrounding cytoplasm, serving as a barrier to prevent macromolecules from diffusing freely between the nucleoplasm and the cytoplasm.][[1.0]]
[The outer nuclear membrane is continuous with the membrane of the rough endoplasmic reticulum -LRB-RER-RRB-, and is similarly studded with ribosomes.][[0.0]]
[The space between the membranes is called the perinuclear space and is continuous with the RER lumen.][[0.0]]
[Nuclear pores, which provide aqueous channels through the envelope, are composed of multiple proteins, collectively referred to as nucleoporins.][[0.0]]
[The pores are about 125 million daltons in molecular weight and consist of around 50 -LRB-in yeast-RRB- to 100 proteins -LRB-in vertebrates-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The pores are 100 nm in total diameter; however, the gap through which molecules freely diffuse is only about 9 nm wide, due to the presence of regulatory systems within the center of the pore.][[0.0]]
[This size allows the free passage of small water-soluble molecules while preventing larger molecules, such as nucleic acids and larger proteins, from inappropriately entering or exiting the nucleus.][[1.0]]
[These large molecules must be actively transported into the nucleus instead.][[0.0]]
[The nucleus of a typical mammalian cell will have about 3000 to 4000 pores throughout its envelope, each of which contains a donut-shaped, eightfold-symmetric ring-shaped structure at a position where the inner and outer membranes fuse.][[0.0]]
[Attached to the ring is a structure called the nuclear basket that extends into the nucleoplasm, and a series of filamentous extensions that reach into the cytoplasm.][[0.0]]
[Both structures serve to mediate binding to nuclear transport proteins.][[0.0]]
[Most proteins, ribosomal subunits, and some RNAs are transported through the pore complexes in a process mediated by a family of transport factors known as karyopherins.][[0.0]]
[Those karyopherins that mediate movement into the nucleus are also called importins, while those that mediate movement out of the nucleus are called exportins.][[0.0]]
[Most karyopherins interact directly with their cargo, although some use adaptor proteins.][[0.0]]
[Steroid hormones such as cortisol and aldosterone, as well as other small lipid-soluble molecules involved in intercellular signaling can diffuse through the cell membrane and into the cytoplasm, where they bind nuclear receptor proteins that are trafficked into the nucleus.][[0.0]]
[There they serve as transcription factors when bound to their ligand; in the absence of ligand many such receptors function as histone deacetylases that repress gene expression.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Nuclear lamina In animal cells, two networks of intermediate filaments provide the nucleus with mechanical support: the nuclear lamina forms an organized meshwork on the internal face of the envelope, while less organized support is provided on the cytosolic face of the envelope.][[0.0]]
[Both systems provide structural support for the nuclear envelope and anchoring sites for chromosomes and nuclear pores.][[0.0]]
[The nuclear lamina is mostly composed of lamin proteins.][[0.0]]
[Like all proteins, lamins are synthesized in the cytoplasm and later transported into the nucleus interior, where they are assembled before being incorporated into the existing network of nuclear lamina.][[0.0]]
[Lamins are also found inside the nucleoplasm where they form another regular structure, known as the nucleoplasmic veil, that is visible using fluorescence microscopy.][[0.0]]
[The actual function of the veil is not clear, although it is excluded from the nucleolus and is present during interphase.][[0.0]]
[The lamin structures that make up the veil bind chromatin and disrupting their structure inhibits transcription of protein-coding genes.][[0.0]]
[Like the components of other intermediate filaments, the lamin monomer contains an alpha-helical domain used by two monomers to coil around each other, forming a dimer structure called a coiled coil.][[0.0]]
[Two of these dimer structures then join side by side, in an antiparallel arrangement, to form a tetramer called a protofilament.][[0.0]]
[Eight of these protofilaments form a lateral arrangement that is twisted to form a ropelike filament.][[0.0]]
[These filaments can be assembled or disassembled in a dynamic manner, meaning that changes in the length of the filament depend on the competing rates of filament addition and removal.][[0.0]]
[Mutations in lamin genes leading to defects in filament assembly are known as laminopathies.][[0.0]]
[The most notable laminopathy is the family of diseases known as progeria, which causes the appearance of premature aging in its sufferers.][[0.0]]
[The exact mechanism by which the associated biochemical changes give rise to the aged phenotype is not well understood.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Chromosomes The cell nucleus contains the majority of the cell's genetic material, in the form of multiple linear DNA molecules organized into structures called chromosomes.][[1.0]]
[During most of the cell cycle these are organized in a DNA-protein complex known as chromatin, and during cell division the chromatin can be seen to form the well defined chromosomes familiar from a karyotype.][[0.0]]
[A small fraction of the cell's genes are located instead in the mitochondria.][[0.0]]
[There are two types of chromatin.][[0.0]]
[Euchromatin is the less compact DNA form, and contains genes that are frequently expressed by the cell.][[0.0]]
[The other type, heterochromatin, is the more compact form, and contains DNA that are infrequently transcribed.][[0.0]]
[This structure is further categorized into facultative heterochromatin, consisting of genes that are organized as heterochromatin only in certain cell types or at certain stages of development, and constitutive heterochromatin that consists of chromosome structural components such as telomeres and centromeres.][[0.0]]
[During interphase the chromatin organizes itself into discrete individual patches, called chromosome territories.][[0.0]]
[Active genes, which are generally found in the euchromatic region of the chromosome, tend to be located towards the chromosome's territory boundary.][[0.0]]
[Antibodies to certain types of chromatin organization, particularly nucleosomes, have been associated with a number of autoimmune diseases, such as systemic lupus erythematosus.][[0.0]]
[These are known as anti-nuclear antibodies -LRB-ANA-RRB- and have also been observed in concert with multiple sclerosis as part of general immune system dysfunction.][[0.0]]
[As in the case of progeria, the role played by the antibodies in inducing the symptoms of autoimmune diseases is not obvious.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Nucleolus The nucleolus is a discrete densely stained structure found in the nucleus.][[0.0]]
[It is not surrounded by a membrane, and is sometimes called a suborganelle.][[0.0]]
[It forms around tandem repeats of rDNA, DNA coding for ribosomal RNA -LRB-rRNA-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[These regions are called nucleolar organizer regions -LRB-NOR-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The main roles of the nucleolus are to synthesize rRNA and assemble ribosomes.][[0.0]]
[The structural cohesion of the nucleolus depends on its activity, as ribosomal assembly in the nucleolus results in the transient association of nucleolar components, facilitating further ribosomal assembly, and hence further association.][[0.0]]
[This model is supported by observations that inactivation of rDNA results in intermingling of nucleolar structures.][[0.0]]
[The first step in ribosomal assembly is transcription of the rDNA, by a protein called RNA polymerase I, forming a large pre-rRNA precursor.][[0.0]]
[This is cleaved into the subunits 5.8S, 18S, and 28S rRNA.][[0.0]]
[The transcription, post-transcriptional processing, and assembly of rRNA occurs in the nucleolus, aided by small nucleolar RNA -LRB-snoRNA-RRB- molecules, some of which are derived from spliced introns from messenger RNAs encoding genes related to ribosomal function.][[1.0]]
[The assembled ribosomal subunits are the largest structures passed through the nuclear pores.][[0.0]]
[When observed under the electron microscope, the nucleolus can be seen to consist of three distinguishable regions: the innermost fibrillar centers -LRB-FCs-RRB-, surrounded by the dense fibrillar component -LRB-DFC-RRB-, which in turn is bordered by the granular component -LRB-GC-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Transcription of the rDNA occurs either in the FC or at the FC-DFC boundary, and therefore when rDNA transcription in the cell is increased more FCs are detected.][[0.0]]
[Most of the cleavage and modification of rRNAs occurs in the DFC, while the latter steps involving protein assembly onto the ribosomal subunits occurs in the GC.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Other subnuclear bodies Besides the nucleolus, the nucleus contains a number of other non-membrane delineated bodies.][[1.0]]
[These include Cajal bodies, Gemini of coiled bodies, polymorphic interphase karyosomal association -LRB-PIKA-RRB-, promyelocytic leukaemia -LRB-PML-RRB- bodies, paraspeckles and splicing speckles.][[0.0]]
[Although little is known about a number of these domains, they are significant in that they show that the nucleoplasm is not uniform mixture, but rather contains organized functional subdomains.][[0.0]]
[Other subnuclear structures appear as part of abnormal disease processes.][[0.0]]
[For example, the presence of small intranuclear rods have been reported in some cases of nemaline myopathy.][[0.0]]
[This condition typically results from mutations in actin, and the rods themselves consist of mutant actin as well as other cytoskeletal proteins.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Cajal bodies and gems A nucleus typically contains between 1 and 10 compact structures called Cajal bodies or coiled bodies -LRB-CB-RRB-, whose diameter measures between 0.2�µm and 2.0�µm depending on the cell type and species.][[0.0]]
[When seen under an electron microscope, they resemble balls of tangled thread and are dense foci of distribution for the protein coilin.][[0.0]]
[CBs are involved in a number of different roles relating to RNA processing, specifically small nucleolar RNA -LRB-snoRNA-RRB- and small nuclear RNA -LRB-snRNA-RRB- maturation, and histone mRNA modification.][[0.0]]
[Similar to Cajal bodies are Gemini of coiled bodies, or gems, whose name is derived from the Gemini constellation in reference to their close ``twin'' relationship with CBs.][[0.0]]
[Gems are similar in size and shape to CBs, and in fact are virtually indistinguishable under the microscope.][[0.0]]
[Unlike CBs, gems do not contain small nuclear ribonucleoproteins -LRB-snRNPs-RRB-, but do contain a protein called survivor of motor neurons -LRB-SMN-RRB- whose function relates to snRNP biogenesis.][[0.0]]
[Gems are believed to assist CBs in snRNP biogenesis, though it has also been suggested from microscopy evidence that CBs and gems are different manifestations of the same structure.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- PIKA and PTF domains PIKA domains, or polymorphic interphase karyosomal associations, were first described in microscopy studies in 1991.][[0.0]]
[Their function was and remains unclear, though they were not thought to be associated with active DNA replication, transcription, or RNA processing.][[0.0]]
[They have been found to often associate with discrete domains defined by dense localization of the transcription factor PTF, which promotes transcription of snRNA.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- PML bodies Promyelocytic leukaemia bodies -LRB-PML bodies-RRB- are spherical bodies found scattered throughout the nucleoplasm, measuring around 0.2--1.0�µm.][[0.0]]
[They are known by a number of other names, including nuclear domain 10 -LRB-ND10-RRB-, Kremer bodies, and PML oncogenic domains.][[0.0]]
[They are often seen in the nucleus in association with Cajal bodies and cleavage bodies.][[0.0]]
[It has been suggested that they play a role in regulating transcription.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Paraspeckles Discovered by Fox et al. in 2002, paraspeckles are irregularly shaped compartments in the nucleus' interchromatin space.][[0.0]]
[First documented in HeLa cells, where there are generally 10--30 per nucleus, paraspeckles are now known to also exist in all human primary cells, transformed cell lines and tissue sections.][[0.0]]
[Their name is derived from their distribution in the nucleus; the ``para'' is short for parallel and the ``speckles'' refers to the splicing speckles to which they are always in close proximity.][[0.0]]
[Paraspeckles are dynamic structures that are altered in response to changes in cellular metabolic activity.][[0.0]]
[They are transcription dependent and in the absence of RNA Pol II transcription, the paraspeckle disappears and all of its associated protein components -LRB-PSP1, p54nrb, PSP2, CFI-LRB-m-RRB-68 and PSF-RRB- form a crescent shaped perinucleolar cap in the nucleolus.][[0.0]]
[This phenomenon is demonstrated during the cell cycle.][[0.0]]
[In the cell cycle, paraspeckles are present during interphase and during all of mitosis except for telophase.][[0.0]]
[During telophase, when the two daughter nuclei are formed, there is no RNA Pol II transcription so the protein components instead form a perinucleolar cap.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Splicing speckles Sometimes referred to as interchromatin granule clusters or as splicing-factor compartments, speckles are rich in splicing snRNPs and other splicing proteins necessary for pre-mRNA processing.][[0.0]]
[Because of a cell's changing requirements, the composition and location of these bodies changes according to mRNA transcription and regulation via phosphorylation of specific proteins.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Function The main function of the cell nucleus is to control gene expression and mediate the replication of DNA during the cell cycle.][[1.0]]
[The nucleus provides a site for genetic transcription that is segregated from the location of translation in the cytoplasm, allowing levels of gene regulation that are not available to prokaryotes.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Cell compartmentalization The nuclear envelope allows the nucleus to control its contents, and separate them from the rest of the cytoplasm where necessary.][[0.0]]
[This is important for controlling processes on either side of the nuclear membrane.][[0.0]]
[In some cases where a cytoplasmic process needs to be restricted, a key participant is removed to the nucleus, where it interacts with transcription factors to downregulate the production of certain enzymes in the pathway.][[0.0]]
[This regulatory mechanism occurs in the case of glycolysis, a cellular pathway for breaking down glucose to produce energy.][[0.0]]
[Hexokinase is an enzyme responsible for the first the step of glycolysis, forming glucose-6-phosphate from glucose.][[0.0]]
[At high concentrations of fructose-6-phosphate, a molecule made later from glucose-6-phosphate, a regulator protein removes hexokinase to the nucleus, where it forms a transcriptional repressor complex with nuclear proteins to reduce the expression of genes involved in glycolysis.][[0.0]]
[In order to control which genes are being transcribed, the cell separates some transcription factor proteins responsible for regulating gene expression from physical access to the DNA until they are activated by other signaling pathways.][[1.0]]
[This prevents even low levels of inappropriate gene expression.][[0.0]]
[For example in the case of NF-κB-controlled genes, which are involved in most inflammatory responses, transcription is induced in response to a signal pathway such as that initiated by the signaling molecule TNF-α, binds to a cell membrane receptor, resulting in the recruitment of signalling proteins, and eventually activating the transcription factor NF-κB.][[0.0]]
[A nuclear localisation signal on the NF-κB protein allows it to be transported through the nuclear pore and into the nucleus, where it stimulates the transcription of the target genes.][[0.0]]
[The compartmentalization allows the cell to prevent translation of unspliced mRNA.][[0.0]]
[Eukaryotic mRNA contains introns that must be removed before being translated to produce functional proteins.][[0.0]]
[The splicing is done inside the nucleus before the mRNA can be accessed by ribosomes for translation.][[0.0]]
[Without the nucleus ribosomes would translate newly transcribed -LRB-unprocessed-RRB- mRNA resulting in misformed and nonfunctional proteins.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Gene expression Gene expression first involves transcription, in which DNA is used as a template to produce RNA.][[0.0]]
[In the case of genes encoding proteins, that RNA produced from this process is messenger RNA -LRB-mRNA-RRB-, which then needs to be translated by ribosomes to form a protein.][[1.0]]
[As ribosomes are located outside the nucleus, mRNA produced needs to be exported.][[0.0]]
[Since the nucleus is the site of transcription, it also contains a variety of proteins which either directly mediate transcription or are involved in regulating the process.][[0.0]]
[These proteins include helicases that unwind the double-stranded DNA molecule to facilitate access to it, RNA polymerases that synthesize the growing RNA molecule, topoisomerases that change the amount of supercoiling in DNA, helping it wind and unwind, as well as a large variety of transcription factors that regulate expression.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Processing of pre-mRNA Newly synthesized mRNA molecules are known as primary transcripts or pre-mRNA.][[0.0]]
[They must undergo post-transcriptional modification in the nucleus before being exported to the cytoplasm; mRNA that appears in the nucleus without these modifications is degraded rather than used for protein translation.][[0.0]]
[The three main modifications are 5' capping, 3' polyadenylation, and RNA splicing.][[0.0]]
[While in the nucleus, pre-mRNA is associated with a variety of proteins in complexes known as heterogeneous ribonucleoprotein particles -LRB-hnRNPs-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Addition of the 5' cap occurs co-transcriptionally and is the first step in post-transcriptional modification.][[0.0]]
[The 3' poly-adenine tail is only added after transcription is complete.][[0.0]]
[RNA splicing, carried out by a complex called the spliceosome, is the process by which introns, or regions of DNA that do not code for protein, are removed from the pre-mRNA and the remaining exons connected to re-form a single continuous molecule.][[0.0]]
[This process normally occurs after 5' capping and 3' polyadenylation but can begin before synthesis is complete in transcripts with many exons.][[0.0]]
[Many pre-mRNAs, including those encoding antibodies, can be spliced in multiple ways to produce different mature mRNAs that encode different protein sequences.][[0.0]]
[This process is known as alternative splicing, and allows production of a large variety of proteins from a limited amount of DNA.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Dynamics and regulation -LSB-edit-RSB- Nuclear transport The entry and exit of large molecules from the nucleus is tightly controlled by the nuclear pore complexes.][[0.0]]
[Although small molecules can enter the nucleus without regulation, macromolecules such as RNA and proteins require association karyopherins called importins to enter the nucleus and exportins to exit.][[0.0]]
[``Cargo'' proteins that must be translocated from the cytoplasm to the nucleus contain short amino acid sequences known as nuclear localization signals which are bound by importins, while those transported from the nucleus to the cytoplasm carry nuclear export signals bound by exportins.][[0.0]]
[The ability of importins and exportins to transport their cargo is regulated by GTPases, enzymes that hydrolyze the molecule guanosine triphosphate to release energy.][[1.0]]
[The key GTPase in nuclear transport is Ran, which can bind either GTP or GDP -LRB-guanosine diphosphate-RRB- depending on whether it is located in the nucleus or the cytoplasm.][[0.0]]
[Whereas importins depend on RanGTP to dissociate from their cargo, exportins require RanGTP in order to bind to their cargo.][[0.0]]
[Nuclear import depends on the importin binding its cargo in the cytoplasm and carrying it through the nuclear pore into the nucleus.][[0.0]]
[Inside the nucleus, RanGTP acts to separate the cargo from the importin, allowing the importin to exit the nucleus and be reused.][[0.0]]
[Nuclear export is similar, as the exportin binds the cargo inside the nucleus in a process facilitated by RanGTP, exits through the nuclear pore, and separates from its cargo in the cytoplasm.][[0.0]]
[Specialized export proteins exist for translocation of mature mRNA and tRNA to the cytoplasm after post-transcriptional modification is complete.][[0.0]]
[This quality-control mechanism is important due to the these molecules' central role in protein translation; mis-expression of a protein due to incomplete excision of exons or mis-incorporation of amino acids could have negative consequences for the cell; thus incompletely modified RNA that reaches the cytoplasm is degraded rather than used in translation.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Assembly and disassembly During its lifetime a nucleus may be broken down, either in the process of cell division or as a consequence of apoptosis, a regulated form of cell death.][[0.0]]
[During these events, the structural components of the nucleus--the envelope and lamina--are systematically degraded.][[0.0]]
[During the cell cycle the cell divides to form two cells.][[0.0]]
[In order for this process to be possible, each of the new daughter cells must have a full set of genes, a process requiring replication of the chromosomes as well as segregation of the separate sets.][[0.0]]
[This occurs by the replicated chromosomes, the sister chromatids, attaching to microtubules, which in turn are attached to different centrosomes.][[0.0]]
[The sister chromatids can then be pulled to separate locations in the cell.][[0.0]]
[In many cells the centrosome is located in the cytoplasm, outside the nucleus, the microtubules would be unable to attach to the chromatids in the presence of the nuclear envelope.][[0.0]]
[Therefore the early stages in the cell cycle, beginning in prophase and until around prometaphase, the nuclear membrane is dismantled.][[0.0]]
[Likewise, during the same period, the nuclear lamina is also disassembled, a process regulated by phosphorylation of the lamins.][[0.0]]
[Towards the end of the cell cycle, the nuclear membrane is reformed, and around the same time, the nuclear lamina are reassembled by dephosphorylating the lamins.][[0.0]]
[However, in dinoflagellates the nuclear envelope remains intact, the centrosomes are located in the cytoplasm, and the microtubules come in contact with chromosomes, whose centromeric regions are incorporated into the nuclear envelope -LRB-the so-called closed mitosis with extranuclear spindle-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[In many other protists -LRB-e.g. ciliates, sporozoans-RRB- and fungi the centrosomes are intranuclear, and their nuclear envelope also does not disassemle during cell division.][[0.0]]
[Apoptosis is a controlled process in which the cell's structural components are destroyed, resulting in death of the cell.][[0.0]]
[Changes associated with apoptosis directly affect the nucleus and its contents, for example in the condensation of chromatin and the disintegration of the nuclear envelope and lamina.][[0.0]]
[The destruction of the lamin networks is controlled by specialized apoptotic proteases called caspases, which cleave the lamin proteins and thus degrade the nucleus' structural integrity.][[0.0]]
[Lamin cleavage is sometimes used as a laboratory indicator of caspase activity in assays for early apoptotic activity.][[0.0]]
[Cells that express mutant caspase-resistant lamins are deficient in nuclear changes related to apoptosis, suggesting that lamins play a role in initiating the events that lead to apoptotic degradation of the nucleus.][[0.0]]
[Inhibition of lamin assembly itself is an inducer of apoptosis.][[0.0]]
[The nuclear envelope acts as a barrier that prevents both DNA and RNA viruses from entering the nucleus.][[0.0]]
[Some viruses require access to proteins inside the nucleus in order to replicate and/or assemble.][[0.0]]
[DNA viruses, such as herpesvirus replicate and assemble in the cell nucleus, and exit by budding through the inner nuclear membrane.][[0.0]]
[This process is accompanied by disassembly of the lamina on the nuclear face of the inner membrane.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Anucleated and polynucleated cells Although most cells have a single nucleus, some eukaryotic cell types have no nucleus, and others have many nuclei.][[0.0]]
[This can be a normal process, as in the maturation of mammalian red blood cells, or a result of faulty cell division.][[0.0]]
[Anucleated cells contain no nucleus and are therefore incapable of dividing to produce daughter cells.][[0.0]]
[The best-known anucleated cell is the mammalian red blood cell, or erythrocyte, which also lacks other organelles such as mitochondria and serves primarily as a transport vessel to ferry oxygen from the lungs to the body's tissues.][[0.0]]
[Erythrocytes mature through erythropoiesis in the bone marrow, where they lose their nuclei, organelles, and ribosomes.][[0.0]]
[The nucleus is expelled during the process of differentiation from an erythroblast to a reticulocyte, which is the immediate precursor of the mature erythrocyte.][[0.0]]
[The presence of mutagens may induce the release of some immature ``micronucleated'' erythrocytes into the bloodstream.][[0.0]]
[Anucleated cells can also arise from flawed cell division in which one daughter lacks a nucleus and the other has two nuclei.][[0.0]]
[Polynucleated cells contain multiple nuclei.][[0.0]]
[Most Acantharean species of protozoa and some fungi in mycorrhizae have naturally polynucleated cells.][[0.0]]
[Other examples include the intestinal parasites in the genus Giardia, which have two nuclei per cell.][[0.0]]
[In humans, skeletal muscle cells, called myocytes, become polynucleated during development; the resulting arrangement of nuclei near the periphery of the cells allows maximal intracellular space for myofibrils.][[0.0]]
[Multinucleated cells can also be abnormal in humans; for example, cells arising from the fusion of monocytes and macrophages, known as giant multinucleated cells, sometimes accompany inflammation and are also implicated in tumor formation.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Evolution As the major defining characteristic of the eukaryotic cell, the nucleus' evolutionary origin has been the subject of much speculation.][[0.0]]
[Four major theories have been proposed to explain the existence of the nucleus, although none have yet earned widespread support.][[0.0]]
[The theory known as the ``syntrophic model'' proposes that a symbiotic relationship between the archaea and bacteria created the nucleus-containing eukaryotic cell.][[0.0]]
[It is hypothesized that the symbiosis originated when ancient archaea, similar to modern methanogenic archaea, invaded and lived within bacteria similar to modern myxobacteria, eventually forming the early nucleus.][[0.0]]
[This theory is analogous to the accepted theory for the origin of eukaryotic mitochondria and chloroplasts, which are thought to have developed from a similar endosymbiotic relationship between proto-eukaryotes and aerobic bacteria.][[0.0]]
[The archaeal origin of the nucleus is supported by observations that archaea and eukarya have similar genes for certain proteins, including histones.][[0.0]]
[Observations that myxobacteria are motile, can form multicellular complexes, and possess kinases and G proteins similar to eukarya, support a bacterial origin for the eukaryotic cell.][[0.0]]
[A second model proposes that proto-eukaryotic cells evolved from bacteria without an endosymbiotic stage.][[0.0]]
[This model is based on the existence of modern planctomycetes bacteria that possess a nuclear structure with primitive pores and other compartmentalized membrane structures.][[1.0]]
[A similar proposal states that a eukaryote-like cell, the chronocyte, evolved first and phagocytosed archaea and bacteria to generate the nucleus and the eukaryotic cell.][[0.0]]
[The most controversial model, known as viral eukaryogenesis, posits that the membrane-bound nucleus, along with other eukaryotic features, originated from the infection of a prokaryote by a virus.][[0.0]]
[The suggestion is based on similarities between eukaryotes and viruses such as linear DNA strands, mRNA capping, and tight binding to proteins -LRB-analogizing histones to viral envelopes-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[One version of the proposal suggests that the nucleus evolved in concert with phagocytosis to form an early cellular ``predator''.][[0.0]]
[Another variant proposes that eukaryotes originated from early archaea infected by poxviruses, on the basis of observed similarity between the DNA polymerases in modern poxviruses and eukaryotes.][[0.0]]
[It has been suggested that the unresolved question of the evolution of sex could be related to the viral eukaryogenesis hypothesis.][[0.0]]
[Finally, a very recent proposal suggests that traditional variants of the endosymbiont theory are insufficiently powerful to explain the origin of the eukaryotic nucleus.][[0.0]]
[This model, termed the exomembrane hypothesis, suggests that the nucleus instead originated from a single ancestral cell that evolved a second exterior cell membrane; the interior membrane enclosing the original cell then became the nuclear membrane and evolved increasingly elaborate pore structures for passage of internally synthesized cellular components such as ribosomal subunits.][[1.0]]
