[-LSB-edit-RSB- Realisation of the potential In January 2006 the total installed electrical generating capacity from all forms of renewable energy was less than 2 GW, about a fifth of the total electrical production.][[1.0]]
[By January 2007 wind power capacity, which has been growing rapidly, reached 1 GW capacity, and the total for renewables had grown to over 2.3 GW.][[0.0]]
[By August 2009 wind power capacity was a fraction short of 1.5 GW and total renewables capacity had reached over 3.1 GW.][[0.0]]
[Renewables are now contributing over 19% of total electrical production, and about 4% of all energy usage.][[0.0]]
[It should be borne in mind that electricity production is only part of the overall energy use budget.][[0.0]]
[In 2002, Scotland consumed a total of 175 Terawatt-hours -LRB-TWh-RRB- of energy in all forms, some 2% less than in 1990.][[0.0]]
[Of this, only 20% was consumed in the form of electricity by end users, the great majority of energy utilised being from the burning of oil -LRB-41%-RRB- and gas -LRB-36%-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Scotland also has significant quantities of fossil fuel deposits, including 62.4% of the EU's proven reserves of oil, 12.5% of the EU's proven reserves of gas and 69% of UK coal reserves.][[0.0]]
[Nonetheless, the Scottish Government has set ambitious targets for renewable energy production.][[0.0]]
[In 2005 the aim was for 18% of Scotland's electricity production to be generated by renewable sources by 2010, rising to 40% by 2020.][[0.0]]
[In 2007 this was increased to 50 per cent of electricity from renewables by 2020, with an interim target of 31 per cent by 2011.][[0.0]]
[The following year new targets to reduce overall greenhouse gas emissions by 80% by 2050 were announced and then confirmed in the 2009 Climate Change Delivery Plan.][[0.0]]
[Maf Smith, director of the Sustainable Development Commission in Scotland said ``Governments across the world are shying away from taking the necessary action.][[0.0]]
[The Scottish Government must be commended for its intention to lead the way''.][[0.0]]
[An important reason for this ambition is growing international concern about human-induced climate change.][[0.0]]
[The Royal Commission on Environmental Pollution's proposal that carbon dioxide emissions should be reduced by 60% was incorporated into the UK government's 2003 Energy White Paper.][[0.0]]
[The 2006 Stern Review proposed a 55% reduction by 2030.][[0.0]]
[The recent Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change's Fourth Assessment Report has further increased the profile of the issue.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Wind power Wind power is a renewable technology and produces no greenhouse gases during operation, although inevitably some are produced during construction and transport.][[0.0]]
[The precise amounts involved are a matter of controversy.][[0.0]]
[Manufacturers typically state that carbon emissions are `paid back' within 3--18 months of production, but recent research claims that turbines located on peat bogs create incidental emissions that may increase this to 8 years or more.][[0.0]]
[Wind turbines are the fastest growing of the renewable energy technologies in Scotland.][[0.0]]
[Most turbines in the EU produce electricity at an average of 25% of their rated maximum power due to the intermittency of wind resources, but Scotland's wind regime provides average of 40% or higher on the west and northern coasts.][[0.0]]
[A small wind farm on Shetland with five Vestas V47 660�kW turbines recently achieved a world record of 58% capacity over the course of a year.][[0.0]]
[There are now numerous large on-shore power stations including Black Law Wind Farm rated at over 96 MW, Hadyard Hill Wind Farm, which is the first wind farm in the UK able to generate over 100 MW, and Whitelee Wind Farm, a 322 MW project that is the largest onshore wind farm in Europe.][[0.0]]
[Nevertheless the siting of turbines has become a controversial issue amongst those concerned about the value of natural landscapes.][[0.0]]
[It is estimated that 11.5 GW of onshore wind potential exists, enough to provide 45 TWh of energy.][[0.0]]
[More than double this amount exists on offshore sites where mean wind speeds are greater than on land.][[0.0]]
[The total offshore potential is estimated at 25 GW, which although more expensive to install, could be enough to provide almost half the total energy used in Scotland.][[0.0]]
[The first offshore turbines are operated by Talisman Energy, who have erected two large machines 25�kilometres -LRB-13�nmi-RRB- offshore adjacent to the Beatrice oilfield.][[0.0]]
[These turbines are 88�metres -LRB-289�ft-RRB- high with the blades 63�metres -LRB-207�ft-RRB- long and have a capacity of 5 MW each, making them amongst the largest in the world.][[0.0]]
[Plans to harness up to 4.8 GW of the potential in the inner Moray Firth and Firth of Forth were announced in January 2010.][[0.0]]
[Moray Offshore Renewables and SeaGreen Wind Energy were awarded development contracts by the Crown Estate as part of a UK-wide initiative.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Wave power Various systems are under development at present aimed at harnessing the enormous potential available for wave power off Scotland's coasts.][[0.0]]
[Pelamis Wave Power -LRB-previously Ocean Power Delivery-RRB- are an Edinburgh-based company whose Pelamis system has been tested off Orkney and Portugal.][[0.0]]
[These devices are 150�metres -LRB-492�ft-RRB- long, 3.5�metres -LRB-11.5�ft-RRB- diameter floating tubes which capture the mechanical action of the waves.][[0.0]]
[Future wave farm projects could involve an arrangement of interlinked 750�kW machines connected to shore by a subsea transmission cable.][[0.0]]
[Another approach is used by the LIMPET 500 -LRB-Land Installed Marine Power Energy Transformer-RRB- energy converter installed on the island of Islay by Wavegen Ltd..][[0.0]]
[It is a shore-based unit and generates power when waves run up the beach, creating pressure inside an inclined oscillating water column.][[0.0]]
[This in turn creates pneumatic power which drives twin 250�kW the generators.][[0.0]]
[Islay LIMPET was opened in 2001 and is the world's first commercial scale wave-energy device.][[0.0]]
[The manufacturers are now developing a larger system in the Faroe Islands.][[0.0]]
[Funding for the UK's first wave farm was announced by the Scottish Executive on 22 February 2007.][[0.0]]
[It will be the world's largest, with a capacity of 3 MW generated by four Pelamis machines at a cost of over 4 million pounds.][[0.0]]
[The funding is part of a new £13 million funding package for marine power projects in Scotland that will also support developments to Aquamarine's Oyster and Ocean Power Technology's PowerBuoy wave systems, AWS Ocean Energy's sub-sea wave devices, ScotRenewables' 1.2 MW floating rotor device, Cleantechcom's tidal surge plans for the Churchill barriers between various Orkney islands, the Open Hydro tidal ring turbines, and further developments to the Wavegen system proposed for Lewis as well as a further £2.5 million for the European Marine Energy Centre -LRB-EMEC-RRB- based in Orkney.][[0.0]]
[This is a new Scottish Executive-backed research facility that has installed a wave testing system at Billia Croo on the Orkney mainland and a tidal power testing station on the nearby island of Eday.][[0.0]]
[At the official opening of the Eday project the site was described as ``the first of its kind in the world set up to provide developers of wave and tidal energy devices with a purpose-built performance testing facility.''][[0.0]]
[The Siadar Wave Energy Project was announced in 2009.][[0.0]]
[This is a 4 MW system to be built by npower Renewables and Wavegen, 400 metres off the shore of Siadar Bay, in Lewis.][[0.0]]
[In early 2010 two areas were identified for substantial offshore wind development, in the Moray Firth basin and outer Firth of Forth.][[0.0]]
[Shortly afterwards the Government earmarked eleven sites they expected to benefit from the construction of up to 8,000 offshore turbines by 2020.][[0.0]]
[These included Campbeltown and Hunterston, four sites previously used for offshore oil fabrication at Ardersier, Nigg Bay, Arnish and Kishorn and five east coast locations from Peterhead to Leith.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Tidal power Unlike wind and wave, tidal power is an inherently predictable source.][[0.0]]
[However the technology is in its infancy and numerous devices are in the prototype stages.][[0.0]]
[Today it is known that a tall tubular tower with three blades attached to it is the typical profile of a wind turbine, but twenty-five years ago there were a wide variety of different systems being tested.][[0.0]]
[This is the current situation with regard to tidal power.][[0.0]]
[Some systems capture energy from the tides in a vertical direction.][[0.0]]
[The tide comes in and raises the water level in a basin.][[0.0]]
[As the tide lowers the water in the basin is discharged through a turbine.][[0.0]]
[Tidal stream power captures energy from the flow of tides, usually using underwater plant resembling a small wind turbine.][[0.0]]
[To date the only installed tidal power plant of any size is the 240�MW rated barrage scheme at the Rance Estuary in Brittany, which has been operating successfully for more than 25 years, although there are a number of other much smaller projects around the world.][[1.0]]
[An example is Marine Current Turbines SeaGen 1.2�MW device at Strangford Lough in Northern Ireland, which is the first commercial scale tidal turbine in the world.][[0.0]]
[The Pentland Firth between Orkney and mainland Scotland has been described as the ``Saudi Arabia of tidal power'' and may be capable of generating up to 10 GW.][[1.0]]
[In March 2010 a total of ten sites in the area, capable of providing an installed capacity of 1.2 GW of tidal and wave generation were leased out by the Crown Estates.][[1.0]]
[Several other tidal sites with considerable potential exist in the Orkney archipelago.][[0.0]]
[Tidal races on the west coast at Kyle Rhea between Skye and Lochalsh, the Grey Dog north of Scarba, the Dorus Mòr off Crinan and the Gulf of Corryvreckan also offer significant prospects.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Hydro-electric power Scotland has 85% of the UK's hydro-electric energy resource, much of it developed by the North of Scotland Hydro-Electric Board in the 1950s.][[0.0]]
[The ``Hydro Board'', which brought ``power from the glens'', was a nationalised industry at the time although it was privatised in 1989 and is now part of Scottish and Southern Energy plc..][[0.0]]
[Numerous remote straths were flooded by these schemes, many of the largest of which involved tunneling through mountains as well as damming rivers.][[0.0]]
[Emma Wood, the author of a study of these pioneers wrote: Current capacity is 1.33 GW and includes major developments such as the 120 MW Breadalbane scheme and the 245 MW Tummel system.][[0.0]]
[It is estimated that little more than another 0.3 GW remains available to develop.][[0.0]]
[There is further potential for new pump storage schemes that would work well with intermittent sources of power such as wind and wave.][[0.0]]
[Examples include the 440 MW Ben Cruachan and 300 MW Falls of Foyers schemes.][[0.0]]
[The 100 MW Glen Doe project, currently under construction and Scotland's largest civil engineering project, is the first large scale scheme in Scotland for almost fifty years but is likely to be one of the last of its kind.][[0.0]]
[There is certainly further potential for small-scale run of the river local schemes such as the existing one in Knoydart and planned for Kingussie, but the total effect of such schemes, although important locally, will be tiny on a national basis.][[1.0]]
[The production of hydro electricity has a long history in Scotland but given that the available catchment areas have practically all been exploited it is unlikely that there will be scope for the further development of significant amounts of new hydro generation.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Biofuels -LSB-edit-RSB- Biodiesel Various biodiesel schemes exist at present, and as with most renewables, interest is growing in the subject.][[0.0]]
[Westray Development Trust operate a biodiesel vehicle fueled by the residual vegetable oils from the Orkney archipelago fish and chip outlets.][[0.0]]
[On a larger scale Argent Energy's plant in Motherwell recycles tallow and used cooking oil to produce 50 million litres of biodiesel per annum.][[0.0]]
[A major benefit of biodiesel is lower carbon emissions, although the energy balance of liquid biofuels is a matter of controversy.][[0.0]]
[Research is being undertaken into converting rapeseed oil into biodiesel, and the European biofuels directive intends to ensure that 5.75% Europe's transport fuel comes from renewable sources by 2010.][[0.0]]
[However, there is only enough used vegetable oil in the UK to contribute 0.38% of current road fuel demand and if all the arable land in the UK were turned over to biofuel crops this would still only satisfy 22% of the existing requirement for road transport.][[0.0]]
[Serious concerns regarding the ethics of growing biodiesel in developing countries and importing the fuel to Europe have been raised on the grounds that they may replace much needed food crops.][[0.0]]
[Converting any mainstream transport system to a renewable one also involves the conundrum that for consumers to use it the infrastructure must be in place, but high levels of use may be required to finance the infrastructure.][[0.0]]
[Developments are thus slow at present and renewably powered vehicles very much the exception.][[0.0]]
[Due to the relatively short growing season for sugar producing crops, ethanol is not commercially produced as a fuel in Scotland at present.][[0.0]]
[However there are encouraging developments in cellulosic decomposition that might enable grass or tree crops to be used to this end in future and which may prove to have lower net carbon emissions than other production techniques.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Biogas, anaerobic digestion and landfill gas Biogas, or landfill gas, is a biofuel produced through the intermediary stage of anaerobic digestion consisting mainly of 45--90% biologically produced methane and carbon dioxide.][[0.0]]
[In early 2007 a thermophilic anaerobic digestion facility was commissioned in Stornoway in the Western Isles.][[0.0]]
[The Scottish Environment Protection Agency -LRB-SEPA-RRB- and the Renewable Energy Association are also leading the way towards the establishment of a digestate standard to facilitate the use of solid outputs from digesters on land.][[0.0]]
[Anaerobic digestion and mechanical biological treatment facilities have been planned at a number of other locations in Scotland, such as Westray.][[0.0]]
[It has been recognised that biogas -LRB-mainly methane-RRB- -- produced from the anaerobic digestion of organic matter -- is potentially a valuable and prolific feedstock.][[0.0]]
[It is estimated that 0.4 GW of generating capacity might be available from agricultural waste in Scotland.][[0.0]]
[The Scottish Executive and SEPA has funded seven small scale farm trial plants with the British anaerobic digestion company Greenfinch in Southwest Scotland.][[0.0]]
[Landfill sites have the potential for a further 0.07 GW with sites such as the Avondale Landfill in Falkirk already utilising their potential.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Solid biomass Wood fuel almost certainly exceeds hydroelectric and wind as the largest source of renewable energy at present.][[0.0]]
[Scotland's forests, which currently make up 60% of the UK resource base, could provide up to 1 million tonnes of wood fuel per annum.][[0.0]]
[The biomass energy supply in Scotland could reach 450 MW or higher in coming years, -LRB-predominantly from wood-RRB-, with power stations requiring 4,500--5,000 oven dry tonnes per annum per megawatt of generating capacity.][[0.0]]
[The energy company E.ON has constructed a 44 MW biomass power station at Lockerbie using locally sourced crops while the smaller but not insignificant EPR Westfield power plant in Fife produces 9.8 MW of output using chicken litter as fuel.][[0.0]]
[The Forestry Commission are developing a Scottish Biomass Action Plan in conjunction with the Scottish Executive, and the latter is expected to provide a £7.5 million grant scheme to support biomass energy.][[0.0]]
[There is growing demand for automatic wood pellet boilers which can be as convenient to use as conventional central heating systems, and which may be cheaper to run as well as being carbon neutral.][[0.0]]
[There is also local potential for energy crops such as short-rotation willow or poplar coppice, miscanthus energy grass, agricultural wastes such as straw and manure, and forestry residues.][[1.0]]
[These crops could provide 0.8 GW of generating capacity.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Micro systems The Energy Savings Trust has estimated that micro-generation could provide a significantly increased proportion of the UK's electricity demand by 2050 although only a fraction of this would come from renewable sources.][[0.0]]
[The current Scottish output is negligible.][[0.0]]
[In May 2006 the then Communities Minister Malcolm Chisholm launched a Planning Advice Note aimed at promoting micro-renewables.][[0.0]]
[Small-scale `wind2heat' projects, which use wind turbines to power electrical storage heaters directly, have proven to be successful in remoter rural areas; as have various other local schemes such as air source heat pumps.][[0.0]]
[Whisky distilleries may have a locally important part to play.][[0.0]]
[Caithness Heat and Power have announced plans to tackle fuel poverty in Wick by utilising a wood chip CHP scheme in partnership with the Old Pulteney Distillery.][[0.0]]
[On the island of Islay, a swimming pool is heated using waste heat from the Bowmore distillery.][[0.0]]
[In Edinburgh, Tynecastle High School, due to be completed in 2010, will be heated by waste heat from the neighbouring North British Distillery.][[0.0]]
[In 2009 the Diageo Cameron Bridge distillery announced plans for a £65�million facility to generate energy from the spent ``wash'' created in the manufacturing process, which will aim to replace 95% of the plan's existing fossil fuel use.][[0.0]]
[There are also a growing number of Micro hydro systems on smaller watercourses, especially in more remote rural locations.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Solar energy Despite Scotland's relatively low level of sunshine hours, solar thermal panels can work effectively as they are capable of producing hot water even in cloudy weather.][[0.0]]
[The technology was developed in the 1970s and is well-established with various installers in place such as Solar Power Scotland of Montrose, although AES Solar based in Forres -LRB-who provided the panels for the Scottish Parliament building-RRB- are Scotland's only manufacturer.][[0.0]]
[There are few examples of photovoltaic panels, which generate electricity, in Scotland as the price is not currently competitive.][[0.0]]
[The introduction of Feed-In Tariffs in April 2010 will shorten the payback period on the cost of a PV installation, making solar PV a competitive form of renewable generation.][[0.0]]
[The largest installation in Scotland is a 21 kWp system at the Sir E. Scott secondary school in Tarbert, Harris.][[0.0]]
[The UK's practicable resource is estimated at 7.2 TWh per annum, which in the Scottish context is the approximate equivalent of 70 MW or less of installed capacity.][[1.0]]
[The ``road energy system'' uses water pipes buried beneath a layer of tarmac.][[0.0]]
[In the summer, the dark asphalt is heated by the sun which in turn heats the water in the pipes.][[0.0]]
[This water can be stored in an underground aquifer and the heat extracted in winter using a heat pump.][[0.0]]
[The system can be used to warm or cool down roads, keeping them ice-free and/or preventing softening due to overheating.][[0.0]]
[Alternatively, the stored energy can be used for cooling buildings.][[0.0]]
[The system was developed in the Netherlands and has been licensed by Ullapool-based Invisible Energy Systems, who have installed the technology in their car park.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Geothermal energy Geothermal energy is obtained by tapping the heat of the earth itself.][[0.0]]
[Most systems in Scotland provide heating through a ground source heat pump which brings energy to the surface via shallow pipe works.][[0.0]]
[An example is the Glenalmond Street project in Shettleston, which uses a combination of solar and geothermal energy to heat 16 houses.][[0.0]]
[Water in a coal mine 100�metres -LRB-328�ft-RRB- below ground level is heated by geothermal energy and maintained at a temperature of about 12 °C -LRB-54 °F-RRB- throughout the year.][[0.0]]
[The warmed water is raised and passed through a heat pump, boosting the temperature to 55�°C -LRB-131�°F-RRB-, and is then distributed to the houses providing heating to radiators.][[0.0]]
[Although the pumps may not be powered from renewable sources, up to four times the energy used can be recovered.][[0.0]]
[Installation costs can vary from £7,000 to £10,000, and grants may be available from the Scottish Community and Householders Renewables Initiative operated by Community Energy Scotland for domestic properties up to a maximum of £4,000.][[0.0]]
[Perhaps up to 7.6 TWh of energy is available on an annual basis from this source.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Other means of reducing carbon emissions It is clear that if carbon emissions are to be reduced, a combination of increased production from renewables and decreased consumption of energy in general and fossil fuels in particular will be required.][[0.0]]
[On the latter front, Gordon Brown, the then UK Chancellor of the Exchequer, announced in November 2006 that within a decade all new houses would have to be `zero carbon'.][[0.0]]
[A variety of other options exist, most of which may affect development of renewable technologies even if they are not means of producing energy from renewable sources themselves.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Other renewable options Various other ideas for renewable energy in the early stages of development, such as ocean thermal energy conversion, deep lake water cooling, and blue energy, have received little attention in Scotland, presumably because the potential is so significant for less speculative technologies.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Carbon offsetting Carbon offsetting involves individuals or organisations compensating for their use of fossil fuels by making payments to projects that aim to neutralise the effect of these carbon emissions.][[0.0]]
[Although the idea has become fashionable, the theory has received serious criticism of late.][[0.0]]
[Nonetheless, a credible option may be to plant trees within the local bioregion and maintain the forest on a permanent basis, thus locking up carbon produced by burning fossil fuels.][[0.0]]
[In British growing conditions this method can compensate for carbon at a rate of 200 tonnes per square kilometre -LRB-0.89�tons/acre-RRB- planted over a 100 year period.][[0.0]]
[Thus a 4-square-kilometre -LRB-988-acre-RRB- plantation could uptake 200 tonnes -LRB-220�tons-RRB- of carbon over twenty-five years.][[0.0]]
[This is the equivalent of 10,000 tonnes -LRB-11,000�short tons-RRB- of carbon dioxide.][[0.0]]
[The weaknesses of the approach include uncertainty as to whether the planting might have occurred anyway and who, in the future, will ensure permanence.][[0.0]]
[However, there is likely to be a greater level of credibility inherent in a nearby and visible scheme than in a far-distant one.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Challenges and opportunities offered by non-renewables The following technologies are means of reducing the effect of carbon emissions and form an important aspect of the energy debate in Scotland and are included here for completeness.][[0.0]]
[Their effect is likely to influence the future direction of commercial renewable energy, but they are not renewable forms of energy production themselves.][[0.0]]
[Carbon sequestration: Also known as carbon capture and storage, this technology involves the storage of carbon dioxide -LRB-CO2-RRB- that is a by-product of industrial processes through its injection into oil fields.][[0.0]]
[It is not a form of renewable energy production, but it may be a way to significantly reduce the effect of fossil fuels whilst renewables are commercialised.][[0.0]]
[It may also be an intermediate step towards a `hydrogen economy' -LRB-see below-RRB-, which could either enable further renewable development or conceivably out-compete it.][[0.0]]
[The technology has been successfully pioneered in Norway but is still a relatively untried concept.][[0.0]]
[`Clean coal' technology: It is has been estimated that it will be 2020 to 2025 before any commercial-scale clean coal power stations -LRB-coal-burning power stations with carbon capture and sequestration-RRB- are widely adopted.][[0.0]]
[Moreover, some have criticised the clean coal approach and it is at best a means of ameliorating carbon emissions.][[0.0]]
[It is not a form of renewable energy production, although like carbon sequestration it offers a significant commercial challenge to renewable developments.][[0.0]]
[In 2009 a licence to test underground coal gassification technology in Fife was granted to Thornton New Energy.][[0.0]]
[However, a plan to build a new ``clean coal'' power station at Hunterston collapsed in 2009 after financial backing was withdrawn.][[0.0]]
[Nuclear power: Renewable energy as a concept generally excludes nuclear power although this stance has been challenged.][[0.0]]
[Incineration: There is a successful waste-to-energy incineration plant at Lerwick in Shetland which burns 22,000 tonnes -LRB-24,250�tons-RRB- of waste every year and provides district heating to over 600 customers.][[0.0]]
[Although such plants generate carbon emissions through the combustion of the biological material and plastic wastes -LRB-which derive from fossil fuels-RRB-, they also reduce the damage done to the atmosphere from the creation of methane in landfill sites.][[0.0]]
[This is a much more damaging greenhouse gas than the carbon dioxide the burning process produces, although other systems which do not involve district heating may have a similar carbon footprint to straightforward landfill degradation.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Hydrogen Although hydrogen offers significant potential as an alternative to hydrocarbons as a carrier of energy, neither hydrogen itself nor the associated fuel cell technologies are sources of energy in themselves.][[0.0]]
[Nevertheless, the combination of renewable technologies and hydrogen is of considerable interest to those seeking alternatives to fossil fuels.][[0.0]]
[There are a number of Scottish projects involved in this research, supported by the Scottish Hydrogen & Fuel Cell Association -LRB-SHFCA-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The PURE project on Unst in Shetland is a ground-breaking training and research centre which uses a combination of the ample supplies of wind power and fuel cells to create a wind hydrogen system.][[0.0]]
[Two 15�kW turbines are attached to a `Hypod' fuel cell, which in turn provides power for heating systems, the creation of stored liquid hydrogen and an innovative fuel-cell driven car.][[0.0]]
[The project is community-owned and part of the Unst Partnership, the community's development trust.][[0.0]]
[In the Western Isles a plan to enable a £10 million waste management plant into a hydrogen production facility was announced in June 2006.][[0.0]]
[The Council have also agreed to purchase hydrogen-fuelled buses and hope the new plant, which will be constructed in partnership with the local Hydrogen Research Laboratory, will supply island filling stations and houses and the industrial park at Arnish.][[0.0]]
[ITI Energy is a company with the aim of funding Research and Development programmes in the energy sector.][[0.0]]
[It is a division of ITI Scotland, which also includes a life sciences and digital media division.][[0.0]]
[ITI Energy has attracted the Alterg project, a French company that is developing technology for the cost-effective storage of hydrogen.][[0.0]]
[In July 2008 the SHFCA announced plans for a ``hydrogen corridor'' from Aberdeen to Peterhead.][[0.0]]
[The proposal involves running hydrogen powered buses along the A 90 and is supported by Aberdeenshire Council and the Royal Mail.][[0.0]]
[The economics and practical application of hydrogen vehicles are currently being investigated by the University of Birmingham in England.][[0.0]]
[The ``Hydrogen Office'' in Methil aims to demonstrate the benefits of improved energy efficiency and renewable and hydrogen energy systems.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Local vs national concerns A significant feature of Scotland's renewable potential is that the resources are largely distant from the main centres of population.][[1.0]]
[This is by no means coincidental.][[0.0]]
[The power of wind, wave and tide on the north and west coasts and for hydro in the mountains makes for dramatic scenery, but sometimes harsh living conditions.][[0.0]]
[W. H. Murray described the Hebrides as ``the Isles on the Edge of the Sea where men are welcome--if they are hard in body and in spirit tenacious.''][[0.0]]
[This happenstance of geography and climate has created various tensions.][[0.0]]
[There is clearly a significant difference between a renewable energy production facility of modest size providing an island community with all its energy needs, and an industrial scale power station in the same location that is designed to export power to far distant urban locations.][[0.0]]
[Thus, plans for one of the world's largest onshore windfarms on the Hebridean island of Lewis, have generated considerable debate.][[0.0]]
[A related issue is the planned high-voltage Beauly--Denny power line which will bring electricity from renewable projects in the north and west to the cities of the south.][[0.0]]
[The matter went to a public inquiry and has been described by Ian Johnston of The Scotsman as a ``battle that pitches environmentalists against conservationists and giant energy companies against aristocratic landowners and clan chiefs''.][[0.0]]
[In January 2010 Jim Mather, the Energy Minister, announced that the project would be going ahead, notwithstanding the more than 18,000 objections received.][[0.0]]
[There is considerable support for community-scale energy projects.][[0.0]]
[For example, Alex Salmond, First Minister of Scotland, has stated that ``we can think big by delivering small'' and aspires to have a ``million Scottish households with access to their own or community renewable generation within ten years''.][[0.0]]
[The John Muir Trust has also stated that ``the best renewable energy options around wild land are small-scale, sensitively sited and adjacent to the communities directly benefiting from them'', although even community-owned schemes can prove controversial.][[0.0]]
[A related issue is the position of Scotland within the United Kingdom.][[0.0]]
[It has been alleged that UK transmission pricing structures are weighted against the development of renewables in Scotland, a debate which highlights the contrast between the sparsely populated north of Scotland and the highly urbanised south and east of England.][[0.0]]
[Although the ecological footprints of Scotland and England are similar the relationship between this footprint and the biocapacities of the respective countries are not.][[0.0]]
[Scotland's biocapacity -LRB-a measure of the biologically productive area-RRB- is 4.52 global hectares -LRB-gha-RRB- per head, some 15% less than the current ecological effect.][[0.0]]
[In other words, with a 15% reduction in consumption, the Scottish population could live within the productive capacity of the land to support them.][[0.0]]
[However, the UK ecological footprint is more than three times the biocapacity, which is only 1.6 gha head, amongst the lowest in Europe.][[0.0]]
[Thus, to achieve the same end in the UK context, consumption would have to be reduced by about 66%.][[0.0]]
[The developed world's economy is presently very dependent on inexpensive `point-source' fossil fuels.][[0.0]]
[Scotland, as a relatively sparsely populated country with significant renewable resources, is in a unique position to demonstrate how the transition to a low-carbon, widely distributed energy economy may be undertaken.][[0.0]]
[A balance will need to be struck between supporting this transition and providing exports to the economies of densely populated regions in the Central Belt and elsewhere, as they seek their own solutions.][[0.0]]
[The tension between local and national needs in the Scottish context may therefore also play out on the wider UK and European stage.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Promotion of renewables Growing national concerns regarding ``peak oil'' and climate change have driven the subject of renewable energy high up the political agenda.][[1.0]]
[Various public bodies and public-private partnerships have been created to develop the potential.][[0.0]]
[The Forum for Renewable Energy Development in Scotland, -LRB-FREDS-RRB- is a partnership between industry, academia and Government aimed at enabling Scotland to capitalise on its renewable energy resource.][[1.0]]
[The Scottish Renewables Forum is an important intermediary organisation for the industry, hosting the annual Green Energy Awards.][[0.0]]
[Community Energy Scotland provides advice, grant funding and finance for renewable energy projects developed by community groups.][[0.0]]
[Aberdeen Renewable Energy Group -LRB-AREG-RRB- is a public-private partnership created to identify and promote renewable energy opportunities for businesses in the north-east.][[0.0]]
[In 2009 AREG formed an alliance with North Scotland Industries Group to help promote the North of Scotland as an ``international renewable energy hub''.][[0.0]]
[The Forestry Commission is active in promoting the biomass potential.][[0.0]]
[The Climate Change Business Delivery Group aims to act as a way for businesses to share best practice and address the climate change challenge.][[0.0]]
[Numerous universities are playing a role in supporting energy research under the Supergen programme, including fuel cell research at St Andrews, marine technologies at Edinburgh, distributed power systems at Strathclyde and biomass crops at the UHI Millennium Institute's Orkney College.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Recent events New data appears on a regular basis and milestones in 2007-9 include the following.][[0.0]]
[In February 2007 the commissioning of the Braes of Doune wind farm took the UK renewables installed capacity up to 2 GW.][[0.0]]
[Total Scottish capacity at October 2007 was 1.13 GW from 760 turbines and increased to 1.3 GW by September 2008 and 1.48 GW by August 2009.][[0.0]]
[The Robin Rigg 180 MW wind farm in the Solway Firth, Scotland first large offshore wind farm, is expected to commence production in the summer of 2009.][[0.0]]
[Also during 2007 Scottish and Southern Energy plc in conjunction with the University of Strathclyde began the implementation of a `Regional Power Zone' in the Orkney archipelago.][[0.0]]
[This ground-breaking scheme -LRB-that may be the first of its kind in the world-RRB- involves `active network management' that will make better use of the existing infrastructure and allow a further 15MW of new `non-firm generation' output from renewables onto the network.][[0.0]]
[Heat and Power Ltd. of Westray are involved in developing an innovative digestor system that is being trialled at Tuquoy farm.][[0.0]]
[Designed by Sam Harcus and Colin Risbridger, it is capable of handling up to 1,500 tonnes of feedstock per annum.][[0.0]]
[Scottish & Southern Energy have been asked to provide for an export capacity of 40kWe.][[0.0]]
[The aim is to help move the farm towards being powered by 100% renewable energy.][[0.0]]
[In January 2008 it was reported that Professor Graeme Walker of the University of Abertay is leading a project aimed at using grain that is a by-product of whisky distilling as a biofuel.][[0.0]]
[In February 2008 plans to build a 10MW prototype tidal energy plant in the Pentland Firth were announced by Tocardo Tidal Energy Ltd. of Wick.][[0.0]]
[Production is expected to commence in 2009.][[0.0]]
[The following September, Scottish Power announced plans for two tidal projects in the same area, pending successful tests of a £6 million prototype.][[0.0]]
[In January 2009 the government announced the launch of a ``Marine Spatial Plan'' to map the potential of the Pentland Firth and Orkney coasts and agreed to take part in a working group examining options for an offshore grid to connect renewable energy projects in the North Sea to on-shore national grids.][[0.0]]
[In July 2009 a new study called ``The Power of Scotland Renewed'' was published.][[0.0]]
[Its findings indicated that the country could meet all its electricity needs by 2030 without the requirement for either nuclear or fossil fuel powered installations.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Summary of Scotland's resource potential Table notes a. ^ Note on `installed capacity' and `potential energy'.][[0.0]]
[The former is an estimate of the maximum productive output of a given technology or individual generation station at a single point in time.][[0.0]]
[The latter takes into account the likely intermittency of energy supply and is a measure of output over a period of time.][[0.0]]
[Thus, for example, individual wind turbines may have a `capacity factor' of between 15% and 45% depending on their location, with a higher capacity factor giving a greater potential energy output for a given installed capacity.][[0.0]]
[The `potential energy' column is thus an estimate based on a variety of assumptions including the installed capacity.][[0.0]]
[Although `potential energy' is in some ways a more useful method of comparing the current output and future potential of different technologies, using it would require cumbersome explanations of all the assumptions involved in each example, so installed capacity figures are generally used.][[0.0]]
[b. Table notes and sources:][[0.0]]

[-LSB-edit-RSB- Background The First Indochina War had raged, as guerrilla warfare, since 19 December 1946.][[0.0]]
[From 1949, it evolved into conventional warfare, due largely to aid from the People's Republic of China -LRB-``PRC''-RRB- to the north.][[0.0]]
[Subsequently, the French strategy of occupying small, poorly defended outposts throughout Indochina, particularly along the Vietnamese-Chinese border, was failing.][[0.0]]
[Thanks to the terrain and the close border with China, the Viet Minh had succeeded in turning a ``clandestine guerrilla movement into a powerful conventional army'', something which previously had never been encountered by the western world.][[0.0]]
[In October 1952, fighting around the Red River Delta spread into the Thai Highlands, resulting in the battle of Na San, at which the Viet Minh were defeated.][[0.0]]
[The French used the lessons learned at Na San -- strong ground bases, versatile air support, and a model based on the British Burma campaign -- as the basis for their new strategy.][[0.0]]
[The Viet Minh, however, remained unbeatable in the highland regions of Vietnam, and the French ``could not offset the fundamental disadvantages of a roadbound army facing a hill and forest army in a country which had few roads but a great many hills and forests''.][[0.0]]
[In May 1953, General Henri Navarre arrived to take command of the French forces, replacing General Raoul Salan.][[0.0]]
[Navarre spoke of a new offensive spirit in Indochina -- based on strong, fast-moving forces -- and the media quickly took Operation Camargue to be the ``practical realization'' of that.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Chinese and American backing Following the Communist victory in the Chinese Civil War in 1949, the Viet Minh established close ties with China.][[0.0]]
[It enabled the Chinese to expand their area of influence into Indochina and the Viet Minh to receive much needed Chinese matériel and strategical planning support.][[0.0]]
[From mid-1950, PRC military advisers were seconded to the Viet Minh at battalion, regiment and divisional level.][[0.0]]
[The common border meant that ``China became a `sanctuary' where the Viet Minh could be trained and refitted''.][[0.0]]
[When the Korean War broke out, Indochina became ``an important pawn in cold war strategy''.][[0.0]]
[In December 1950, the United States, concerned about growing Chinese Communist influence, started providing military aid to the French, with a first payment of US$15 million.][[0.0]]
[In the spring of 1953, the Viet Minh launched campaigns in Laos and succeeded in linking up Laotian territorial gains with their bases in north-western Vietnam.][[0.0]]
[Meanwhile, the winding down of the Korean War meant that China was able ``to give much more attention to its southern neighbour''.][[0.0]]
[Similarly, the US ``released from its heavy burden in the Korean conflict ... dramatically increased its military and financial support'' to the French.][[0.0]]
[By June 1953, the US ``had sent: 1,224 tanks and combat vehicles; 120,792 rifles and machine guns; more than 200 million rifle and machine gun cartridges; more than five million artillery projectiles; 302 boats and 304 aircraft'' -LRB-by end of the war, total US aid would amount to nearly four billion dollars-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Prelude to the battle Route One, also known as Route Coloniale One -LRB-or RC1-RRB-, had been the main north--south artery along the coastline of North Vietnam since the outbreak of violence in 1949.][[0.0]]
[Communications and convoys along these lines suffered from regular attacks by Viet Minh irregulars, despite efforts by the French during 1952 in Operation Sauterelle.][[0.0]]
[The Viet Minh paramilitary forces around Road One originated mainly from a region of fortified villages dispersed along sand dunes and salt marshes between Hué to the south, and Quang-Tri to the north.][[0.0]]
[French forces had suffered from Viet Minh ambushes, an attack that the latter had become very proficient at throughout the war, most notably in the annihilation of Group Mobiles 42 and 100.][[0.0]]
[The roads in Vietnam were almost all closed during the night and ``abandoned to the enemy''.][[0.0]]
[Between 1952 and 1954, 398 armored vehicles were destroyed, 84% of them from mines and booby traps.][[0.0]]
[Typically, the Viet Minh ambushed convoys by obstructing the road with a fallen tree or pile of boulders, and then destroying the first and last vehicles of the halted convey with remote mines.][[0.0]]
[Caltraps, mines and the steep cliff faces naturally found at the road side aided in funneling the target convoy into a small area, where machine guns, mortars and recoilless rifles were trained.][[0.0]]
[Viet Minh Regiment 95 repeatedly deployed these tactics, inflicting severe losses on the French forces passing along Road One, which led to its French nickname of la rue sans joie -LRB-``the street without joy''-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Regiment 95 was, along with Regiments 18 and 101, part of the Viet Minh Division 325, commanded by General Tran Quy Ha.][[0.0]]
[The division was formed in 1951 from pre-existing units in Thua Thien just north of Road One, and became operational in the summer of 1952.][[0.0]]
[By early summer 1953, thanks in part to the wind-down of hostilities in the Korean War, the French command had ``sufficient reserves'' at hand to begin clearing the Viet Minh back from Road One.][[1.0]]
[They assembled 30 battalions, two armored regiments and two artillery regiments for one of the largest operations of the conflict.][[0.0]]
[Called Operation Camargue, it was named for the sandy marshland to the west of Marseilles, France.][[0.0]]
[The difficult terrain was to prove the decisive factor and gave a major advantage to the one Viet Minh regiment tasked with defending the Street Without Joy.][[1.0]]
[From a 100-meter -LRB-109�yd-RRB- deep beach of ``hard sand'' the French landing forces were to advance through a series of dunes.][[0.0]]
[The dunes were up to 20�meters -LRB-22�yd-RRB- high and interspersed with precipices, ditches and a handful of small villages.][[0.0]]
[Beyond this was an 800-meter -LRB-875�yd-RRB- belt of pagodas and temples, which war correspondent Bernard Fall describes as having excellent defensive potential.][[0.0]]
[Beyond these temples was Road One itself with a series of closely packed and fortified villages, including Tan An, My Thuy, Van Trinh and Lai-Ha.][[0.0]]
[This network of villages and hedgerows made both ground and air surveillance difficult.][[0.0]]
[Across from Road One the villages continued amid an area of quicksand, swamps and bogs, which would stop all but a few of the vehicles at the disposal of the French.][[0.0]]
[Although there were roads, most were mined or damaged.][[0.0]]
[Throughout the area, the civilian population remained and provided a further complication for the French high command.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- French order of battle The French divided their forces into four Groupement mobiles -LRB-``mobile groups''-RRB-: A through D. Group A consisted of Mobile Group 14, which contained 3rd Amphibious Group, 2nd Marine Commando, 2nd Battalion 1st Colonial Parachute Regiment, and 3rd Vietnamese Parachute Battalion.][[0.0]]
[Operation Camargue was to be one of the final proving grounds for the use of French armor during the war.][[0.0]]
[It was to land on the beach in line with the center of Road One.][[0.0]]
[Meanwhile, Group B was to advance over land from the west of the north-east facing beach.][[0.0]]
[This group consisted of Mobile Group Central Vietnam's 6th Moroccan Spahis, 2nd Amphibious Group, a tank platoon from 1st Foreign Cavalry Regiment, and two infantry companies from the Quang-Tri military base.][[0.0]]
[Group C was to advance from the south-west into the back of Van Trinh through the swamps, and consisted of the 9th Moroccan Tabor, 27th Vietnamese Infantry Battalion, 2nd Battalion of the 4th Moroccan Rifle Regiment, 1 Commando, a tank platoon of the Moroccan Colonials, an Armored Patrol Boat Platoon, and an LCM Platoon.][[0.0]]
[Group D consisted of 3rd Battalion of the 3rd Algerian Rifles, the 7th Amphibious Group, and a Commando group, and was to land at the south-east end of the beach, below Group A.][[0.0]]
[These forces in total formed ``two amphibious forces, three land-borne groupments and one airborne force'' all of which was commanded by General Leblanc.][[0.0]]
[This French force, vastly outnumbering the Viet Minh regiment opposing it, was tasked with sealing the Communist forces into a tight pocket, and systematically destroying them, as well as capturing as many prisoners, arms caches and as much equipment as possible.][[1.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Securing the Street Without Joy -LSB-edit-RSB- French landing On 27 July 1953, the French landing craft departed from their assembly points, and by 04:00 on the following had begun disembarking 160 amphibious landing craft belonging to Group A's 3rd Amphibious opposite the coastline.][[0.0]]
[By 06:00, these vehicles had landed on the beach and proceeded to occupy sand ridges overlooking the dunes beyond.][[0.0]]
[Proceeding into the dunes, the vehicles of 3rd Amphibious became stuck in the sand; in the meantime, other regular infantry elements of Group A were experiencing more difficulties in the sea, taking two extra hours to reach the beach.][[0.0]]
[Thus unsupported, elements of 3rd Amphibious that either disembarked floundering vehicles or were pushed, managed to escape the dunes and advance between Tan An and My Thuy.][[0.0]]
[The French amphibious vehicles were the World War II-era 29-C cargo carriers, nicknamed the Crab or Crabe and LVT 4 or 4As, known as the Alligator.][[0.0]]
[The latter was armed with two .30 caliber and two .50 caliber Browning machine guns and an M20 recoilless rifle.][[0.0]]
[While the Alligators were heavily armored and well suited to the water, they struggled on land.][[0.0]]
[In contrast, the Crab had difficulty in water and its large size presented too great a target on land; however, it was lighter and more maneuverable, except in paddy fields where its suspension became clogged with vegetation.][[0.0]]
[While Group A's forward elements were breaching the dune barrier unopposed, two of Group B's battalions crossed the Van Trinh Canal.][[0.0]]
[By 07:45, when they made visual contact with the Crabs and Alligators of Group A, they had succeeded in sealing off the northern escape route of Regiment 95.][[0.0]]
[By 08:30, the 6th Moroccan Spahis also reached the canal, having had difficulty crossing the swamps on the landward side with their M24 Chaffee tanks.][[0.0]]
[No French units, as yet, had made any major contact with the Viet Minh.][[0.0]]
[A minor fire-fight had taken place on the southern edge of Group B's advance when an Algerian company exchanged fire with 20--30 Viet Minh and suffered the first French fatalities.][[0.0]]
[Simultaneously, Group C had advanced into the center of the area of operation, and executed ``the most complicated maneuver of the operation''.][[0.0]]
[This involved crossing Road One and sealing off the land side of the operational area, and was completed by 08:30.][[0.0]]
[Group D, finally, was tasked with advancing south from its landing point to close off an escape route that ran between the sea and an inland lagoon towards the city of Hué.][[0.0]]
[Landing at 04:30, the group made quick progress through the beach and dunes, secured the small city of Thé Chi Dong and hit the north coast of the lagoon by 05:30, thereby sealing off that escape route with no enemy contact.][[0.0]]
[The final act of sealing the noose was to move some of the French Navy vessels north to the Vietnamese villages of Ba-Lang and An-Hoi where any attempt by Regiment 95 to flee by sea would have taken place.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Tightening the loop With the landings and the encirclement of Regiment 95 complete and the net deemed secure, the French forces began the second phase of the operation and began to sweep through the area for the encircled Viet Minh.][[1.0]]
[Each French group began to move through the villages around Road One in an attempt to locate the Viet Minh forces.][[0.0]]
[Group B, which was lined up along the canal -- the jump-off point for the second phase of the operation -- moved to sweep the northern villages while Group C did the same further south.][[0.0]]
[The method of searching each village was to seal it off entirely with encircling troops, and then inspect it with a heavily armed unit of minesweepers and K-9 teams.][[0.0]]
[Men of military age were arrested and screened by intelligence officers.][[0.0]]
[This process was time-consuming, and by 11:00 Group B had traveled 7�kilometers -LRB-4.3�mi-RRB- through the network of villages with no results or resistance.][[0.0]]
[At this time, the 6th Moroccan Spahis entered the village of Dong-Qué with their M-24 tanks and the support of the 1st Battalion of the Moroccan Rifles and the artillery of Colonel Piroth -LRB-later commander of the artillery at the battle of Dien Bien Phu-RRB- and his 69th African Artillery Regiment.][[0.0]]
[The Moroccan infantry took the lead, and the French commanders sealed themselves in their tank turrets and advanced behind.][[0.0]]
[Viet Minh forces, which were waiting in ambush, fired almost the same instant as the lead Moroccan units who noticed their presence.][[0.0]]
[The Moroccan forces spread out into the surrounding rice paddies, and the bazookas of the Viet Minh missed the French tanks.][[0.0]]
[The French commander called in Piroth's artillery and Dong-Qué ``disintegrated under the impact of their high-angle fire'', particularly when a French shell found the Viet Minh ammunition depot.][[0.0]]
[As the French tanks approached, the Viet Minh drove the civilians out to clog up the entrance to the village, however as the Viet Minh retreated they were spotted through the civilians by the Moroccan infantry and killed by 13:00.][[0.0]]
[During this battle, however, most of the Regiment 95 personnel who had been elsewhere managed to escape towards the southern end of the French encirclement.][[0.0]]
[Leblanc had realized the intentions of Regiment 95's commander, and had requested one of the two reserve paratroop units to be deployed at the border between the network of temples and the dune-filled area in front of where Group D had originally landed.][[0.0]]
[This paratroop unit, 2nd Battalion of the 1st Colonial Parachute Regiment, began to advance towards the canal at 10:45, 15 minutes before Group B entered Dong-Qué.][[0.0]]
[Group C's 9th Tabor had also, like the M-24's of Group B, struggled through the marshes during the first phase of the operation, and were late in arriving at the jumping-off point for phase two, the canal.][[0.0]]
[At 08:45, Moroccan units of Group C were investigating the village of Phu-An on the opposite side of the lagoon from Group D's landing area, when they came under heavy fire.][[0.0]]
[Despite being nearer to Group D, the engaged units radioed their immediate commanders back in Group C, who were by now some distance away, further inland.][[0.0]]
[This delay, coupled with the failure of many of the units SCR300 radios, meant that these advance elements of Group C failed to get through until 09:10.][[0.0]]
[At 09:40, the commander of Group C called up various reinforcements from Hué including two companies of Vietnamese trainee NCOs and five infantry companies, two of which came via landing craft and did not reach the beleaguered elements of Group C until 18:00, half an hour after the Moroccans had finally counter-attacked and occupied Phu-An.][[0.0]]
[The 2nd Battalion of the Parachute Chasseurs Regiment had been requested to drop at 14:00 to support the advanced elements of Group C but did not jump until 16:50 and thus failed to assemble before the Moroccans themselves occupied Phu-An.][[0.0]]
[With the final capture of Phu-An, the extreme southern tip of the encirclement, the pincer movement was complete.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Escape of Regiment 95 By 17:30, with Phu-An captured, all French reserves now committed, and one half of the pocket fully swept by Groups B and A at the northern end of the battlefield, the French appeared to have gained the upper hand.][[0.0]]
[By now, the expected windfall of arms caches and prisoners should have taken place.][[0.0]]
[However, the unexpected time taken to capture Phu-An, and the delayed arrival of the paratroop reinforcements who had been scattered by the winds, had left a gap between Phu-An and the southern edge of the lagoon.][[0.0]]
[This 12-kilometer -LRB-7.5�mi-RRB- gap was eventually covered by only four French battalions, leaving gaps through which the Viet Minh could escape.][[0.0]]
[Crabs and Alligators were stationed on, or in some cases in, the canal network, and French infantry were scattered across the edge of the pocket throughout that night in order to detect escaping Viet Minh.][[0.0]]
[However, despite the occasional shot, flare and searchlight, no Viet Minh were detected.][[0.0]]
[On the morning of 29 July 1953, the French forces continued to advance into the remaining 23-square-kilometer -LRB-9�sq�mi-RRB- pocket, encountering neither Viet Minh nor civilian.][[0.0]]
[Groups A, B and D reached the edge of the canal opposite Group C by 13:00, having retrieved a small number of suspected Viet Minh and a ``few weapons''.][[0.0]]
[At this time, however, a Morane aircraft detected the movement of elements of Regiment 95 towards An-Hoi on the extreme northern corner of the operational area, outside of the pocket.][[0.0]]
[The French carried out a raid on An-Hoi by commando groups and elements of Group A, which took place at 15:00 and returned with suspected Viet Minh by 18:00.][[1.0]]
[The French then undertook a methodical house-to-house search of the entire area, sweeping each village, and the surrounding paddy fields and jungle, risking encounter with Viet Minh caltrops.][[0.0]]
[Meanwhile, 2nd and 3rd Amphibious used their Crabs and Alligators to herd prisoners towards Trung-An for interrogation.][[0.0]]
[By the end of 29 July, with resistance to the French forces having ceased, a general withdrawal of paratroopers, amphibious groups and marines began.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Aftermath -LSB-edit-RSB- Rebuilding and reaction After the departure of all but regular French infantry, efforts to make the area suitable for permanent occupation by French forces and French-friendly civilians began.][[0.0]]
[This involved the rebuilding of road and rail links -LRB-a rail line ran alongside Road One-RRB-, the repairing of infrastructure, demining, the installation of new Vietnamese government administrators, and the provision of ``everything from rice to anti-malaria tablets''.][[0.0]]
[Over 24 villages were placed under the authority of the Vietnamese government, and Regiment 95 had been driven from the area.][[0.0]]
[In comparison to Fall, South Vietnamese general Lam Quang Thi states in his memoirs that Operation Camargue was ``one of the most successful French military operations during the Indochina war'' in the area of Road One.][[1.0]]
[Newspapers stated that the operation had been a ``total success, demonstrating once more the new aggressiveness and mobility'' of the French forces.][[0.0]]
[However, in the days following the end of the fighting, press reports on the French failure to capture the anticipated large numbers of Viet Minh began to appear though the British newspaper, The Times, did publish claimed casualty figures of 1,550 for the Viet Minh, 200 of which killed.][[0.0]]
[This estimate was altered by the French the next day to 600 killed or wounded and 900 captured, and it was suggested that the operation did ``not appear to have been successful''.][[0.0]]
[In contrast to these figures, Bernard Fall records 182 Viet Minh casualties and 387 prisoners.][[0.0]]
[He also notes that ``51 rifles, eight sub-machine guns, two mortars, and five BARs'' were captured.][[0.0]]
[Of the prisoners, however, it is not recorded how many were confirmed to be members of Regiment 95.][[0.0]]
[Both Fall and the newspapers published in the days following the official termination of the operation on 10 August 1953, give French casualties as 17 dead and 100 wounded.][[0.0]]
[Giap wrote ``it was announced we suffered heavy losses although in fact our losses were insignificant ... their troops had to withdraw with heavy losses''.][[0.0]]
[Fall goes on to record that the ``major defect'' of Operation Camargue was that the French had nothing like the numerical superiority to encircle a force in the terrain around Road One, 15:1 as opposed to the 20:1 or 25:1 that he believed required.][[0.0]]
[He states that the slow French progress -LRB-around 1,500 yards an hour-RRB- and the large distances each unit had to guard from Viet Minh infiltration meant that the Viet Minh could easily escape the net.][[1.0]]
[He also states that Viet Minh intelligence were always aware of French movements, as the size of French units and the complex technology involved in the operation gave its presence and intentions away almost immediately, whereas in contrast the simpler Viet Minh operations were far more difficult to detect.][[1.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Road One and Regiment 95 Regiment 95 survived Operation Camargue and resumed ambushes in 1954, as well as assaulting a Vietnamese garrison near Hué.][[1.0]]
[The regiment remained in the area, taking part in General Giap's 1954 campaign season, until Vietnam was split into North and South Vietnam by the cease-fire, whereupon it infiltrated back to the north along Road One during broad daylight, leaving small cells of guerillas in the area.][[0.0]]
[The regiment returned to resume ambushes of the South Vietnamese Army in 1962.][[1.0]]
