[-LSB-edit-RSB- History -LSB-edit-RSB- Early history Archaeological investigations around Navenby indicate the area has been occupied since at least the British Bronze Age, about 600�BC.][[0.0]]
[The remains of British Iron Age farms have been found at Chapel Lane, a site now protected as a public open space by the district and parish councils and supported by Navenby Archaeology Group.][[1.0]]
[Significant Roman finds include parts of shops and houses that would have fronted onto Ermine Street, down which Roman armies marched to and from the Legionary Fortress at Lincoln.][[0.0]]
[The city of Lincoln was very important at that time, probably the capital of the late Roman Province of Flavia Caesariensis.][[0.0]]
[Evidence suggests that Navenby was a significant staging point along Ermine Street.][[0.0]]
[The Romans are reported to have maintained a small base or garrison in the village, and a possible Romano-British temple and burial sites have been unearthed in the area.][[1.0]]
[A 2009 archaeological dig uncovering a road, building foundations and Roman graves along with pottery and coins, showed Navenby to be a Roman Service Station.][[0.0]]
[Cremations dated to the middle Saxon period have been discovered near the junction of High Dyke with Chapel Lane.][[0.0]]
[Late Saxon remains have also been found under and around St Peter's Church, suggesting the original Roman village had moved from Ermine Street to Church Lane and North Lane by the late-Saxon period.][[0.0]]
[Navenby's Saxon name is unknown.][[0.0]]
[The present name is derived from the Old Norse Nafni+by, which means ``farmstead or village of a man called Nafni''.][[0.0]]
[In the Domesday Book of 1086, Navenby appears as Navenbi and Navenebi.][[0.0]]
[The Vikings exerted considerable influence over Lincolnshire in the 9th and 10th centuries, as can be seen in the many local place names ending in -by.][[1.0]]
[The Viking Way, a 147-mile -LRB-237�km-RRB- footpath that cuts through the village, is a lasting reminder of their presence.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Middle Ages Navenby, originally an agricultural village, became a market town after receiving a charter from Edward the Confessor in the 11th century.][[1.0]]
[The charter was later renewed by William Rufus, Edward III and Richard II.][[1.0]]
[The wide main street, down which farmers once drove their sheep to market, is lasting evidence of its market town status.][[0.0]]
[A market square once stood at the centre, marked by a cross in honour of Queen Eleanor.][[0.0]]
[Today, the square has gone and the cross is a ruin.][[0.0]]
[Parish records exist for Navenby from 1681, although bishops' transcripts go back to 1562.][[0.0]]
[The documents show the village hosted several annual fairs each year: a market fair on 17 October at which farm animals were traded; a feast on the Thursday before Easter; and a Hiring Fair held each May Day, at which servants gathered to seek employment.][[0.0]]
[The records also show that part of the parish of Navenby was enclosed in 1772.][[0.0]]
[Such was the significance of Navenby at this time that a workhouse for the parish poor was erected here, although the building was later given over to other uses.][[0.0]]
[A Sick Society was founded in 1811 and a Parish School was built next to St Peter's Church in 1816, paid for by subscription.][[0.0]]
[Following the 1834 Poor Law Amendment Act Navenby parish became part of the Lincoln Poor Law Union.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- 19th century When the market closed in the early 19th century Navenby lost its status as a market town, and once again became an agricultural village.][[1.0]]
[The Penny Cyclopaedia of 1839, published by The Society for the Diffusion of Useful Knowledge, described the village in this way:  Many buildings were erected in Navenby during the 19th century, including a small Wesleyan Methodist chapel in about 1830, which was completely rebuilt in 1840.][[0.0]]
[A Temperance Hall was built in 1852, later used as a second base by the Wesleyan Reformers.][[0.0]]
[A Volunteer Fire Brigade was founded in 1844, comprising five men and a manual engine.][[0.0]]
[The Provincial Gas Light and Coke Company began supplying gas lighting to the village in 1857, and in 1867 a railway station was built three-quarters of a mile -LRB-1.2�km-RRB- west of the village, on the Lincoln-to-Grantham branch of the Great Northern Railway.][[0.0]]
[By 1871, the Dean and Chapter of Lincoln was the principal landowner and Lord of the Manor of Navenby.][[0.0]]
[A witch bottle was discovered in the foundations of a Navenby farmhouse in 2005, thought to date back to about 1830.][[0.0]]
[Containing pins, human hair and urine, the bottle was believed to protect a household against evil spells.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Modern history Navenby was an agricultural village at the beginning of the 20th century, but the outbreak of the First World War brought changes for the community.][[0.0]]
[A small airfield, Wellingore Heath, was opened on land bordering Navenby in 1917, to provide a base for the Royal Flying Corps and the Royal Naval Air Service.][[0.0]]
[The flat landscape, with its cliff-top situation, proved an ideal situation for flight operations.][[0.0]]
[T. E. Lawrence, perhaps better known as Lawrence of Arabia, was stationed at nearby RAF Cranwell just after the war, in 1926, where he wrote a revised version of his Seven Pillars Of Wisdom.][[0.0]]
[He mentioned Navenby in a letter to a friend at the time, saying: ``I'm too shy to go looking for dirt.][[0.0]]
[That's why I can't go off stewing into the Lincoln or Navenby brothels with the fellows.][[0.0]]
[They think it's because I'm superior: proud, or peculiar or `posh', as they say: and its because I wouldn't know what to do, how to carry myself, where to stop.][[0.0]]
[Wellingore airfield closed after the war ended, but it was re-opened in 1935 and its facilities were expanded during the winter of 1939--40.][[0.0]]
[By then known as RAF Wellingore, notable officers stationed there include Group Captain Douglas Bader and Wing Commander Guy Gibson -- both regular visitors to Navenby.][[0.0]]
[The base served as a satellite field for RAF Digby until 1944 and as a relief landing ground for RAF Cranwell from April 1944 until its final closure in 1945, after which it was used as a camp for prisoners of war from Germany and Ukraine; the inmates were often made to work on the surrounding farmland.][[0.0]]
[Navenby lost many men during the two World Wars.][[0.0]]
[The village war memorial, a rough hewn stone Celtic Cross mounted on a plinth with a three-stepped base, is in the churchyard of St Peter's.][[0.0]]
[It was manufactured by Messrs G Maile & Son Ltd at a cost of £200, and unveiled in April 1921.][[0.0]]
[On it are inscribed the names of the 22�casualties from the First World War and the 8 from the Second World War.][[0.0]]
[Following an initial decline in the population of Navenby at the turn of the 20th century, the post-war years saw numbers rise steeply.][[0.0]]
[This increase can be directly linked to the 35-plus new houses built from the end of the Great War until the 1950s, as well as to other building projects from the 1970s onwards.][[0.0]]
[Other post-war changes include the move away from a dependence on farming.][[0.0]]
[Although Navenby continues to be surrounded by farms, it is now largely a dormitory village for Lincoln, Grantham and beyond.][[0.0]]
[Figures from the 2001 census show that, out of a population of 1,666, almost 600 commute to work each day.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Governance The parish of Navenby was originally in the higher division of the ancient Boothby Graffoe wapentake, in the North Kesteven division of the county of Lincolnshire.][[0.0]]
[The term wapentake dates back to the Vikings and was used to describe a collection of local parishes.][[0.0]]
[It originally meant ``show your weapon'' and the idea behind the term was that all those in favour of a resolution would raise their sword or axe to show agreement.][[0.0]]
[The History of the County of Lincoln, a book written by Thomas Allen in 1834, states:  Navenby was classed as an ancient parish from the 11th to the 19th century, as it came ``under the jurisdiction of a clergyman'' and existed before 1597.][[0.0]]
[Early records show that the Manor of Navenby was granted to the Dean and Chapter of Lincoln in 1292.][[0.0]]
[The money generated by land rent was used by Roger de Newton, the first incumbent of the chantry chapel at Harby, Nottinghamshire, to maintain the building.][[0.0]]
[This followed the death of Queen Eleanor, wife of Edward I, while on a visit to Lincoln.][[0.0]]
[Eleanor died at de Newton's manor house at Harby in November 1290 and the chapel was erected in her honour.][[0.0]]
[The parish began to take on civil as well as ecclesiastical duties following the 16th-century Dissolution of the Monasteries and the Tudor Poor Law Acts of 1601.][[0.0]]
[The ecclesiastical parish of Navenby was originally placed in the Longoboby Rural Deanery, but was transferred to the Graffoe Rural Deanery in 1968, and it is still part of the Diocese of Lincoln.][[0.0]]
[Navenby officially became a civil parish in the 19th century and became a member of the Lincoln Poor Law Union in 1834.][[0.0]]
[The parish was also part of the Lincoln Rural Sanitary District.][[0.0]]
[The Navenby civil parish boundaries were adjusted in 1931, to include the civil parish of nearby Skinnand.][[1.0]]
[Following the Local Government Act 1888, Navenby was governed by Branston Rural District Council from 1894--1931.][[0.0]]
[The village then came under the control of North Kesteven Rural District Council from 1931--1974, after the Local Government Act 1972 reformed the districts of Holland, Kesteven and much of Lindsey into the shire county of Lincolnshire.][[0.0]]
[Today Navenby remains part of the North Kesteven district.][[1.0]]
[Before the 1832 Reform Act, Lincolnshire sent twelve members to parliament, including two for the county, two for the city of Lincoln and two for the boroughs of Boston, Grantham, Great Grimsby and Stamford.][[0.0]]
[As a result of the act, Lincolnshire's electoral divisions were amended, and Navenby became part of the South Division Parliamentary District for Lincolnshire.][[0.0]]
[Two Whig candidates, Henry Handley and Gilbert John Heathcote, were returned in the first election.][[0.0]]
[The village remained in the South Division until 1867, when it was transferred to the Mid Division.][[0.0]]
[In 1885 it joined the North Kesteven Division, and in 1918 it became part of the Grantham Division, until 1974.][[0.0]]
[Today, Navenby has its own parish council, dealing with issues such as play-area revamps and the protection of public open spaces.][[0.0]]
[The council is based at High Street, Navenby.][[0.0]]
[As of 2010, the chairman is Ken Gunning and the vice chairwoman is Margaret Lyon.][[0.0]]
[The second tier of local government provided for Navenby is the Conservative-controlled North Kesteven District Council, which is responsible for housing problems and public health.][[0.0]]
[The council is based at Kesteven Street, Sleaford.][[0.0]]
[Navenby is also represented by two local councillors on the district council.][[0.0]]
[Marianne Jane Overton, an Independent, and Michael David Wylie, a former Independent but now Conservative.][[0.0]]
[They were elected in May 2007.][[0.0]]
[Conservative-controlled Lincolnshire County Council provides the top tier of local government for Navenby, with responsibility for highways, sites of special interest and schools.][[0.0]]
[It is based at the County Offices in Newland, Lincoln.][[0.0]]
[Navenby has one representative on this council, Marianne Jane Overton, who also represents Branston ward.][[0.0]]
[At government level, Navenby is represented by the Right Honorable Douglas Hogg QC, the Conservative MP for Sleaford and North Hykeham constituency.][[0.0]]
[He is a former Minister of Agriculture, Fisheries and Food and his constituency head office is in Sleaford.][[0.0]]
[Navenby comes under the East Midlands constituency for the European Parliament, which is represented by six politicians, including former Conservative turned Liberal Democrat Bill Newton Dunn.][[0.0]]
[Newton Dunn lives in Navenby and his European responsibilities include Vice-Chairman of Foreign Affairs, Human Rights, Common Security and Defence Policy Committee.][[0.0]]
[In the 1980s, Newton Dunn coined the much-used phrase ``Democratic deficit''.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Geography -LSB-edit-RSB- Topography The civil parish of Navenby straddles the old Roman Ermine Street, known locally as High Dyke.][[1.0]]
[The road runs between the neighbouring villages of Boothby Graffoe and Wellingore and covers more than 2,100�acres -LRB-8.5�km2-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The Viking Way, a 147-mile -LRB-237�km-RRB- footpath between the Humber Bridge in North Lincolnshire and Oakham in Rutland, also passes through the village.][[1.0]]
[Navenby is located at 53°06′36″N 0°31′26″W﻿ / ﻿53.11°N 0.52389°W﻿ / 53.11; -0.52389 and known as a Lincolnshire Cliff Village, as it is situated on a ridge of Jurassic limestone called the Lincoln Edge or Lincoln Cliff.][[1.0]]
[The small cliff is one of only a few hills in Lincolnshire.][[0.0]]
[Lying 8.7�miles -LRB-14�km-RRB- south of Lincoln and 8.9�miles -LRB-14.3�km-RRB- north-northwest of Sleaford, Navenby enjoys warm summers and dry frosty winters.][[1.0]]
[During the Ice Age, most of the region surrounding Navenby was covered by ice sheets and this has influenced the topography and nature of the soils.][[0.0]]
[Much of Lincolnshire is low-lying, in some places below sea level, but Navenby's cliff-top position means it is 226�ft -LRB-69�m-RRB- above sea level, giving it commanding views over the River Witham valley.][[0.0]]
[The parish of Navenby is elongated in an east--west direction, extending east to the Lincoln Heath and west to the River Brant.][[0.0]]
[The size of the parish has varied over the past two centuries.][[0.0]]
[In 1821 it covered 2,110�acres -LRB-8.5�km2-RRB-; in 1951 it was 3,345�acres -LRB-13.54�km2-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Housing, streets and nearby places Although house prices have traditionally been lower than the national average in Navenby, they have risen quickly in recent years.][[0.0]]
[In 2004, the average house price rose by 8.2% to £163,186, according to the Land Registry, while in 1999 the average was £60,000.][[0.0]]
[The district surrounding Navenby -- North Kesteven -- remains a relatively inexpensive place to purchase property.][[0.0]]
[Consequently, owner occupation is 77% -- which is higher than both the regional and national averages.][[0.0]]
[However, the rising prices of recent years are resulting in housing becoming increasingly ``out of reach for those on average incomes''.][[0.0]]
[A 2004 Housing Needs Study, carried out by Fordham Associates for North Kesteven District Council, highlighted the need for 460�``affordable homes'' to be built each year for the next five years in Navenby and the surrounding villages.][[0.0]]
[Houses of specific interest in Navenby include Grade II listed Dial House in North Lane, which has a priest hole, and the Old Rectory in North Lane, which was built of Ashlar stone in 1859 by H A Darbyshire, a London architect.][[0.0]]
[Several of Navenby's street names hint at its past.][[1.0]]
[For example, the street now known as Clint Lane was formerly Watery Lane, apparently due to the number of springs that ran along it.][[0.0]]
[The only ``watery'' part of Clint Lane today is a duck pond.][[0.0]]
[The street includes a mix of 18th-century cottages, Victorian properties, Grade II listed farm buildings and 20th-century homes.][[0.0]]
[Gas Lane, which is next to Clint Lane, was originally called Meg's Lane.][[0.0]]
[It was re-named Gas Lane after The Provincial Gas Light and Coke Company set up a base there in 1857.][[0.0]]
[The firm later became the Navenby and Wellingore Gas Light and Coke Company.][[0.0]]
[Although the business has long since disappeared, the street name still survives.][[0.0]]
[The street now known as Church Lane was originally Church Street.][[0.0]]
[The road was named for the village church, St Peter's, which is located here, as is a former village school, now a private house.][[0.0]]
[The school was built by subscription in 1816 and carries the inscription ``The Benefit Society 1821''.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Climate According to the Köppen classification, the British Isles experience a maritime climate characterised by relatively cool summers and mild winters.][[0.0]]
[Compared with other parts of the country, Lincolnshire -- and Navenby -- are slightly warmer and sunnier in the summer and colder and frostier in the winter.][[0.0]]
[Owing to Navenby's inland position, far from the landfall of most Atlantic depressions, it is one of the driest places to live in the UK, receiving, on average, less than 3�ft -LRB-600�mm-RRB- of rain per year.][[0.0]]
[The mean annual daily duration of bright sunshine is four hours and 12�minutes; the absence of any high ground is probably responsible for the area being one of the sunniest parts of the British Isles.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Demography The United Kingdom Census 2001 found Navenby had 861 households and a population of 1,666, of which 792 were male and 874 female.][[0.0]]
[This figure shows a population growth of more than 70% in the past 30�years, mainly due to on-going house-building projects.][[0.0]]
[The 2001 census revealed there were only three second homes in the village, but that many villagers commuted each day to work.][[0.0]]
[The great majority of properties are owner-occupied, with just over 100 rented from the council or private landlords.][[0.0]]
[Most of the houses -LRB-over 500-RRB- are classified as detached, the average number of rooms per property is 6.03 and the average household size is 2.31.][[0.0]]
[Ethnic diversity is minimal in Navenby.][[0.0]]
[Statistics for 2001 show that of 707�households questioned, 703 were classed as white.][[0.0]]
[Of those 707�households, 104 were pensioners living alone, 176 were couples without children, 27 were lone parents and 153�couples with dependent children.][[0.0]]
[343 of the households had at least one vehicle.][[0.0]]
[Sickness rates were surprising high -- with 237�households reporting one or more people with limiting long-term illness.][[0.0]]
[A total of 82% call themselves Christians.][[0.0]]
[People aged between 25 and 44 represent the majority of Navenby residents, with 451 recorded in 2001.][[0.0]]
[This is closely followed by people aged 45--64 -LRB-442-RRB- and children aged 5--15 -LRB-221-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Children aged under 4 -LRB-69-RRB- and people aged 16--24 -LRB-115-RRB- are in the minority.][[0.0]]
[Almost 200�people -LRB-190-RRB- over the age of 75 were living in Navenby in 2001.][[0.0]]
[The mean age of Navenby's population is 43.48 and the median age is 43.50.][[0.0]]
[In 1851, 136�babies in every 1,000 died in their first year.][[0.0]]
[This figure dropped to 107 per 1,000 in 1911 and three per 1,000 by 2001.][[0.0]]
[The people of Navenby enjoy a high employment rate, although most work outside the village.][[0.0]]
[Almost 600 commute each day, with the average journey to work being 12.5�miles -LRB-20.18�km-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The 2001 census revealed that of 1,186�people questioned aged 16--74, 722 were economically active, with men working an average of 44.79�hours each week and women an average of 31.23.][[0.0]]
[The service industry was the largest sector of the local economy, employing 67% of all workers, compared to 1841 when 48% worked in agriculture.][[0.0]]
[Manufacturing work was carried out by 178�people in 2001 and 565 were in the service industries.][[0.0]]
[Of these workers, 371 were classed as managerial and professional.][[0.0]]
[In 1841, just 16.6% of male workers had middle class jobs, but the 2001 census showed this figure had increased to 47.9%.][[0.0]]
[Those with few or no qualifications numbered 597 in 2001, while 328 had higher diplomas, degrees or further degrees.][[0.0]]
[In contrast, about 53% of children aged 5--14 went to school in 1851, but today 58% of those aged 16--17 stay on at school.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Economy and media Navenby was originally an agricultural village, with most people living off the land or trading goods in the local market.][[0.0]]
[Statistics show that, in 1841, 48% of villagers worked in agriculture, but today the majority of villagers, 67%, are employed in the service industry and most commute to work away from Navenby.][[0.0]]
[The village does, however, offer some work opportunities, with the High Street lined with shops, fast-food stores and public houses.][[0.0]]
[A doctors' surgery, a building society and a residential care home are in the village.][[0.0]]
[Nearby Lincoln offers a wider variety of work, with its economy based mainly on public administration, commerce, arable farming and tourism.][[0.0]]
[Over the past few years, Lincoln has also seen rapid development in its retail sector, in an attempt to compete with the neighbouring cities of Nottingham and Sheffield.][[0.0]]
[The local newspaper for Navenby is the Lincolnshire Echo, which includes news and sports reports, as well as job advertisements.][[0.0]]
[The local radio stations for the village are BBC Radio Lincolnshire on 94.9�FM and Lincs FM on 102.2�FM.][[0.0]]
[The newest addition to the local airwaves is Siren FM, a community radio station that broadcasts on 107.3�FM from the University of Lincoln.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Landmarks The centre of Navenby village is a designated conservation area; many of the stone- and brick-built houses date back hundreds of years.][[0.0]]
[More than 20 of the properties, as well as the 1935 red telephone kiosk in High Street, have listed building status.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Mrs Smith's Cottage Mrs Smith's Cottage is a mid-19th century Grade II listed building made from early Victorian red bricks.][[0.0]]
[The range, the heart of the house, was in daily use for cooking and heating until the mid-1990s.][[0.0]]
[The only access to the bedrooms is by a ladder.][[0.0]]
[Electricity was installed in the 1930s, and the only other visible modern innovations are the coldwater tap -- installed to prevent the local council condemning the cottage in the late 1970s -- and an inside toilet.][[0.0]]
[The original outside privy and washhouse can still be viewed.][[0.0]]
[The cottage is named after its last resident, Mrs Hilda Smith, who lived there until 1995, when she was 102 years old.][[0.0]]
[When Mrs Smith died, villagers mounted a campaign to ensure the cottage was kept as ``something special'' for Navenby.][[0.0]]
[Today the cottage is run as a museum, having been granted official museum status in March 2000.][[0.0]]
[It is open for much of the year and staffed by volunteers.][[0.0]]
[The old pig sty and storage shed, deemed beyond repair, were demolished and the bricks used to construct a purpose-built visitor centre, used for exhibitions about Navenby and the local area.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- St Peter's Church The Grade I listed Anglican parish church in Navenby is dedicated to Saint Peter.][[0.0]]
[Despite parish registers existing from 1681, and Bishop's transcripts that go back to 1562, it is difficult to date the building as it has a mishmash of styles, although its origins are probably 13th century.][[0.0]]
[St Peter's is made up of three parts, including a mid-19th-century west tower, which replaced the original in 1859--60 after it fell down.][[0.0]]
[The perpendicular clerestory is decorated with shields in quatrefoils and is lit with closely set three-light windows.][[0.0]]
[The tall decorated chancel has very large windows.][[0.0]]
[The side windows have reticulated tracery.][[0.0]]
[The large east window was partly rebuilt in 1875--76, and is composed of six lights with two large mouchettes nodding to each other, as well as a very large reticulation unit.][[0.0]]
[The sedilia and piscina are thought to date back to William de Herleston, who was rector of St Peter's from 1325--29.][[0.0]]
[He was Edward I's chancellor and later became Canon of Llandaff.][[0.0]]
[A founder's tomb, which is in a slightly different style, is probably that of his successor, John de Fenton, who was rector until 1832.][[0.0]]
[The font is a lavish Victorian affair by Charles Kirk Junior, which was shown at the 1862 International Exhibition in London.][[0.0]]
[The Pulpit is Jacobean and the Rood screen by Temple Moore dates from 1910.][[0.0]]
[The Royal Arms are signed ``Thomas Hunton of Lincoln.][[0.0]]
[There is a late 13th-century grave slab, with an inscription in Norman script which says ``Pray for Richard de Lue'' -LRB-Louth-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The church also contains an Easter Sepulchre.][[0.0]]
[The carving is recognised as one of the finest in Lincolnshire, if not in the country, and receives a mention in virtually every book written on churches and their architecture.][[0.0]]
[The churchyard is managed as a nature reserve.][[0.0]]
[In 1982, church officials threatened to close St Peter's Church after being refused permission to sell silverware to pay for urgent repairs.][[0.0]]
[The Archdeacon, the Venerable Michael Adie, ruled that such sales were against church policy, and that funds must be raised locally.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Transport Navenby village lies at the eastern end of Navenby parish and is best accessed by road, as the A607 trunk road passes through the heart of the village.][[0.0]]
[Navenby can also be reached from the A15 road, which runs past the end of Green Man Lane and links Lincoln with Sleaford.][[0.0]]
[Editors of the website RoadGhosts.com claim this is one of the most haunted roads in Britain.][[0.0]]
[Navenby once had its own railway station, built in 1867 as part of the Lincoln to Grantham branch of the Great Northern Railway.][[0.0]]
[It fell victim, however, to the government's post-war railway closure programme, which was designed to modernise the service and return it to profitability.][[0.0]]
[Some 3,318�miles -LRB-5,340�km-RRB- of railway were closed between 1948 and 1962 under this scheme, including Navenby station, which was shut in 1962.][[0.0]]
[Today, the closest stations to Navenby are Lincoln, Sleaford, Grantham and Newark North Gate.][[0.0]]
[Grantham and Newark are both part of the high-speed London to Scotland East Coast Main Line; Lincoln and Sleaford are branch line stations.][[0.0]]
[Bus services to and from Navenby are limited.][[0.0]]
[The main service is provided by the Stagecoach travel company, which runs the Number�1 service from Lincoln to Grantham, via Navenby, along the A607 every 30�minutes, from 7�am until 7�pm, every day except Sunday.][[0.0]]
[There is only one bus on a Sunday.][[0.0]]
[The same Number�1 service runs in the opposite direction too, from Grantham to Lincoln via Navenby, every 30�minutes each day except Sunday.][[0.0]]
[Hodson's Coaches of Navenby runs an extra bus each Sunday to Lincoln.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Education Navenby is served by its own village school, Navenby Church of England Primary School.][[0.0]]
[It is a voluntary controlled school for children aged 4--11.][[0.0]]
[There is a choice of nearby senior schools for older Navenby pupils, although none are within the village.][[0.0]]
[Lincolnshire County Council operates a preference system for parents, which allows them to choose a preferred school, rather than one for which they are in the catchment area.][[0.0]]
[The closest senior school to Navenby, and the one that is within the Designated Transport Area, is Sir William Robertson High School at Welbourn.][[0.0]]
[This is a mixed comprehensive school named after Field Marshal William Robertson, who was born in Welbourn and served in the First World War.][[0.0]]
[Other senior schools within a 10-mile -LRB-16�km-RRB- radius include Branston Community College, and two grammar schools in nearby Sleaford -- Carre's Grammar School for boys and Kesteven and Sleaford High School for girls.][[0.0]]
[Private schools are available in Lincoln.][[0.0]]
[Nearby Lincoln has two higher education institutions, the older being Bishop Grosseteste University College, which started life in 1862 as a teacher training college linked to the Anglican Church.][[0.0]]
[During the 1990s, the college branched out into new subject areas with a focus on the arts and drama.][[0.0]]
[The larger University of Lincoln began as the University of Lincolnshire and Humberside in 1996, when the University of Humberside opened a Lincoln campus next to Brayford Pool.][[0.0]]
[Lincoln Art College and Riseholme Agricultural College, which had previously been part of De Montfort University in Leicester, were absorbed into the university in 2001.][[0.0]]
[The university changed its name to the University of Lincoln in 2002.][[0.0]]
[In the 2005/6 academic year, 8,292�full-time undergraduates were studying at the university.][[0.0]]
[Many local senior schools offer sixth form tuition, and further education courses for students aged 16 and over are also provided by Lincoln College, which is the largest educational institution in Lincolnshire.][[0.0]]
[It has 18,500�students, of whom 2,300 are full time.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Culture and community Navenby used to be served by several public houses, but The Butcher's Arms and The Green Man Inn have long been converted into private houses.][[0.0]]
[Now, just the King's Head and The Lion and Royal remain.][[0.0]]
[The Grade II listed 18th-century King's Head is probably the oldest public house in the village; the nearby Lion and Royal dates from 1824 and is also Grade II listed.][[0.0]]
[It was probably just called ``The Lion'' when it first opened, but added ``Royal'' to its name in honour of a special visitor.][[0.0]]
[There is a large emblem over the front door, topped by the Prince of Wales's feathers, presented after the Prince -LRB-later Edward VII-RRB- stayed there, albeit briefly, in 1870.][[0.0]]
[The former Green Man Inn, at the junction of Green Man Lane and the A15, was once a staging post for travellers and may have also been a court house.][[0.0]]
[The Lincoln Club was established here in about 1741, catering for the ``distinguished gentlemen of Lincolnshire''.][[0.0]]
[Sir Francis Dashwood, founder of the notorious Hellfire Club, was a member, as were Lord Monson of Burton and Lord Robert Manners of Bloxholm.][[0.0]]
[Lincolnshire has a number of local dishes, including stuffed chine and haslet, and Navenby is home to several local food champions.][[0.0]]
[Navenby baker Pete Welbourne was named as Great Britain's Baker of the Year in 2004, for his Lincolnshire Plum Bread recipe, and Odling Bros butchers' shop has enjoyed repeated success in an annual competition to find the best Lincolnshire pork sausages in the county.][[0.0]]
[Local legend has it that Navenby is part of the Temple Bruer Pentagram.][[0.0]]
[The pentagram includes the nearby villages of Temple Bruer -- which has strong connections with the Knights Templar of the 12th century -- as well as Wellingore and Harmston.][[0.0]]
[It appears the sign is centred on the sewage works just west of Navenby.][[0.0]]
[The pentagram is seen as having magical associations and is often said to have offered protection to witches.][[0.0]]
[It also, however, has links with Christianity, Freemasonry and the Knights Templar, who used the pentagram symbol to represent ``infinity, connectiveness and oneness''.][[0.0]]
[Although the A607 trunk road passes through Navenby, the village can also be accessed from the ``haunted'' A15, which runs past the end of Green Man Lane.][[0.0]]
[According to local legend, a hanging tree once stood at the junction of the two roads, and those who died there still haunt the area.][[0.0]]
[Scottish singer Barbara Dickson, OBE, briefly lived in Navenby in the 1970s.][[0.0]]
[She went on to star in the Willy Russell musical Blood Brothers in 1982, as well as sing on the cast album recording of Chess, dueting with Elaine Paige.][[0.0]]
[Another Navenby notable is Fenella Stoner, an artist who has exhibited at the Royal Academy and often paints pictures of the village and her garden.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Public services Navenby's water is supplied by the Anglian Water company, and there is a sewage treatment works just west of the village.][[0.0]]
[Healthcare is provided by the United Lincolnshire Hospitals NHS Trust, and a doctors' surgery operates in North Lane.][[0.0]]
[The nearest NHS hospital is Lincoln County Hospital, Greetwell Road, Lincoln, which is 8.7�miles -LRB-14�km-RRB- north of Navenby.][[0.0]]
[The second closest is Grantham and District Hospital, Manthorpe Rd, Grantham.][[0.0]]
[The nearest dentists are also based in Lincoln or Grantham.][[0.0]]
[The East Midlands Ambulance Service NHS Trust operates ambulances in the Navenby area, and the village is also covered by The Lincolnshire and Nottinghamshire Air Ambulance, based at nearby Royal Air Force Waddington on the edge of Waddington village.][[0.0]]
[Other emergency services are provided by Lincolnshire Fire and Rescue, whose nearest fire stations are in Lincoln and Grantham, and the Lincolnshire Police Force.][[0.0]]
[The nearest police stations are in Lincoln, Sleaford and Grantham.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Sport and clubs Navenby has a bowls club, Navenby Bowls Club, with approximately 50�members.][[0.0]]
[The bowls season lasts from May to August and club members take part in three Bowls Leagues; Division�3 of the Cliff Bowls League, Section�C of the City Evening League and Division�3 of the Lincoln and District League.][[0.0]]
[At least three evening matches are held each week during the season.][[0.0]]
[Navenby also has several football teams for children, run under the title of Navenby Juniors FC.][[0.0]]
[The teams play in the Under-12, Under-11, Under-10, Under-9 and Under-8 sections of the Grantham Youth Football League.][[0.0]]
[The Under-10 team were the 2006--07 League Champions and the Under-11s were the Fair Play League Winners in 2006--07.][[0.0]]
[Nearby Lincoln has a professional football team, Lincoln City F.C., nicknamed ``The Imps'', which plays at the Sincil Bank stadium.][[0.0]]
[Navenby villager Tracey Duxbury is a member of the Lady Imps team, the women's team attached to Lincoln City.][[0.0]]
[She became one of the youngest people to achieve the UEFA B coaching license in 2006.][[0.0]]
[Other clubs include Navenby Archaeology Group, which aims to uncover the village's extensive historic past.][[0.0]]
[There is also the Women's Institute, which has celebrated its 40th anniversary in 2007, and Artists of Navenby, a 40-strong group of artists.][[0.0]]

[-LSB-edit-RSB- Background to the migration The Latter Day Saints were first organized as the Church of Christ in 1830.][[0.0]]
[Early members of the Church often encountered hostility, primarily due to their practice of withdrawing from secular society and gathering in locales to practice their distinct religious beliefs.][[0.0]]
[Their neighbors felt threatened by the Church's rapid growth in numbers, by its tendency to vote as a bloc and acquire political power, by its claims of divine favor, and by the practice of polygamy.][[0.0]]
[Violence directed against the Church and its members caused the body of the Church to move from Ohio to Missouri, then to Illinois.][[0.0]]
[Despite the frequent moves, Church members were unable to escape opposition, which culminated in the extermination order against all Mormons living in the state by Missouri Governor Lilburn Boggs and the murder of its leader Joseph Smith in 1844.][[0.0]]
[Brigham Young, Smith's successor as Church leader, said that he had received divine direction to organize the church members and head beyond the western frontier of the United States.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Need for handcart companies Soon after the first Mormon pioneers reached Utah in 1847, the Church began encouraging its converts in the British Isles and elsewhere in Europe to emigrate to Utah.][[1.0]]
[From 1849 to 1855, about 16,000 European Latter-day Saints traveled to Utah by ship, rail and then ox and wagon.][[0.0]]
[Although most of these emigrants paid their own expenses, the Church established the Perpetual Emigration Fund to provide financial assistance for poor emigrants to trek west, which they would repay as they were able.][[0.0]]
[Contributions to expand the fund were encouraged.][[0.0]]
[When contributions and loan repayments dropped off in 1855 after a poor harvest in Utah, President Young decided to begin using handcarts because the Latter-day Saints who remained in Europe were mostly poor.][[0.0]]
[Young also believed it would speed the journey.][[0.0]]
[Young proposed the plan in a letter to Franklin D. Richards, President of the European Mission, in September 1855.][[0.0]]
[His letter was published in the Millennial Star, the Church's England-based periodical, on December 22, 1855, along with an editorial by Richards endorsing the project.][[0.0]]
[The cost of the migration was expected to be reduced by one-third.][[0.0]]
[The response was overwhelming -- in 1856 the Perpetual Emigration Fund supported the travel of 2,012 European emigrants, compared with 1,161 the year before.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Outfitting Emigrants departed from an English port -LRB-generally Liverpool-RRB- and travelled by ship to New York or Boston, then by railroad to Iowa City, Iowa, the western terminus of the rail line, where they would be outfitted with handcarts and other supplies.][[0.0]]
[Built to Brigham Young's design, the handcarts resembled a large wheelbarrow, with two wheels five feet -LRB-1.5�m-RRB- in diameter and a single axle four and half feet -LRB-1.4�m-RRB- wide, and weighing 60 pounds -LRB-27�kg-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Running along each side of the bed were seven-foot -LRB-2.1 m-RRB- pull shafts ending with a three-foot -LRB-0.9�m-RRB- crossbar at the front.][[0.0]]
[The crossbar allowed the carts to be pushed or pulled.][[0.0]]
[Cargo was carried in a box about three feet by four feet -LRB-0.9�m by 1.2�m-RRB-, with 8 inch -LRB-0.2�m-RRB- walls.][[0.0]]
[The handcarts generally carried up to 250 pounds -LRB-110�kg-RRB- of supplies and luggage, though they were capable of handling loads as heavy as 500 pounds -LRB-230�kg-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Carts used in the first year's migration were made entirely of wood -LRB-``Iowa hickory or oak''-RRB-; in later years a stronger design was substituted, which included metal elements.][[0.0]]
[The handcart companies were organized using the handcarts and sleeping tents as the primary units.][[0.0]]
[Five persons were assigned per handcart, with each individual limited to 17 pounds -LRB-7.7�kg-RRB- of clothing and bedding.][[0.0]]
[Each round tent, supported by a center pole, housed 20 occupants and was supervised by a tent captain.][[0.0]]
[Five tents were supervised by the captain of a hundred -LRB-or ``sub-captain''-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[Provisions for each group of one hundred emigrants were carried in an ox wagon, and were distributed by the tent captains.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- 1856: First three companies The first two ships departed England in late March and mid-April and sailed to Boston.][[0.0]]
[The emigrants spent several weeks in Iowa City, where they constructed their handcarts and were outfitted with supplies before beginning their trek of about 1,300 miles -LRB-2,093�km-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[About 815 emigrants from the first two ships were organized into the first three handcart companies, headed by captains Edmund Ellsworth, Daniel D. McArthur, and Edward Bunker.][[0.0]]
[The captains were missionaries returning to their homes in Utah and were familiar with the route.][[0.0]]
[Most of the sub-captains were also returning missionaries.][[0.0]]
[Across Iowa they followed an existing road about 275 miles -LRB-443�km-RRB- to Council Bluffs, following a route that is close to current U.S. Route 6.][[0.0]]
[After crossing the Missouri River, they paused for a few days at a Mormon outpost in Florence, Nebraska -LRB-modern-day Omaha-RRB- for repairs, before beginning the remaining 1,030-mile -LRB-1,658�km-RRB- journey along the Mormon Trail to Salt Lake City.][[0.0]]
[The companies made good time, and their trips were largely uneventful.][[0.0]]
[The emigrant companies included many children and elderly individuals, and pushing and pulling handcarts was difficult work.][[0.0]]
[Journals and recollections describe periods of illness and hunger.][[0.0]]
[Like other companies traveling on the Emigrant Trail, deaths occurred along the way.][[1.0]]
[Hafen and Hafen's Handcarts to Zion lists 13 deaths from the first company, seven from the second, and fewer than seven from the third.][[0.0]]
[Journal entries reflect the optimism of the handcart pioneers, even amid their hardships:  The first two companies arrived in Salt Lake City on September 26 and the third followed less than a week later.][[0.0]]
[The first three companies were regarded as having demonstrated the feasibility of emigrating using handcarts.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- 1856: Willie and Martin handcart companies The last two handcart companies of 1856 departed late from England.][[0.0]]
[The ship Thornton, carrying the emigrants who became the Willie Company, did not leave England until May 4.][[0.0]]
[The leader of the Latter-day Saints on the Thornton was James G. Willie.][[0.0]]
[Another three weeks passed before the Horizon, carrying the emigrants who formed the Martin Company, departed.][[0.0]]
[The late departures may have been the result of difficulties in procuring ships in response to the unexpected demand, but the results would be tragic.][[0.0]]
[With slow communications in the era before the transatlantic telegraph, the Church agents in Iowa City were not expecting the additional emigrants and had to make frantic preparations for their arrival.][[0.0]]
[Critical weeks were spent hastily assembling the carts and outfitting the companies.][[0.0]]
[When the companies reached Florence, additional time was lost making repairs to the poorly built carts.][[0.0]]
[Emigrant John Chislett describes the problems with the carts:  Prior to the Willie Company departing Florence, the company met to debate the wisdom of such a late departure.][[0.0]]
[Because the emigrants were unfamiliar with the trail and the climate, they deferred to the returning missionaries and Church agents.][[0.0]]
[One of the returning missionaries, Levi Savage, urged them to spend the winter in Nebraska.][[0.0]]
[He argued that such a late departure with a company consisting of the elderly, women and young children would lead to suffering, sickness and even death.][[0.0]]
[All of the other Church elders argued that the trip should go forward, expressing optimism that the company would be protected by divine intervention.][[0.0]]
[Some members of the company, perhaps as many as 100, decided to spend the winter in Florence or in Iowa, but the majority, about 404 in number -LRB-including Savage-RRB- continued the journey west.][[0.0]]
[The Willie Company left Florence on August 17 and the Martin Company on August 27.][[0.0]]
[Two ox-wagon trains, led by captains W.B. Hodgett and John A. Hunt, followed the Martin Company.][[0.0]]
[Near Wood River, Nebraska, a herd of bison caused the Willie Company's cattle to stampede, and nearly 30 cattle were lost.][[0.0]]
[Left without enough cattle to pull all of the wagons, each handcart was required to take on an additional 100 pounds -LRB-45�kg-RRB- of flour.][[0.0]]
[In early September, Franklin D. Richards, returning from Europe where he had served as the Church's mission president, passed the emigrant companies.][[0.0]]
[Richards and the 12 returning missionaries who accompanied him, traveling in carriages and light wagons pulled by horses and mules, pressed on to Utah to obtain assistance for the emigrants.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Disaster and rescue In early October the two companies reached Fort Laramie, Wyoming, where they expected to be restocked with provisions, but no provisions were there for them.][[0.0]]
[The companies had to cut back food rations, hoping that their supplies would last until help could be sent from Utah.][[0.0]]
[To lighten their loads, the Martin Company cut the luggage allowance to 10 pounds -LRB-4.5�kg-RRB- per person, discarding clothing and blankets that soon would be desperately needed.][[0.0]]
[On October 4 the Richards party reached Salt Lake City and conferred with president Brigham Young and other Church leaders.][[0.0]]
[The next morning the Church was meeting in a general conference, where Young and the other speakers called on the Church members to provide wagons, mules, supplies, and teamsters for a rescue mission.][[0.0]]
[On the morning of October 7 the first rescue party left Salt Lake City with 16 wagonloads of food and supplies, pulled by four-mule teams with 27 young men serving as teamsters and rescuers.][[0.0]]
[The party elected George D. Grant as their captain.][[0.0]]
[Throughout October more wagon trains were assembled, and by the end of the month 250 relief wagons were on the road.][[0.0]]
[Meanwhile, the Willie and Martin companies were running out of food and encountering bitterly cold temperatures.][[0.0]]
[On October 19 a blizzard struck the region, halting the two companies and the relief party.][[0.0]]
[The Willie Company was along the Sweetwater River approaching the Continental Divide.][[0.0]]
[A scouting party sent ahead by the main rescue party found and greeted the emigrants, gave them a small amount of flour, encouraged them that rescue was near, and then rushed onward to try to locate the Martin Company.][[0.0]]
[The members of the Willie Company had just reached the end of their flour supplies.][[0.0]]
[They began slaughtering the handful of broken-down cattle that still remained while their death toll mounted.][[0.0]]
[On October 20 Captain Willie and Joseph Elder went ahead by mule through the snow to locate the supply train and inform them of the company's desperate situation.][[0.0]]
[They arrived at the rescue party's campsite near South Pass that evening, and by the next evening the rescue party reached the Willie Company and provided them with food and assistance.][[0.0]]
[Half of the rescue party remained to assist the Willie Company while the other half pressed forward to assist the Martin Company.][[0.0]]
[The difficulties of the Willie Company were not yet over.][[0.0]]
[On October 23, the second day after the main rescue party had arrived, the Willie Company faced the most difficult section of the trail--the ascent up Rocky Ridge.][[0.0]]
[The climb took place during a howling snowstorm through knee-deep snow.][[0.0]]
[That night 13 emigrants died.][[0.0]]
[On October 19, the Martin Company was about 110 miles -LRB-177�km-RRB- further east, making its last crossing of the North Platte River near present-day Casper, Wyoming.][[0.0]]
[Shortly after completing the crossing, the blizzard struck.][[0.0]]
[Many members of the company suffered from hypothermia or frostbite after wading through the frigid river.][[0.0]]
[They set up camp at Red Bluffs, unable to continue forward through the snow.][[0.0]]
[Meanwhile the original scouting party continued eastward until it reached a small vacant fort at Devil's Gate, where they had been instructed to wait for the rest of the rescue party if they had not found the Martin Company.][[0.0]]
[When the main rescue party rejoined them, another scouting party consisting of Joseph Young, Abel Garr, and Daniel Webster Jones was sent forward.][[0.0]]
[The Martin company remained in their camp at Red Bluffs for nine days until the three scouts finally arrived on October 28.][[0.0]]
[By the time the scouts arrived, 56 members of the company had died.][[0.0]]
[The scouts urged the emigrants to begin moving again.][[0.0]]
[Three days later the main rescue party met the Martin Company and the Hodgett and Hunt wagon companies and helped them on to Devil's Gate.][[0.0]]
[George D. Grant, who headed the rescue party, reported to President Young:  At Devil's Gate the rescue party unloaded the baggage carried in the wagons of the Hodgett and Hunt wagon companies that had been following the Martin Company so the wagons could be used to transport the weakest emigrants.][[0.0]]
[A small group remained at Devil's Gate over the winter to protect the property.][[0.0]]
[On November 4 the company had to cross the Sweetwater River, which was about 2 feet -LRB-0.6 m-RRB- deep and 90 to 120 feet -LRB-27 to 37 m-RRB- wide.][[0.0]]
[The stream was clogged with floating ice.][[0.0]]
[The young men of the rescue party -LRB-accounts mention George W. Grant, C. Allen Huntington, David P. Kimball, and Stephen W. Taylor-RRB- spent much of the day pulling the carts and carrying many of the emigrants across the river.][[0.0]]
[Andrew Jensen later stated that some of the young rescuers died from the effects of the exposure.][[0.0]]
[The severe weather forced the Martin Company to halt for another five days at Martin's Cove, a few miles west of Devil's Gate.][[0.0]]
[The rescue parties escorted the emigrants from both companies to Utah through more snow and severe weather while their members continued to suffer death from disease and exposure.][[1.0]]
[The Willie Company arrived in Salt Lake City on November 9; 68 members of the company had lost their lives.][[0.0]]
[Meanwhile, a backup relief party of 77 teams and wagons was making its way east to provide additional assistance to the Martin Company.][[0.0]]
[After passing Fort Bridger the leaders of the backup party concluded that the Martin Company must have wintered east of the Rockies, so they turned back.][[0.0]]
[When word of the returning backup relief party was communicated to Young, he ordered the courier to return and tell them to turn back east and continue until they found the handcart company, but several days had been lost.][[0.0]]
[On November 18 the backup party met the Martin Company with the greatly needed supplies.][[0.0]]
[At last all the members of the handcart party were now able to ride in wagons.][[0.0]]
[The 104 wagons carrying the Martin Company arrived in Salt Lake City on November 30; at least 145 members of the company had lost their lives.][[1.0]]
[Many of the survivors had to have fingers, toes, or limbs amputated due to severe frostbite.][[0.0]]
[After the companies arrived in Utah, the residents generously opened their homes to the arriving emigrants, feeding and caring for them over the winter.][[0.0]]
[The emigrants would eventually go on to Latter-day Saint settlements throughout Utah and the West.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Responsibility for the tragedy American West historian, Wallace Stegner, described the inadequate planning and improvident decisions leading to the tragedy when he wrote,  As early as November 2, 1856, while the Willie and Martin companies were still making their way to safety, Brigham Young responded to criticism of his own leadership by rebuking Franklin Richards and Daniel Spencer for allowing the companies to leave so late.][[0.0]]
[However, many authors argued that Young, as author of the plan, was responsible.][[0.0]]
[Ann Eliza Young, daughter of one of the men in charge of building the carts and a former plural wife of Brigham Young, described her ex-husband's plan as a ``cold-blooded, scheming, blasphemous policy.''][[0.0]]
[Stegner described Richards as a scapegoat for Young's fundamental errors in planning, though Howard Christy, professor emeritus at Brigham Young University, noted that Richards, as the highest ranking official in Florence, Nebraska area, was, in fact, the official who would have had the authority and capability to have averted the tragedy by halting their late departure.][[0.0]]
[Many survivors of the tragedy refused to blame anyone.][[0.0]]
[Survivor John Jacques wrote, ``I blame nobody.][[1.0]]
[I am not anxious to blame anybody�... I have no doubt that those who had to do with its management meant well and tried to do the best they could under the circumstances.''][[0.0]]
[Another survivor, Francis Webster, was quoted as having said, ``Was I sorry that I chose to come by hand cart?][[0.0]]
[Neither then nor any minute of my life since.][[0.0]]
[The price we paid to become acquainted with God was a privilege to pay and I am thankful that I was privileged to come in the Martin Hand Cart Company.''][[0.0]]
[On the other hand, survivor John Chislett, who later left the Church, wrote bitterly of Richards promising them that ``we should get to Zion in safety.''][[0.0]]
[In May 2006, a panel of researchers at the annual conference of the Mormon History Association blamed the tragedy on a failure of leadership.][[0.0]]
[Lyndia Carter, a trails historian, said Franklin D. Richards ``was responsible, in my mind, for the late departure'' because ``he started the snowball down the slope'' that eventually ``added up to disaster.''][[0.0]]
[Christy agreed that ``leadership from the top, from the outset, was seriously short of the mark.''][[0.0]]
[Robert Briggs, an attorney, said ``It's almost a foregone conclusion�... there is evidence of negligence.][[0.0]]
[With leaders all the way up to Brigham Young, there was mismanagement.''][[0.0]]
[On the other hand, Rebecca Bartholomew and Leonard J. Arrington wrote, ``Memories of what was perhaps the worst disaster in the history of western migration have been palliated by what could also be regarded as the most heroic rescue of the Mormon frontier.''][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- 1857--60: Last five companies A number of lessons had been learned from the 1856 disaster that allowed the Church to continue the handcart system while avoiding another disaster.][[1.0]]
[Never again would a handcart company depart Florence later than July 7.][[0.0]]
[The construction of the handcarts was modified to strengthen them and reduce repairs.][[0.0]]
[The handcarts would now be regularly greased.][[0.0]]
[Arrangements were made to replenish supplies along the route.][[0.0]]
[By 1857 the Perpetual Emigration Fund was exhausted; almost all of the handcart emigrants that year and in subsequent years had to pay their own way.][[0.0]]
[With the increased cost, the number of handcart emigrants dropped from nearly 2,000 in 1856 to about 480 in 1857.][[0.0]]
[Nevertheless, in 1857 two companies made the trek, both arriving in Salt Lake City by September 13.][[0.0]]
[Perhaps the most notable incident was when a captain of the U.S. Army's Utah Expedition, on its way to Utah to confront Young and the Mormons, donated an ox to the hungry emigrants.][[0.0]]
[With the uncertainty caused by the Utah War, the Church called off all European emigration for 1858.][[0.0]]
[In 1859 one handcart company crossed the plains.][[0.0]]
[The emigrants were now able to travel by rail to Saint Joseph, Missouri, after which they went by riverboat to Florence where they were outfitted with handcarts and supplies.][[0.0]]
[When the 1859 company reached Fort Laramie, they discovered their food was running dangerously short, so they cut back on rations.][[0.0]]
[When they reached Devil's Gate the last flour was distributed.][[0.0]]
[Emigrant Ebeneezer B. Beesley recalled an incident in which a group of rough mountain men fed the hungry emigrants.][[0.0]]
[One of the mountain men then asked a young woman from the company to stay with him, which the tired woman agreed to do.][[0.0]]
[-LRB-William Atkin recalled another version of the story in which two young women married two mountain men.-RRB-][[0.0]]
[The hunger worsened when expected supplies were not available when they reached the Green River.][[0.0]]
[Three days later wagons from Utah carrying provisions finally rescued the famished emigrants.][[0.0]]
[The last two handcart companies made the journey in 1860, again following the route through St. Joseph.][[1.0]]
[Although the journey continued to be difficult for the emigrants, these companies had relatively uneventful trips and experienced little loss of life.][[0.0]]
[After 1860 handcarts were no longer used.][[0.0]]
[The Church implemented a new system of emigration, in which wagon trains travelled east from Salt Lake City in the spring and returned with emigrants in the summer.][[0.0]]
[The transcontinental railroad was being constructed, and the railroad terminus gradually moved westward, shortening the trip.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Legacy Handcart pioneers and the handcart movement are important parts of LDS culture, music and fiction.][[1.0]]
[Arthur King Peters described the importance of this part of Mormon history in Seven Trails West:  Wallace Stegner praised the examples of those of the handcart companies, particularly in comparison to other pioneer parties:  -LSB-edit-RSB- Reenactments Reenactments, in which a group dressed in 19th century garb travels for one or more days pushing and pulling handcarts, have become a popular activity among LDS wards, youth groups, and families.][[1.0]]
[The reenactments have been lauded by LDS leaders; for example, M. Russell Ballard of the Quorum of the Twelve Apostles said, ``Through music, drama, and stirring reenactments, we will be reminded of incredible pioneer journeys, both temporal and spiritual.''][[0.0]]
[The reenactments have become so popular that the Bureau of Land Management is studying the impact on the trail and its environment, especially in the area around Rocky Ridge, Wyoming.][[0.0]]
[According to the Casper Star-Tribune, the BLM has had to impose a fee to offset the costs of monitoring the impacts of reenactors and other campers on the trail.][[0.0]]
[A re-creation of the 1856 handcart disaster was featured on the History Channel show, Wild West Tech.][[0.0]]

[-LSB-edit-RSB- Gameplay Lego Star Wars II's gameplay is from a third-person perspective, and takes place in a 3D game world that contains objects, environments and characters designed to resemble Lego pieces.][[0.0]]
[Its gameplay--a combination of the action-adventure, platform, and sometimes puzzle genres--shares elements with that of Lego Star Wars: The Video Game.][[0.0]]
[While Lego Star Wars followed the events of The Phantom Menace, Attack of the Clones and Revenge of the Sith, Lego Star Wars II is based on A New Hope, The Empire Strikes Back and Return of the Jedi.][[1.0]]
[The game comically retells the trilogy's events using cut scenes without dialogue.][[0.0]]
[The player assumes the roles of the films' characters, each of which possess specific weapons and abilities.][[0.0]]
[At any time, a second player can join the game by activating a second controller.][[0.0]]
[During game play, players can collect Lego studs--small, disk-shaped objects that serve as the game's currency.][[0.0]]
[The player has a health meter, which is displayed on the game's heads-up display.][[0.0]]
[The player's health is represented by four hearts; when these hearts are depleted the player dies and a small amount of their studs bounce away.][[0.0]]
[However, they are instantly reincarnated and can often recollect the lost studs.][[0.0]]
[The game's central location is the Mos Eisley Cantina, a spaceport bar on the planet Tatooine.][[0.0]]
[At the counter, the player may use their Lego studs to purchase characters, vehicles, gameplay hints and extras, or activate cheat codes.][[0.0]]
[In a small area outside the cantina, players may view collected vehicles.][[0.0]]
[The game is broken into levels, which are accessed from the cantina; each film is represented by six levels, representing key locations and scenes in that film.][[0.0]]
[The locations include Hoth, Bespin, Dagobah, Tatooine, the Death Star, and Endor.][[0.0]]
[The game also features bonus levels.][[0.0]]
[During levels, the player defeats enemies, builds objects out of Lego bricks and drives vehicles, Certain levels are played entirely while piloting vehicles, including a TIE fighter, a Snowspeeder, and the Millennium Falcon.][[0.0]]
[Levels must first be played in story mode.][[0.0]]
[This unlocks the next level as well as a ``free play'' mode for the recently completed level.][[0.0]]
[Gameplay is identical in the two modes.][[0.0]]
[However, story mode restricts playable characters to those followed in the film scenes the levels are based on, while free play offers all those unlocked.][[0.0]]
[Levels can be replayed in either mode to collect studs and secret items.][[0.0]]
[Three types of secret items are available: gold bricks, minikits and power bricks.][[0.0]]
[Within each level is hidden one power brick.][[0.0]]
[When a power brick is collected, its corresponding extra, such as invincibility or stud multipliers, becomes available for purchase.][[0.0]]
[Each level also contains ten hidden minikits, that is, ten pieces of a Star Wars vehicle.][[0.0]]
[When all ten have been collected, the player is awarded a gold brick.][[0.0]]
[Collecting a certain number of gold bricks unlocks free rewards, such as a spigot that spews out studs.][[0.0]]
[Gold bricks are also awarded when levels are completed and when a predefined number of studs is accumulated in a level; ninety-nine gold bricks are available.][[0.0]]
[The vehicles represented by the minikits are displayed outside the cantina.][[0.0]]
[As each vehicle is completed -LRB-all ten minikits collected-RRB-, it becomes available for play in a bonus level.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Playable characters Over 50 characters from the films are playable over the course of the game, including Luke Skywalker, Princess Leia, Han Solo, Chewbacca, Lando Calrissian, R2-D2, C-3PO, Darth Vader, an Ewok, and Boba Fett.][[0.0]]
[Character abilities have a greater role in Lego Star Wars II than in Lego Star Wars.][[0.0]]
[Certain characters armed with guns can use a grappling hook in predesignated areas.][[0.0]]
[Characters wielding lightsabers can deflect projectiles, double jump and use the Force.][[0.0]]
[R2-D2, C-3PO, and other droid characters are needed to open certain doors.][[0.0]]
[Small characters like the Ewok can crawl through hatches to reach otherwise inaccessible areas.][[0.0]]
[Bounty hunters, such as Boba Fett, may use thermal detonators to destroy otherwise indestructible objects.][[0.0]]
[Sith, like Darth Vader, can use the Force to manipulate black Lego objects.][[0.0]]
[Some characters have unique abilities; for example, Chewbacca can rip enemies' arms from their sockets, Darth Vader can choke enemies with the Force, Princess Leia possesses a slap attack, and Lando Calrissian can use a kung-fu-like attack.][[0.0]]
[Special abilities are often necessary to unlock secrets, and story mode does not always provide characters with needed abilities.][[0.0]]
[This means that some secrets can only be found in free play mode.][[0.0]]
[The player can unlock the ``Use Old Save'' extra, which imports all unlocked characters from Lego Star Wars for use in free play; however, a Lego Star Wars saved game must be present on the same memory card that contains Lego Star Wars II's save data.][[1.0]]
[Players can create two customized characters in the Mos Eisley Cantina.][[0.0]]
[These characters can be built using both miscellaneous parts and those of unlocked characters; millions of combinations are possible.][[0.0]]
[Entering two cheat codes, publicized by IGN, makes pieces for a Santa Claus character available.][[0.0]]
[The game generates names for the characters based on the pieces used -LRB-for example, a character made from pieces of Darth Vader and C-3PO might have the name ``Darth-3PO''-RRB-; alternately, the player may create a name.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Development Lego Star Wars II was created by the Cheshire, England, game developer Traveller's Tales.][[0.0]]
[LucasArts--busy with other projects--had deferred publishing of Lego Star Wars to Eidos Interactive, but regained the ``necessary resources'' to publish its sequel alongside TT Games.][[0.0]]
[Lego Star Wars II was created for Microsoft Windows, Mac OS X, Xbox, GameCube, PlayStation 2, Game Boy Advance -LRB-GBA-RRB-, Nintendo DS, PlayStation Portable -LRB-PSP-RRB-, and Xbox 360.][[0.0]]
[Differences exist between platforms: the DS and GBA versions have some different playable characters than the other versions, and the DS and PSP versions support a ``Wireless Lobby'' for multiplayer gameplay.][[0.0]]
[The Xbox 360 and PlayStation 2 versions of the game feature higher-definition graphics.][[0.0]]
[Lego Star Wars II uses a modified Lego Star Wars engine.][[0.0]]
[However, many gameplay improvements were made over its predecessor, most notably camera angles and movement.][[0.0]]
[Camera movement in co-op was a specific point of concern, as LucasArts received critical feedback from fans over this issue.][[0.0]]
[TT Games looked to expand upon the concept of levels completed entirely in vehicles.][[0.0]]
[These ``vehicle levels'' were explored more thoroughly in Lego Star Wars II than in its predecessor.][[1.0]]
[In response to complaints from fans, LucasArts and Traveller's Tales granted the ability to build bricks to all non-droid characters.][[0.0]]
[Character customization, an entirely new concept, was considered a significant improvement over the original game, and is one of three features highlighted on the game's final back cover.][[0.0]]
[Tom Stone, director at TT Games, stated of the various improvements made over the original game: The designers attempted to recreate the films' characters and events in a ``cute'' way.][[0.0]]
[Assistant producer Jeff Gullet said that, in the game's recreation of a Return of the Jedi scene where Luke Skywalker ``jumps off the plank ... and somersaults onto the skiff'', Skywalker ``performs an all-out acrobatic routine with all sorts of jumps from the plank.][[0.0]]
[LucasArts producer David Perkinson said, ''-LSB-u-RSB-nless you've got the heart of the Emperor, you are going to chuckle at many of -LSB-the characters-RSB- the first time you see them -- you just have to.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Marketing and release history On February 2, 2006, images of the game were leaked to the Internet.][[0.0]]
[However, they were quickly removed, and LucasArts, if telephoned, did not confirm or deny the game's development.][[0.0]]
[The game was formally announced on February 10 at American International Toy Fair 2006.][[0.0]]
[A preview was later hosted at Electronic Entertainment Expo -LRB-E3-RRB- 2006.][[0.0]]
[Because the original Lego Star Wars had been well received commercially and critically, selling 3.3 million copies by March 2006 and winning several awards, its sequel was highly anticipated by both fans of the original game and video game publications such as IGN and GameSpot.][[0.0]]
[Shortly before the game's release, promotions were set up at chain stores across the United States, including Toys ``R'' Us, Wal-Mart, Target, Best Buy, GameStop, and Circuit City.][[1.0]]
[In Europe, Lego Star Wars II was released on September 11, 2006 for PC, Xbox, GameCube, GBA, DS, and Xbox 360; on September 15 for PlayStation 2; and on November 10 for PSP.][[1.0]]
[The game's North American release fell on September 12 for all platforms, coinciding with the release of the individual two-disc DVD releases of the films on which it was based.][[0.0]]
[The game's Australian release fell on September 15 for all platforms, but the Xbox 360 version was not released in this region.][[0.0]]
[The Mac OS X version of the game was released in 2007.][[0.0]]
[The game received a rating of E10+ from the Entertainment Software Rating Board -LRB-for ``cartoon violence'' and ``crude humor''-RRB- and 3+ from PEGI.][[0.0]]

[-LSB-edit-RSB- Reception -LSB-edit-RSB- Critical response Upon release, Lego Star Wars II was generally well-received by critics, who praised its portrayal of the films' characters and events.][[0.0]]

[The game was praised as a result of reviewers' preference of the original trilogy over the prequel trilogy.][[1.0]]
[Anthony Reiner of Game Informer said that ``comparing -LSB-the prequel trilogy-RSB- to the films in the original trilogy is similar to comparing Jar Jar Binks to Han Solo''.][[0.0]]
[Shepperd praised the level design of Lego Star Wars II, and called its predecessor's environments ``sterile''.][[0.0]]
[These views were echoed by reviewers from 1UP.com, Variety, GameSpot, GameSpy, Official U.S. PlayStation Magazine -LRB-for the PlayStation 2-RRB-, and BusinessWeek.][[0.0]]
[Critics were divided on the game's level of difficulty.][[0.0]]
[Fritz claimed that, though Lego Star Wars II provided only a ``short journey'', it was ``loads of fun''.][[1.0]]
[GameSpot's Ryan Davis estimated that it could be completed in six hours, but praised its bonus content.][[0.0]]
[GameSpy and 1UP.com's reviewers thought similarly.][[0.0]]
[A review by USA Today's Brett Molina claimed that ''-LSB-t-RSB-he game's difficulty is balanced well enough so kids won't feel too frustrated while older gamers will still find a solid challenge'' and gave the game an overall score of 8 out of 10.][[0.0]]
[Official Xbox Magazine's review praised its ``weird puzzles''.][[0.0]]
[IGN's Jeremy Dunham and Reiner were more critical of the perceived low difficulty.][[0.0]]
[Critics disliked the game's Game Boy Advance and Nintendo DS versions.][[1.0]]
[Davis believed that the Game Boy Advance version could be completed in two hours.][[0.0]]
[GameSpy staff writer Phil Theobald bemoaned this version's poor controls, easy levels and vehicle-piloting sections.][[0.0]]
[He concluded that ``for goodness sake, -LSB-one should-RSB- buy one of the -LSB-home-RSB- console versions''.][[0.0]]
[Theobald, Davis, and IGN's Craig Harris criticized the high number of glitches in the DS version.][[0.0]]
