[-LSB-edit-RSB- Early years The US Congress laid the groundwork for the establishment of Texas A&M with their proposal of the Morrill Act.][[0.0]]
[The Morrill Act, signed into law July 2, 1862, was created to enable states to establish colleges where the ``leading object shall be, without excluding other scientific and classical studies and including military tactics, to teach such branches of learning as are related to agriculture and mechanical arts...in order to promote the liberal and practical education of the industrial classes in the several pursuits and professions in life''.][[0.0]]
[States were granted public lands to be sold at auctions to establish a permanent fund to support the schools.][[0.0]]
[Both the Republic of Texas and the Texas State Legislature also set aside public lands for a future college.][[0.0]]
[The Agricultural and Mechanical College of Texas, known as Texas A.M.C., was established by the state legislature on April 17, 1871 as the state's first public institution of higher education.][[1.0]]
[The legislature provided US$75,000 for the construction of buildings at the new school, and state leaders invested profits from the sale of 180,000�acres -LRB-730�km2-RRB- received under the Land-Grant College Act in gold frontier defense bonds, creating a permanent endowment for the college.][[0.0]]
[A committee tasked with finding a home for the new college chose Brazos County, which agreed to donate 2,416�acres -LRB-10�km2-RRB- of land.][[0.0]]
[Jefferson Davis, former President of the Confederate States of America, was offered the presidency of the college but turned it down.][[0.0]]
[The college officially opened on October 4, 1876 with six professors.][[1.0]]
[Forty�students were present on the first day of classes, but by the end of the school year the number had grown to 106�students.][[0.0]]
[Only men were admitted, and all students were required to participate in the Corps of Cadets and receive military training.][[1.0]]
[The campus bore minimal resemblance to its modern counterpart.][[0.0]]
[Wild animals roamed freely around the campus, and the area served as a meeting point for the Great Western Cattle Trail.][[0.0]]
[Despite its name, the college taught no classes in agriculture, instead concentrating on classical studies, languages, literature, and applied mathematics.][[0.0]]
[After four years, students could attain degrees in scientific agriculture, civil and mining engineering, and language and literature.][[0.0]]
[Local farmers complained that the college was abusing its mission, and, in November 1879, the president and faculty were replaced and given a mandated curriculum in agriculture and engineering.][[0.0]]
[During these early years, student life was molded by the Corps of Cadets.][[0.0]]
[The Corps was divided into a battalion of three companies, and rivalry among the companies was strong, giving birth to the Aggie spirit and future traditions.][[0.0]]
[No bonfires, yell practices, or athletics teams existed as yet, and social clubs and fraternities were discouraged.][[0.0]]
[Enrollment, which had climbed as high as 500�students, declined to only 80�students in 1883, the year the University of Texas opened in Austin, Texas.][[0.0]]
[Although the Texas Constitution specified that the Agricultural and Mechanical College was to be a branch of a proposed University of Texas, the Austin school was established with a separate Board of Regents.][[0.0]]
[Texas A.M.C. continued to be governed by its own Board of Directors.][[0.0]]
[The two Texas schools quickly began to battle over the limited funds that the state legislature made available for higher education.][[0.0]]
[In 1887, the Texas Agricultural Experiment Station was established at Texas A.M.C., enabling the college to gain more funding.][[0.0]]
[Many residents of the state saw little need for two colleges in Texas, and some wanted to close the agricultural and mechanical school.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Sul Ross era Texas A.M.C. president Lawrence Sullivan Ross, known affectionately to students as ``Sully'', is credited for saving the school from closure and transforming it into a respected military institution.][[0.0]]
[Ross, the immediate past governor of Texas, had been a well-respected Confederate Brigadier General and enjoyed a good reputation among state residents.][[0.0]]
[When Ross arrived at the school, he found no running water, a housing shortage, a disgruntled faculty, and many students running wild.][[0.0]]
[As Ross began to make improvements, parents began to send their children to the school in the hopes that they would learn from Ross's example.][[0.0]]
[Although enrollment had always been limited to men, in 1893, Ethel Hudson, the daughter of an A&M professor, became the first woman to attend classes at the school and helped edit the annual yearbook.][[0.0]]
[She was made an honorary member of the class of 1895.][[0.0]]
[Several years later her twin sisters became honorary members of the class of 1903, and slowly other daughters of Aggie professors were allowed to attend classes.][[0.0]]
[Under Ross's seven and one-half year tenure, many enduring Aggie traditions formed.][[0.0]]
[These traditions include the first Aggie Ring, the first yearbook, and the formation of the Aggie Band.][[0.0]]
[Ross's tenure also saw the school's first intercollegiate football game, played against the University of Texas.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Program expansion By 1910, the school listed eight degree programs, including agriculture, architecture, agricultural engineering, chemical engineering, civil engineering, electrical engineering, mechanical engineering, and textile engineering.][[0.0]]
[Five years later the state legislature, in cooperation with the United States Department of Agriculture, established the Texas Agricultural Extension Service, organized the Texas Forest Service, and authorized a School of Veterinary Medicine at the college.][[0.0]]
[The college was unprepared for the growth, and for the next ten years several hundred students lived in tents in a field in the middle of campus.][[0.0]]
[During this time, women were also given a more official standing.][[1.0]]
[The Texas Legislature in 1911 refused to give A&M permission to hold a summer semester unless women were also permitted to attend.][[0.0]]
[For the next several decades during the summers cadets were not required to be in uniform and women could attend class and participate in intramural activities.][[0.0]]
[Texas A&M graduates were called to use their military training during World War I, and by 1918, 49% of all graduates of the college were in military service, a larger percentage than any other college or university.][[1.0]]
[In early September 1918, the entire senior class was mustered into military service, with plans to send the younger students at staggered dates throughout the next year.][[0.0]]
[Many of the seniors were fighting in France when the war ended two months later.][[0.0]]
[In total, over 1200 former students served as commissioned officers during World War I.  Texas A&M Hillel, the oldest Hillel organization in the United States, was founded in 1920.][[1.0]]
[The organization occurred three years before the national Hillel Foundation was organized at the University of Illinois.][[0.0]]
[After the war, Texas A&M grew rapidly and became nationally recognized for its programs in agriculture, engineering, and military science.][[0.0]]
[The first graduate school was organized in 1924, and, in 1925, Mary Evelyn Crawford Locke became the first female to receive a diploma from Texas A&M, although she was not allowed to participate in the graduation ceremony.][[0.0]]
[The following month the Board of Directors officially prohibited all women from enrolling.][[0.0]]
[In 1926, they codified that women in summer school had an unofficial status and could not pursue a degree.][[0.0]]
[By 1930, however, over 1800 women had attended classes at A&M.][[0.0]]
[In the late 1920s, following the discovery of oil on university lands, Texas A&M and the University of Texas negotiated a settlement for the division of the Permanent University Fund which enabled A&M to receive one-third of the revenue.][[0.0]]
[This guaranteed wealth enabled A&M to expand.][[0.0]]
[Enrollment increased even during the Great Depression, as student cooperative housing projects enabled the students to attend the school at low costs.][[0.0]]
[During the Depression, as professors were forced to accept a 25% pay cut, the Board of Directors partially rescinded its order against female enrollment, allowing no more than 20 females at a time to enroll in the school, and further restricting the group to daughters of professors.][[0.0]]
[Texas A&M expanded its degree offerings in the late 1930s and awarded its first Ph.D. in 1940.][[0.0]]
[Other programs at the college likewise began offering doctoral degrees throughout the next few decades.][[0.0]]
[World War II gave Texas A&M an opportunity to prove itself on a worldwide stage.][[0.0]]
[The school produced 20,229 fighting men who served in combat; of these, 14,123 were officers, more than the combined total of the United States Naval Academy and the United States Military Academy and more than three times the totals of any other Senior Military College.][[0.0]]
[Seven Aggies received the Medal of Honor during the worldwide conflict, tying with Virginia Tech as the most of any school outside of the military academies at West Point and Annapolis, and 29 former students reached the rank of general.][[0.0]]
[In addition, the college received nationwide exposure during the war when a reporter wrote a widely distributed story about the Aggie Muster on the island of Corregidor.][[0.0]]
[The intense interest resulted in a World War II propaganda movie, We've Never Been Licked, which was filmed on the A&M campus and showcased many of the school traditions.][[0.0]]
[Though Texas A&M was originally established as a branch of the yet-to-be-created University of Texas, subsequent acts of the Texas Legislature never gave the University any authority over Texas A&M.][[1.0]]
[This internal legal conflict in Texas was nullified in 1948 when Texas A&M became the flagship school of the newly created Texas A&M University System, a clear and separate institution from the University of Texas System.][[1.0]]
[A&M's Board of Directors continued to oversee the system.][[0.0]]
[Enrollment soared as many former soldiers used the G.I. Bill to further their education.][[0.0]]
[Unprepared for the growth, between 1949 and 1953 Texas A&M used the former Bryan Air Force Base as an extension of the campus.][[0.0]]
[An estimated 5,500 men lived, studied, ate, showered, and attended classes at the base, which became known as the Annex -LRB-and later as Riverside Campus-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Rudder era The Texas Legislature defeated a nonbinding resolution in the 1950s to encourage A&M to admit women.][[0.0]]
[The school newspaper, The Battalion began writing editorials to encourage coeducation, causing the Student Senate to demand the editor of the paper resign.][[0.0]]
[Later in the year students defeated 2--1 a campus resolution on coeducation.][[0.0]]
[On March 26, 1959 retired Major General James Earl Rudder, who arguably had the most significant effect on the campus, became the 16th president of the college, his alma mater.][[1.0]]
[At the time, the college was still an all-male military school with a 7,500 student enrollment.][[0.0]]
[Within several years of his arrival, the 58th Legislature of Texas officially changed the name of the school from the A&M College of Texas to Texas A&M University.][[1.0]]
[The Legislature specified that in the new name of the school, the A and the M were purely symbolic, reflecting the school's past, and no longer stood for ``Agricultural and Mechanical''.][[0.0]]
[With Rudder's strong encouragement, in 1963, the A&M Board of Directors officially reversed their stance on admitting women.][[0.0]]
[The wives and daughters of faculty, staff and students as well as female staff members were finally allowed to officially participate in undergraduate programs, although they were not permitted to join the Corps of Cadets.][[0.0]]
[The following year the college was officially integrated as A&M welcomed its first African American student.][[0.0]]
[More change ensued, as, in 1965, the Board of Directors voted to make membership in the Corps of Cadets voluntary.][[1.0]]
[The same year the Board voted to allow any woman, not just those connected to students and professors, to attend the university.][[0.0]]
[The Board required that Rudder approve each female applicant; he accepted any woman who met the academic requirements.][[0.0]]
[During Rudder's tenure, African-American students were also welcomed, and in 1967, James L. Courtney of Dallas became the first African-American to receive an undergraduate degree from Texas A&M University.][[0.0]]
[He remained at Texas A&M and, in 1970, became the first African-American to receive a D.V.M. degree from the College of Veterinary Medicine.][[0.0]]
[When Rudder died in 1970, after 11 years as president of the school, Texas A&M University had grown to more than 14,000 students from all 50 states and 75 nations.][[0.0]]
[The school had become coeducational and had even begun construction of an all-female dormitory.][[0.0]]
[The curriculum had been broadened, with upgraded academic and faculty standards, and the school had initiated a multi-million dollar building program.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Recent years On September 17, 1971, Texas A&M University was one of the first four institutions to be designated a sea-grant college in recognition of oceanographic development and research.][[0.0]]
[A third designation was added on August 31, 1989 when Texas A&M was named a space-grant college.][[0.0]]
[The university remains one of few institutions nationwide to hold designations as a land-, sea-, and space-grant college.][[1.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Diversification The Corps welcomed its first female members in the fall of 1974.][[0.0]]
[At the time, the women were segregated into a special unit, known as W-1, and suffered harassment from many of their male counterparts.][[0.0]]
[Women were originally prohibited from serving in leadership positions or in the more elite Corps units such as the band and Ross Volunteers.][[0.0]]
[These groups were opened to female participation in 1988, following a federal court decision in a class-action lawsuit filed by a female cadet.][[0.0]]
[Two years later, in 1990, female-only units were eliminated.][[0.0]]
[In November 1976, the university denied official recognition to the Gay Student Services Organization on the grounds that homosexuality was illegal in Texas, and the group's stated goals--offering referral services and providing educational information to students--were actually the responsibility of university staff.][[0.0]]
[The students sued the university for violation of their First Amendment right to freedom of speech in February 1977.][[0.0]]
[For six years, Gay Student Services v. Texas A&M University wound its way through the courts; although the trial court ruled in favor of Texas A&M several times, the 5th Circuit Court of Appeals repeatedly overturned the verdict.][[0.0]]
[The U.S. Supreme Court declined to take the case, letting stand the circuit court ruling that the students' free speech rights had been compromised.][[0.0]]
[The case set a national precedent by removing all legal restrictions against gay rights groups on campuses.][[0.0]]
[The subsequent recognition of the group provided a university precedent for allowing social organizations.][[0.0]]
[In 1977, the university had also denied recognition to Sigma Phi Epsilon, a national social fraternity, because its presence on campus might result in ``a social caste system''.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Presidential library The George Bush Presidential Library was established in 1997 on 90�acres -LRB-364,220�m2-RRB- of land donated by Texas A&M at the western edge of the campus.][[1.0]]
[This tenth presidential library was built between 1995 and 1997 and contains the presidential and vice-presidential papers of George H.W. Bush and the vice-presidential papers of Dan Quayle.][[0.0]]
[To coincide with the opening of the George Bush Presidential Library, Texas A&M established the George Bush School of Government and Public Service.][[0.0]]
[The school, offers a master's degree in public policy and one in international affairs as well as two research degrees, officially launched in 1997.][[0.0]]
[It became a separate school within the university in 1999.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Bonfire collapse At 2:42�a.m. on November 18, 1999, the partially completed Aggie Bonfire, standing 40�feet -LRB-12�m-RRB- tall and consisting of about 5000 logs, collapsed during construction.][[0.0]]
[Of the 58 students and former students working on the stack, 12 were killed and 27 others were injured.][[0.0]]
[The incident received nationwide attention, with over 50 satellite trucks broadcasting from the Texas A&M campus within hours.][[0.0]]
[On November 25, 1999, the date that Bonfire would have burned, Aggies instead held a vigil and remembrance ceremony on site.][[0.0]]
[Over 40,000 people, including former President George H.W. Bush and his wife Barbara and then-Texas governor George W. Bush and his wife Laura, lit candles and observed up to two hours of silence at the site of the Bonfire collapse.][[0.0]]
[A commission put together by Texas A&M University discovered that a number of factors led to the Bonfire collapse, including ``excessive internal stresses'' on the logs and ``inadequate containment strength'', where the wiring used to tie the logs together was not strong enough.][[0.0]]
[The wiring broke after logs from upper tiers were ``wedged'' into lower tiers.][[0.0]]
[Texas A&M officials, Bonfire student leaders, and the university itself were the subject of several lawsuits by parents of the students injured or deceased in the collapse.][[0.0]]
[On May 21, 2004, Federal Judge Samuel B. Kent dismissed all claims against the Texas A&M officials, and, in 2005, 36 of the 64 original defendants, including all of the student leaders, settled their portion of the case for an estimated US$4.25 million, paid by their insurance companies.][[0.0]]
[A federal appeals court dismissed the remaining lawsuits against Texas A&M and its officials in 2007.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Vision 2020 In 1997, university president Ray Bowen appointed a task force to create a new strategic plan for the university.][[0.0]]
[The task force, made up of more than 250 faculty, staff, students, former students, local residents, and various private- and public-sector representatives, devoted more than two years to examining all aspects of the university and studying benchmark institutions before unveiling the plan, dubbed Vision 2020, in 1999.][[0.0]]
[Vision 2020's goal is to make Texas A&M University recognized as a consensus ``top 10'' public university by the year 2020.][[0.0]]
[The plan identifies 12 areas in which the university should focus on improving.][[0.0]]
[Dr. Robert M. Gates succeeded Bowen in 2002, and during his four year tenure as president, Vision 2020's short-term focus narrowed to four key steps:  Gates' leadership resulted in the largest academic expansion in the university's history.][[0.0]]
[As of September 8, 2006, Vision 2020's progress includes:  -LSB-edit-RSB- Hurricane relief Following Hurricane Katrina in 2005, Texas A&M opened Reed Arena as a temporary shelter to house over 200 evacuees from New Orleans.][[0.0]]
[Although school was barely in session and there was minimal notice, the students and staff of A&M prepared the facility, setting up several hundred beds on the arena floor and making arrangements for the evacuees to get new clothes and have medical checks.][[0.0]]
[Aggie students organized a child care facility, and Aggie athletes escorted teenagers to the Aggie Rec Center to play basketball.][[0.0]]
[Less than three weeks later, Reed Arena was again opened as a temporary shelter for people fleeing Hurricane Rita.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Current status With strong support from Rice University and the University of Texas at Austin, the Association of American Universities inducted Texas A&M in May 2001, based on the depth of the university's research and academic programs.][[0.0]]
[Furthermore, in 2004, the honors organization Phi Beta Kappa opened its 265th chapter at Texas A&M.][[0.0]]
[On December 18, 2006, former Texas A&M University president Robert M. Gates was sworn in as the 22nd U.S. Secretary of Defense.][[0.0]]
[Gates' successor, Elsa Murano, on January 3, 2008 became both the university's first female and first Hispanic president.][[0.0]]
[Murano's term as president ended abruptly June 2009, and was succeeded by Interim President R. Bowen Loftin.][[0.0]]
[Loftin was eventually selected as university president in February of 2010.][[0.0]]
[The university has a current enrollment of more than 45,000, with more than 8,000 graduate and professional students, making it one of the largest universities in America.][[0.0]]
[As of 2007, the percentage of women and men at the school are roughly equal.][[0.0]]
[However, for a number of years female of the freshman class have outnumbered males.][[0.0]]
[The university has awarded more than 320,000 degrees, of which 70,000 have been graduate and professional degrees.][[0.0]]
[Texas A&M has two branch campuses, one in Galveston, Texas, and one in the Middle Eastern country of Qatar.][[0.0]]

[The leak prompted Lindelof and Cuse to enter ``radio silence'', which was temporarily broken at Comic-Con International 2007.][[0.0]]
[The funeral parlor that Jack visits is called ``Hoffs/Drawlar'', which is an anagram of ``flashforward''.][[0.0]]
[The idea of flashforwards was conceived by creators Lindelof and Abrams during the show's conception.][[0.0]]
[However, Cuse and Lindelof only started fleshing out the idea at the end of the first season, after they realized that flashbacks would eventually stop being revelatory and knew that they would eventually have to switch to flashforwards.][[0.0]]
[With the announcement that the series would conclude 48 episodes after ``Through the Looking Glass'', they felt comfortable playing flashforwards as early as the third season finale.][[1.0]]

[Amateur career Early years Earvin Johnson Jr. was born to Earvin Sr., a General Motors assembly worker, and Christine, a school custodian.][[0.0]]
[Johnson grew up in Lansing, Michigan, and came to love basketball as a youngster, idolizing players such as Earl Monroe and Marques Haynes, and practicing ``all day''.][[0.0]]
[Johnson was first dubbed ``Magic'' as a 15-year-old sophomore playing for Lansing's Everett High School, when he recorded a triple-double of 36�points, 18�rebounds and 16�assists.][[0.0]]
[After the game, Fred Stabley Jr., a sports writer for the Lansing State Journal, gave him the moniker despite the belief of Johnson's mother, a Christian, that the name was sacrilegious.][[0.0]]
[In his final high school season, Johnson led Lansing Everett to a 27--1 win--loss record while averaging 28.8�points and�16.8 rebounds per game, and took his team to an overtime victory in the state championship game.][[1.0]]
[Michigan State University Although Johnson was recruited by several top-ranked colleges such as Indiana and UCLA, he decided to play close to home.][[0.0]]
[He initially wanted to attend the University of Michigan, but decided on Michigan State University in East Lansing, after the school's basketball coach, Jud Heathcote, promised that Johnson could play the point guard position.][[1.0]]
[Johnson did not initially aspire to play professionally, focusing instead on his communication studies major and on his desire to become a television commentator.][[0.0]]
[Playing with future NBA draftees Greg Kelser, Jay Vincent and Mike Brkovich, Johnson averaged 17.0�points, 7.9�rebounds, and 7.4�assists per game as a freshman, and led the Spartans to a 25--5�record, the Big Ten Conference title, and a berth in the 1978 NCAA Tournament.][[0.0]]
[The Spartans reached the Elite Eight, but lost narrowly to eventual national champion Kentucky.][[0.0]]
[During the 1978--79 season, Michigan State again qualified for the NCAA Tournament, where they advanced to the championship game and faced Indiana State University, which was led by senior Larry Bird.][[0.0]]
[In what was the most-watched college basketball game ever, Michigan State defeated Indiana State 75--64, and Johnson was voted Most Outstanding Player of the Final Four.][[0.0]]
[After two years in college, during which he averaged 17.1�points, 7.6�rebounds, and 7.9�assists per game, Johnson declared himself eligible for the 1979 NBA Draft.][[1.0]]
[Professional career Rookie season in the NBA -LRB-1979--80-RRB- Johnson was drafted first overall in 1979 by the Los Angeles Lakers.][[0.0]]
[Johnson said that what was ``most amazing'' about joining the Lakers was the chance to play alongside Kareem Abdul-Jabbar, the team's 7�ft 2�in -LRB-2.18�m-RRB- center who became the leading scorer in NBA history.][[0.0]]
[Despite Abdul-Jabbar's dominance, he had failed to win a championship with the Lakers, and Johnson was expected to help them achieve that goal.][[0.0]]
[Johnson averaged 18.0�points, 7.7�rebounds, and 7.3�assists per game for the season, was selected to the NBA All-Rookie Team, and was named an NBA All-Star Game starter.][[0.0]]
[The Lakers compiled a 60--22�record in the regular season and reached the 1980 NBA Finals, in which they faced the Philadelphia 76ers, who were led by forward Julius Erving.][[0.0]]
[The Lakers took a 3--2�lead in the series, but Abdul-Jabbar, who averaged 33�points a game in the series, sprained his ankle in Game 5 and could not play in Game 6.][[0.0]]

[Johnson became the only rookie to win the NBA Finals MVP award, and his clutch performance is still regarded as one of the finest in NBA history.][[0.0]]
[He also became one of four players to win NCAA and NBA championships in consecutive years.][[1.0]]
[Ups and downs -LRB-1980--83-RRB- Early in the 1980--81 season, Johnson was sidelined after he suffered torn cartilage in his left knee.][[0.0]]

[HIV announcement and Olympics -LRB-1991--92-RRB- After a physical before the 1991--92 NBA season, Johnson discovered that he had tested positive for HIV.][[0.0]]
[In a press conference held on November�7, 1991, Johnson made a public announcement that he would retire immediately.][[0.0]]
[He stated that his wife Cookie and their unborn child did not have HIV, and that he would dedicate his life to ``battle this deadly disease''.][[0.0]]
[Johnson initially said that he did not know how he contracted the disease, but later admitted that it was through having multiple sexual partners during his playing career.][[0.0]]
[At the time, only a small percentage of HIV-positive people had contracted it from heterosexual sex, and it was initially rumored that Johnson was gay or bisexual, although he denied both.][[0.0]]
[Johnson's announcement became a major news story in the United States, and in 2004 was named as ESPN's seventh most memorable moment of the past 25 years.][[0.0]]
[Many articles praised Johnson as a hero, and former U.S. President George H. W. Bush said, ``For me, Magic is a hero, a hero for anyone who loves sports.''][[0.0]]

[During the tournament, which the USA won easily, Johnson played infrequently because of knee problems, but he received standing ovations from the crowd, and used the opportunity to inspire HIV-positive people.][[0.0]]

[Off the court Personal life Johnson first fathered a son in 1981, when Andre Johnson was born to Melissa Mitchell.][[0.0]]

[HIV activism After announcing his infection in November 1991, Johnson created the Magic Johnson Foundation to help combat HIV, although he later diversified the foundation to include other charitable goals.][[0.0]]
[In 1992, he joined the National Commission on AIDS, but left after eight months, saying that the commission was not doing enough to combat the disease.][[0.0]]
[He was also the main speaker for the United Nations -LRB-UN-RRB- World AIDS Day Conference in 1999, and has served as a United Nations Messenger of Peace.][[0.0]]
[HIV had been associated with drug addicts and homosexuals, but Johnson's campaigns sought to show that the risk of infection was not limited to those groups.][[0.0]]
[Johnson stated that his aim was to ``help educate all people about what -LSB-HIV-RSB- is about'' and teach others not to ``discriminate against people who have HIV and AIDS''.][[0.0]]
[Johnson was later criticized by the AIDS community for his decreased involvement in publicizing the spread of the disease.][[0.0]]
[To prevent his HIV infection from becoming AIDS, Johnson takes a daily combination of drugs.][[0.0]]
[He has advertised GlaxoSmithKline's drugs, and partnered with Abbott Laboratories to publicize the fight against AIDS in African American communities.][[0.0]]
[Career achievements In 905�NBA games, Johnson scored 17,707�points, 6,559�rebounds, and 10,141�assists, translating to career averages of 19.5�points, 7.2�rebounds, and 11.2�assists per game.][[0.0]]

[Johnson was exceptional because he played point guard despite being 6�ft 9�in -LRB-2.06�m-RRB-, a size reserved normally for frontcourt players.][[0.0]]
[He combined the size of a power forward, the one-on-one skills of a swingman, and the ball handling talent of a guard, making him one of the most dangerous triple-double threats of all time; his 138�triple-double games are second only to Oscar Robertson's 181.][[0.0]]
[For his feats, Johnson was voted as one of the 50 Greatest Players of All Time by the NBA in 1996, and was inducted into the Basketball Hall of Fame in 2002.][[1.0]]
[ESPN's SportsCentury ranked Johnson #17 in their ``50 Greatest Athletes of the 20th Century'' In 2006, ESPN.com rated Johnson the greatest point guard of all time, stating, ``It could be argued that he's the one player in NBA history who was better than Michael Jordan.''][[1.0]]
[Several of his achievements in individual games have also been named among the top moments in the NBA.][[0.0]]
[Rivalry with Larry Bird Johnson and Bird were first linked as rivals after Johnson's Michigan State squad defeated Bird's Indiana State team in the 1979 NCAA finals.][[0.0]]
[The rivalry continued in the NBA, and reached its climax when Boston and Los Angeles met in three out of four NBA Finals from 1984 to 1987.][[1.0]]
[Johnson asserted that for him, the 82-game regular season was composed of 80 normal games, and two Lakers--Celtics games.][[0.0]]
[Similarly, Bird admitted that Johnson's daily box score was the first thing he checked in the morning.][[0.0]]
[Several journalists hypothesized that the Johnson--Bird rivalry was so appealing because it represented many other contrasts, such as the clash between the Lakers and Celtics, between Hollywood flashiness -LRB-``Showtime''-RRB- and Boston/Indiana blue collar grit -LRB-``Celtic Pride''-RRB-, and between blacks and whites.][[0.0]]
[The rivalry was also significant because it drew national attention to the faltering NBA.][[0.0]]
[Prior to Johnson and Bird's arrival, the NBA had gone through a decade of declining interest and low TV ratings.][[0.0]]
[With the two future Hall of Famers, the league won a whole generation of new fans, drawing both traditionalist adherents of Bird's dirt court Indiana game and those appreciative of Johnson's public park flair.][[0.0]]
[Sports journalist Larry Schwartz of ESPN asserted that Johnson and Bird saved the NBA from bankruptcy.][[0.0]]
[Despite their on-court rivalry, Johnson and Bird became good friends; ironically, they became close during the filming of a 1984 Converse shoe advertisement that depicted them as enemies.][[0.0]]
[Johnson appeared at Bird's retirement ceremony in 1992, and described Bird as a ``friend forever''; during Johnson's Hall of Fame ceremony, Bird formally inducted his old rival.][[0.0]]

[-LSB-edit-RSB- History The first amateur radio operator in India was Amarendra Chandra Gooptu -LRB-callsign 2JK-RRB-, licensed in 1921.][[1.0]]
[Later that year, Mukul Bose -LRB-2HQ-RRB- became the second ham operator, thereby introducing the first two-way ham radio communication in the country.][[0.0]]
[By 1923, there were twenty British hams operating in India.][[0.0]]
[In 1929, the call sign prefix VU came into effect in India, replacing three-letter call signs.][[0.0]]
[The first short-wave entertainment and public broadcasting station, ``VU6AH'', was set up in 1935 by E P Metcalfe, vice-chancellor of Mysore University.][[0.0]]
[However, there were fewer than fifty licence holders in the mid-1930s, most of them British officers in the Indian army.][[0.0]]
[With the outbreak of World War II in 1939, the British cancelled the issue of new licences.][[0.0]]
[All amateur radio operators were sent written orders to surrender their transmitting equipment to the police, both for possible use in the war effort and to prevent the clandestine use of the stations by Axis collaborators and spies.][[0.0]]
[With the gaining momentum of the Indian independence movement, ham operator Nariman Abarbad Printer -LRB-VU2FU-RRB- set up the Azad Hind Radio to broadcast Gandhian protest music and uncensored news; he was immediately arrested and his equipment seized.][[0.0]]
[In August 1942, after Mahatma Gandhi launched the Quit India Movement, the British began clamping down on the activities of Indian independence activists and censoring the media.][[0.0]]
[To circumvent media restrictions, Indian National Congress activists, led by Usha Mehta, contacted Mumbai-based amateur radio operators, ``Bob'' Tanna -LRB-VU2LK-RRB- and Nariman Printer to help broadcast messages to grass-roots party workers across the country.][[0.0]]
[The radio service was called the ``Congress Radio'', and began broadcasting from 2 September, 1942 on 7.12�MHz.][[0.0]]
[The station could be received as far as Japanese-occupied Myanmar.][[0.0]]
[By November 1942, Tanna was betrayed by an unknown radio officer and was forced to shut down the station.][[0.0]]
[Temporary amateur radio licences were issued from 1946, after the end of World War II.][[0.0]]
[By 1948, there were 50 amateur radio operators in India, although only a dozen were active.][[0.0]]
[Following India's independence in 1947, the first amateur radio organization, the Amateur Radio Club of India was inaugurated on 15 May 1948 at the School of Signals at Mhow in Madhya Pradesh.][[0.0]]
[The club headquarters was later moved to New Delhi, where it was renamed the Amateur Radio Society of India -LRB-ARSI-RRB- on 15 May, 1954.][[0.0]]
[As India's oldest amateur radio organization, ARSI became its representative at the International Amateur Radio Union.][[0.0]]
[Partly due to low awareness among the general population and prohibitive equipment costs, the number of licensed amateur radio operators did not increase significantly over the next two decades, numbering fewer than a thousand by 1970.][[0.0]]
[CW -LRB-Morse code-RRB- and AM were the predominant modes at that time.][[0.0]]
[The electronic equipment was mostly valve-based, obtained from Indian army surpluses.][[0.0]]
[During the mid-1960s, the modes of operation saw a change from Amplitude Modulation to Single Side Band -LRB-SSB-RRB- as the preferred communication mode.][[0.0]]
[By 1980, the number of amateur radio operators had risen to 1,500.][[0.0]]
[In 1984, then Indian Prime Minister, Rajiv Gandhi, waived the import duty for wireless equipment.][[1.0]]
[After this, the number of operators rose steadily, and by 2000 there were 10,000 licensed ham operators.][[0.0]]
[As of 2007-LSB-update-RSB-, there are more than 16,000 ham radio operators in India.][[1.0]]
[Amateur radio operators have played a significant part in disaster management and emergencies.][[0.0]]
[In 1991, during the Gulf War, a lone Indian ham operator in Kuwait, provided the only means of communication between stranded Indian nationals in that country and their relatives in India.][[0.0]]
[Amateur radio operators have also played a helpful part in disaster management.][[1.0]]
[Shortly after the 1993 Latur and 2001 Gujarat earthquakes, the central government rushed teams of ham radio operators to the epicentre to provide vital communication links.][[1.0]]
[In December 2004, a group of amateur radio operators on DX-pedition on the Andaman Islands witnessed the 2004 Indian Ocean Tsunami.][[0.0]]
[With communication lines between the islands severed, the group provided the only way of relaying live updates and messages to stations across the world.][[0.0]]
[In 2005, India became one of few countries to launch an amateur radio satellite, the HAMSAT.][[0.0]]
[The Indian Space Research Organisation -LRB-ISRO-RRB- launched the microsatellite as an auxiliary payload on the PSLV-6.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Licence categories The Indian Wireless Telegraph -LRB-Amateur Service-RRB- Rules, 1978 lists five licence categories:  To obtain a licence in the first four categories, candidates must pass the Amateur Station Operator's Certificate examination.][[0.0]]
[This examination is held monthly in Delhi, Mumbai, Kolkata and Chennai-RRB-, every two months in Ahmedabad, Nagpur and Hyderabad, and every four months in some smaller cities.][[0.0]]
[The examination consists of two 50-mark written sections: Radio theory and practice, Regulations; and a practical test consisting of a demonstration of Morse code proficiency in both sending and receiving.][[0.0]]
[After passing the examination, the candidate must then clear a police interview.][[0.0]]
[After clearance, the WPC grants the licence along with the user-chosen call sign.][[0.0]]
[This procedure can take up to 12 months.][[0.0]]
[In a 2005 notification, the WPC proposed an amendment to the 1978 Amateur Service Rules in the rationalization of the licence categories to only two: the Amateur Wireless Telegraph Station Licence -LRB-General-RRB- and the Amateur Wireless Telegraph Station Licence -LRB-Restricted-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[As of January 2010-LSB-update-RSB-, amendment has yet to be passed by the Parliament of India.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Call-signs The International Telecommunication Union -LRB-ITU-RRB- has divided all countries into three regions; India is located in ITU Region 3.][[0.0]]
[These regions are further divided into two competing zones, the ITU and the CQ.][[0.0]]
[Mainland India and the Lakshadweep Islands come under ITU Zone�41 and CQ Zone�22, and the Andaman and Nicobar Islands under ITU Zone�49 and CQ Zone�26.][[0.0]]
[The ITU has assigned to India call-sign blocks 8TA to 8YZ, VUA to VWZ, and ATA to AWZ.][[0.0]]
[The WPC allots individual call-signs.][[0.0]]
[Indian amateur radio operators are allotted only the VU call-sign prefix.][[0.0]]
[The V or Viceroy, series prefix was allotted to British colonies.][[0.0]]
[at the 1912 London International Radiotelegraphic Convention.][[0.0]]
[VU call-signs are listed according to licence grade: for Advanced Grade and Grade--I licence holders, the call-sign prefix is VU2; for Grade--II and Grade--II Restricted licence holders, the prefix is VU3.][[0.0]]
[The VU3 prefix has also been granted to foreigners operating in India.][[0.0]]
[As of 2009-LSB-update-RSB-, call-signs consist of only letters, not numerals, and can be either two or three characters long.][[0.0]]
[Examples of Indian amateur radio call-signs are ``VU2XY'' and ``VU2XYZ''.][[0.0]]
[In addition to individual and club call-signs, the WPC allots temporary call-signs for contests and special events.][[0.0]]
[For example, in November 2007, the WPC temporarily allotted the prefixes AT and AU to selected ham operators to mark the anniversary of the birth of radio scientist Jagadish Chandra Bose.][[0.0]]
[The Indian Union territory -LRB-UT-RRB- of Andaman and Nicobar Islands are given the call-sign VU4 -LRB-VU4AN-RRB- and VU5; and the UT of Lakshadweep is given VU7 -LRB-VU7LD-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[The WPC had temporarily allotted these call-signs to hamfests and DX-peditions held at those locations.][[0.0]]
[Defunct call-signs include CR8 -LRB-for Portuguese India-RRB-, FN8 -LRB-for French India-RRB-, and AC3 -LRB-for the former kingdom of Sikkim, which merged with India in 1975-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Organizations The WPC is responsible for regulating amateur radio in India.][[0.0]]
[The WPC has its headquarters in New Delhi with divisional offices in Mumbai -LRB-Bombay-RRB-, Kolkata -LRB-Calcutta-RRB-, and Chennai -LRB-Madras-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[It also has monitoring stations in Ahmedabad, Nagpur, Hyderabad, Ajmer, Bangalore, Darjeeling, Gorakhpur, Jalandhar, Goa -LRB-Betim-RRB-, Mangalore, Shillong, Ranchi, Srinagar, Dibrugarh, Vishakapatnam, and Thiruvananthapuram.][[0.0]]
[Set up in 1952, the organization is responsible for conducting exams, issuing licences, allotting frequency spectrum, and monitoring the airwaves.][[0.0]]
[It is also responsible for maintaining the rules and regulations on amateur radio.][[0.0]]
[In India, amateur radio is governed by the Indian Wireless Telegraphs -LRB-Amateur Service-RRB- Rules, 1978, the Indian Wireless Telegraph Rules, and the Indian Telegraph Act, 1885.][[0.0]]
[The WPC is also responsible for coordinating with the Ministry of Internal Affairs and the Intelligence Bureau in running background checks before issuing amateur radio licences.][[0.0]]
[The Amateur Radio Society of India -LRB-ARSI-RRB-, based in Chennai, is a non-profit organization founded in 1954 that represents the interests of amateur radio operators before the India government, provides technical advice and assistance to amateur radio enthusiasts, and supports a number of educational programs throughout the country.][[1.0]]
[ARSI is India's representative at the International Amateur Radio Union.][[0.0]]
[Another notable organization is the non-governmental National Institute of Amateur Radio -LRB-NIAR-RRB-, based in Hyderabad.][[0.0]]
[NIAR was established by the Ministry of Communications in 1983 to promote amateur radio in India.][[1.0]]
[NIAR is also involved in amateur radio educational programs in the country and sponsors several DX-peditions.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Allotted spectrum The following 14 frequency bands are permitted by the WPC for use by amateur radio operators in India.][[1.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Awareness drives Indian amateur radio operators number just 16,000 for a population of 1.2 billion, or less than 0.002 percent of the population.][[0.0]]
[Factors for the low numbers include low awareness, high equipment cost, and bureaucratic procedures in obtaining a licence where obtaining a licence can take over a year.][[0.0]]
[After decades of lobbying to include ham radio in school syllabi, efforts paid off in 2006 after the Central Board of Secondary Education -LRB-CBSE-RRB- included ham radio in the chapter of Emergency communications on the subject of Disaster Management.][[0.0]]
[The CBSE is one of the two national education boards.][[0.0]]
[The President of India APJ Abdul Kalam, in a speech to the International Union of Radio Science held in New Delhi in 2005, stressed on the promotion of the hobby and setting up of ham stations in local panchayat offices, schools and hospitals.][[0.0]]
[They would also act as early warning systems for the village communities in cases of emergencies.][[0.0]]
[Amateur radio clubs across the country many join the hobby by organizing courses in preparation for the Amateur Station Operator's Certificate.][[0.0]]
[The government-funded NIAR is one such organization that actively promotes the hobby by holding regular classes.][[0.0]]
[The Vigyan Parishad, an apex body for science popularization under the Department of Science and Technology of the Government of India coordinates simulated disaster communication exercises and also organizes trainings to help people getting ham radio licenses in areas vulnerable to natural calamities.][[1.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Activities and events Popular events and activities include Amateur Radio Direction Finding, DX-peditions, hamfests, JOTA, QRP operations, Contesting, DX communications, Light House operation, and Islands on Air.][[1.0]]
[One of the most popular activities is Amateur Radio Direction Finding commonly known as a ``foxhunt''.][[0.0]]
[Several clubs across India regularly organize foxhunts in which participants search for a hidden transmitter around the city.][[0.0]]
[A foxhunt carried out in Matheran near Mumbai in 2005 by the Mumbai Amateur Radio Society was listed in the 2006 Limca Book of Records under the entry ``most ham operators on horseback on a foxhunt.''][[0.0]]
[Despite being a popular recreational activity among hams, no organization has yet participated in an international event.][[0.0]]
[Hamfest India is an annual event that serves for social gathering and comparison and sales of radio equipment.][[0.0]]
[Most hamfests feature a flea market, where the attendees buy and sell equipment, generally from and for their personal stations.][[0.0]]
[The event also seeks to raise amateur radio awareness in the host city.][[0.0]]
[In 2008, Gandhinagar hosted the annual hamfest.][[0.0]]
[Bangalore hosted the hamfest in November 2009.][[0.0]]
[The 2010 hamfest would be held at Pollachi, Tamil Nadu.][[0.0]]
[Two special international hamfests were organized in the Andaman and Nicobar Islands -LRB-VU4-RRB- in 2006, and Lakshadweep -LRB-VU7-RRB- in January 2007.][[0.0]]
[As amateur radio activity is not permitted on the two union territories due to security restrictions, special permissions were needed to be secured to host the event.][[0.0]]
[The two events received widespread international participation through contests, DXing, and DX-peditions.][[0.0]]
[Ham nets, where amateur radio operators ``check into'' are regularly conducted across India.][[1.0]]
[Airnet India, Charminar Net, Belgaum Net, and Nite Owl's Net are some of the well-known ham nets in India.][[0.0]]
[Some amateur radio operators have also provided a service of downloading and decoding satellite signals of the weather over India and publishing them on the internet as a public service.][[0.0]]
[In Mumbai, ham operators also help in disaster management during the monsoon season when heavy rain disrupts general life there.][[0.0]]
[In 2008, the Municipal Corporation of Greater Mumbai, for the first time, formally included ham operators as part of the disaster management plan.][[0.0]]
[Hams in Mumbai also help out at the annual Ganpati Visarjan by providing emergency radio services to civic organizations at the immersion points.][[0.0]]
[The ``Island on the Air'' is another activity where ham enthusiasts visit remote islands and report on the conditions there, similar to DX-peditions.][[0.0]]

[-LSB-edit-RSB- Early life Joel Selwood was born to Bryce and Maree Selwood in the country town of Bendigo, Victoria.][[0.0]]
[He was raised in a family of sportspeople.][[0.0]]
[His mother Maree was a top runner and tennis player, and elder twins Adam and Troy were identified as talented footballers at a young age.][[0.0]]
[As a two-year-old, Selwood was forced to wear splints on his leg to help overcome a walking disability.][[0.0]]
[Selwood displayed athletic talent from an early age.][[0.0]]
[He was the state hurdling champion from under-10s through to the under-15s, and in one year held every running and jumping record at the Bendigo Sports Centre, except the 100�metres sprint.][[0.0]]
[Growing up with his older brothers, Selwood was accustomed to playing with bigger teammates and opponents.][[0.0]]
[By the time he was eight years old, he had played his first competitive game of football, against children four years his senior, and had kicked three goals.][[0.0]]
[Selwood played junior football with the Sandhurst Football Club, until he was chosen to play for the Bendigo Pioneers in the TAC Cup competition throughout 2006.][[1.0]]
[Although his age made him ineligible to enter the 2005 AFL Draft, Selwood's accomplishments during the year led to AFL Talent Manager Kevin Sheehan rating him ``the best 17-year-old in Australia''.][[0.0]]
[Selwood received an array of accolades and honours as a bottom-aged player, winning mid-year State honours for Victoria Country in the AFL National Championships.][[0.0]]
[His performances in the championships earned him end-of-year All-Australian honours and the Most Valuable Player award for Victoria Country.][[0.0]]
[In addition, he was named in the TAC Cup Team of the Year, awarded a scholarship within the prestigious AIS-AFL Academy, and subsequently captained his older teammates in the annual under-18s International Rules Series in Ireland.][[1.0]]
[His performances in Ireland earned him the Ron Barassi Medal, as the adjudicated Most Valuable Player of the tour.][[0.0]]
[Selwood's achievements in local football were recognised when he was listed as a finalist in the 2006 Bendigo Sports Star of the Year Award.][[0.0]]
[Returning in 2006 with the Pioneers, Selwood entered his final year of junior football as one of the top rated prospects in his age group.][[0.0]]
[His achievements at the junior level were recognised early by the AFL Player's Association, when he was awarded the Mike Fitzpatrick Scholarship, and the AFL Life Members Education Fund Award.][[0.0]]
[Rewarded with the captaincy of Bendigo for his final season, Selwood again won mid-year State honours for Victoria Country.][[0.0]]
[However, a knee injury occurred only six rounds into the competition forcing him to undergo surgery--his fourth knee operation within two years--prematurely ending his season.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- AFL career -LSB-edit-RSB- 2007: Debut Selwood was drafted by the Geelong Football Club with their first selection, and was the seventh overall draft pick in the 2006 AFL Draft.][[1.0]]
[Although many recruiting scouts viewed him as ``the finest pure footballer in the draft'', and a potential top pick, there was still concern surrounding the durability of his knee.][[0.0]]
[Nonetheless, as the equal highest-ever draft pick for the Cats, Selwood made his debut in the opening round of the 2007 AFL premiership season and missed only four games during the regular season.][[0.0]]
[Selwood's transition to the AFL drew positive comments throughout the footballing community; former Brisbane coach Leigh Matthews noted how ``from -LSB-his-RSB- first game ... he looked like he had played 200 of them''.][[0.0]]
[Geelong coach Mark Thompson labelled the 18-year-old Selwood the best youngster to have come under his tutelage during his time at the club.][[0.0]]
[From the beginning, Selwood showed an ability to influence games; in just his fourth appearance he led his older and more experienced teammates with a game of 25�disposals and nine tackles.][[0.0]]
[This achievement, described as inspiring, earned him an AFL Rising Star Award nomination.][[0.0]]
[The Geelong coaching staff had a highlights package made of this performance, to screen to the rest of the team during the match review later that week.][[0.0]]
[Selwood's impact on the game as a first--year player prompted numerous comparisons to Chris Judd, who a few years earlier had been lauded for his first-year success as an 18-year-old.][[0.0]]
[At the conclusion of the home and away period, Selwood's regular contributions resulted in several individual accolades.][[0.0]]
[Polling 44 out of a possible 45�votes, he was awarded the inaugural Ron Evans Medal as the winner of the AFL Rising Star Award.][[0.0]]
[Selwood earned the maximum five votes from eight of the nine judges, the other awarding him four out of five.][[0.0]]
[This meant that eight of the nine judges rated him as the best nominee.][[0.0]]
[Selwood was also awarded the prestigious AFLPA Best First Year Player Award, winning over 70% of all votes from his peers.][[1.0]]
[He became only the fourth player to win both the major awards for first--year players.][[0.0]]
[Having helped Geelong finish first on the ladder and win the McClelland Trophy, Selwood made his finals debut in the 2nd Qualifying Final against the Kangaroos -LRB-now North Melbourne-RRB- at the Melbourne Cricket Ground -LRB-MCG-RRB-.][[0.0]]
[In front of nearly 80,000�spectators, Selwood amassed 22�disposals, five marks, five clearances, six inside 50s, five tackles, and five score assists, helping Geelong to a club finals record 106�point victory.][[0.0]]
[Although Selwood struggled to contribute in the Preliminary Final against Collingwood two weeks later, he returned to form for the 2007 AFL Grand Final at the MCG against Port Adelaide.][[0.0]]
[With 18�disposals, five marks, five clearances--the most of any Geelong player--seven inside 50s, four tackles, and an equal game-high four score assists, Selwood helped the Cats to a record 119�point win.][[0.0]]
[At 19�years of age, Selwood became the youngest person to play in a winning Grand Final team in 10�years.][[0.0]]
[He was also the first to win the AFL Rising Star Award and play in a premiership side within the same season, making him one of the most accomplished first�year players in league history.][[0.0]]
[Selwood's debut year achievements resulted in his becoming AFL's highest-paid first--year player.][[0.0]]

[Selwood finished the game with 24�disposals--including a team--high 12�contested possessions and equal game--high 16�handpasses--4�marks, 6�clearances, a team--high 10�first possessions, 3�tackles, and 1�goal to help the Cats record a 12�point win and secure their second premiership in three seasons.][[0.0]]
[Selwood's standout year was recognised by his peers when he became the youngest-ever winner of the AFLPA Robert Rose Most Courageous Player Award, and placed fifth for the Leigh Matthews Trophy as the AFLPA Most Valuable Player.][[0.0]]
[Selwood also finished sixth in the Brownlow Medal, polling 16�votes to finish second at Geelong behind eventual winner Gary Ablett.][[0.0]]

[After averaging 28�disposals and 5�tackles per game over the course of the season, Selwood was also awarded with All-Australian honours for the first time in his career and a second successive top five placing for the Carji Greeves Medal.][[0.0]]
[Selwood finished the year ranked fourth in the league for total disposals -LRB-691-RRB-, third for total handpasses -LRB-389-RRB-, second for contested possessions -LRB-236-RRB- and eighth for hard-ball gets.][[0.0]]
[For the second successive season, he also led the league for total free kicks earned -LRB-59-RRB- and finished the year ranked within the top five at Geelong in several major statistical categories.][[0.0]]
[-LSB-edit-RSB- Player profile Selwood is primarily regarded as an inside midfielder, and is noted for his physical approach, his ability to find space on the ground, and for winning the ball.][[0.0]]
[Leigh Matthews has complimented Selwood as ``already the best wet-weather player in the game'', a view shared by Nathan Buckley, who notes that Selwood ``plays with a physicality and intensity that is the benchmark of the competition, consistently -LRB-finding-RRB- third and fourth efforts to win the contested ball''.][[0.0]]

[They regard him as one of the most physical players in the game.][[0.0]]

[Despite his admitting to being ``not the fastest player, or most skillful'', his hard running, tackling and one percent plays are important elements to his overall game.][[0.0]]
[Because of his aggressive style-of-play, Selwood has gathered a reputation as a ``head--over--the--ball specialist'' and for consistently putting himself in positions to win free kicks.][[0.0]]
[His ability to ``buckle his knees, -LRB-get-RRB- down and pull his arm up so that the tackler's arm goes up above the neck'' is reflected in his regular standing among league leaders for total free kicks received.][[0.0]]
[As of 2009, Selwood also holds the AFL record for highest career average for frees received per game -LRB-2.28-RRB-.][[1.0]]
[Since his arrival at the club his leadership ability has often been praised, leading to predictions of a future post as club captain.][[0.0]]
[During his first three seasons at Geelong, Selwood's all-around ability to contribute as an attacking inside or outside midfielder has been highlighted by his regular ranking among the top five at the club for total disposals, clearances, first possessions, contested possessions, uncontested possessions, tackles, inside 50s, and score assists.][[1.0]]

[Buckley has complimented Selwood's all-around game, making note of his ''-LRB-preparedness-RRB- to pressure the opposition in defence as ferociously as he attacks''.][[0.0]]
[Throughout his career, Selwood has carried an above--average disposal efficiency of 75%.][[0.0]]
[Footballing experts have praised his decision-making ability when considering his overall productivity with the football.][[0.0]]
[However, his lack of natural leg speed is considered one of his more notable weaknesses.][[0.0]]
